% CHAPTER 5 — PYTHON MASTERY
% ============================================================
\chapter{Python Mastery}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} move from writing working Python to writing professional Python. This means stronger mental models for abstraction, testing, typing, packaging, and maintainability.
\end{studybox}

This chapter goes deeper into Python's advanced features, standard library, ecosystem, and production best practices.

\section{Advanced OOP}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Advanced OOP features}]
# Class methods and static methods
class User:
    _count = 0

    def __init__(self, name, email):
        self.name = name
        self.email = email
        User._count += 1

    @classmethod
    def get_count(cls):
        return cls._count

    @staticmethod
    def validate_email(email):
        return "@" in email and "." in email

# Properties (getters/setters)
class Temperature:
    def __init__(self, celsius):
        self._celsius = celsius

    @property
    def celsius(self):
        return self._celsius

    @celsius.setter
    def celsius(self, value):
        if value < -273.15:
            raise ValueError("Below absolute zero!")
        self._celsius = value

    @property
    def fahrenheit(self):
        return self._celsius * 9/5 + 32

# Abstract base classes
from abc import ABC, abstractmethod

class Shape(ABC):
    @abstractmethod
    def area(self):
        pass

    @abstractmethod
    def perimeter(self):
        pass

class Circle(Shape):
    def __init__(self, radius):
        self.radius = radius

    def area(self):
        return 3.14159 * self.radius ** 2

    def perimeter(self):
        return 2 * 3.14159 * self.radius

# Dataclasses (Python 3.7+)
from dataclasses import dataclass, field
from typing import List

@dataclass
class TuneFile:
    name: str
    engine_cc: int
    version: float = 1.0
    tags: List[str] = field(default_factory=list)

    def display_name(self):
        return f"{self.name} v{self.version}"
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Type Hints}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Type hints}]
from typing import List, Dict, Optional, Tuple, Union

def process_users(
    users: List[Dict[str, str]],
    limit: Optional[int] = None
) -> List[str]:
    names = [u["name"] for u in users]
    if limit:
        return names[:limit]
    return names

def get_user(user_id: int) -> Optional[Dict[str, str]]:
    """Returns user dict or None if not found."""
    ...
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Context Managers}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Context managers}]
# Custom context manager using class
class DatabaseConnection:
    def __enter__(self):
        self.conn = create_connection()
        return self.conn

    def __exit__(self, exc_type, exc_val, exc_tb):
        self.conn.close()

# Using contextlib
from contextlib import contextmanager

@contextmanager
def timer(label):
    import time
    start = time.time()
    yield
    print(f"{label}: {time.time() - start:.4f}s")

with timer("Data processing"):
    # ... do work ...
    pass
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Async/Await}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Asynchronous Python}]
import asyncio

async def fetch_data(url: str) -> dict:
    print(f"Fetching {url}...")
    await asyncio.sleep(1)  # simulates network delay
    return {"url": url, "status": 200}

async def main():
    # Run concurrently (not in parallel)
    results = await asyncio.gather(
        fetch_data("https://api.example.com/users"),
        fetch_data("https://api.example.com/posts"),
        fetch_data("https://api.example.com/comments"),
    )
    for r in results:
        print(r)

asyncio.run(main())
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Testing in Python}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Testing with pytest}]
# test_calculator.py
import pytest

def add(a, b):
    return a + b

def divide(a, b):
    if b == 0:
        raise ValueError("Cannot divide by zero")
    return a / b

# Basic tests
def test_add():
    assert add(2, 3) == 5
    assert add(-1, 1) == 0
    assert add(0, 0) == 0

# Testing exceptions
def test_divide_by_zero():
    with pytest.raises(ValueError):
        divide(10, 0)

# Parameterized tests
@pytest.mark.parametrize("a, b, expected", [
    (10, 2, 5.0),
    (7, 3, 2.3333333333333335),
    (0, 5, 0.0),
])
def test_divide(a, b, expected):
    assert divide(a, b) == pytest.approx(expected)

# Fixtures
@pytest.fixture
def sample_users():
    return [
        {"name": "Amzat", "age": 24},
        {"name": "Test", "age": 30},
    ]

def test_user_count(sample_users):
    assert len(sample_users) == 2
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Debugging Patterns and Reading Tracebacks}

Professional Python developers spend a large amount of time debugging, not because they are weak engineers, but because real systems are complex. Good debugging is a repeatable skill.

\subsection{How to Read a Traceback}

When Python throws an error, read the traceback from the \textbf{bottom upward}:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item the last line tells you \emph{what} failed
    \item the lines above show \emph{where} the failure came from
    \item the stack shows the chain of function calls that led to the problem
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Reading a traceback}]
def divide_total(total, count):
    return total / count

def average(numbers):
    return divide_total(sum(numbers), len(numbers))

print(average([]))
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{What happens?}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \texttt{numbers} is an empty list.
    \item \texttt{sum(numbers)} is \texttt{0}.
    \item \texttt{len(numbers)} is \texttt{0}.
    \item \texttt{divide\_total(0, 0)} triggers \texttt{ZeroDivisionError}.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{The lesson:} the crash happened in \texttt{divide\_total}, but the \emph{real} bug is that \texttt{average()} allowed an empty list through without validation.

\subsection{Reliable Debugging Patterns}

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Reproduce reliably.} If you cannot reproduce the bug, you cannot trust the fix.
    \item \textbf{Minimise the example.} Reduce the failing case until only the important lines remain.
    \item \textbf{Inspect real values.} Print or log the exact value and type.
    \item \textbf{Check boundaries.} Empty input, \texttt{None}, single element, huge values, and invalid values cause many bugs.
    \item \textbf{Write a test after the fix.} If the bug mattered enough to debug, it usually matters enough to prevent in future.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{interviewbox}
A good debugging answer in interviews is structured. Say what failed, how you isolated it, which hypothesis you tested, how you confirmed the root cause, and what guard you added to stop regression.
\end{interviewbox}

\section{Python Standard Library Highlights}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \texttt{os} / \texttt{pathlib} --- file system operations
    \item \texttt{sys} --- system-level operations, command-line args
    \item \texttt{json} --- JSON encoding/decoding
    \item \texttt{csv} --- CSV file reading/writing
    \item \texttt{datetime} --- date and time manipulation
    \item \texttt{collections} --- \texttt{defaultdict}, \texttt{Counter}, \texttt{deque}, \texttt{OrderedDict}
    \item \texttt{itertools} --- combinatoric iterators
    \item \texttt{functools} --- \texttt{lru\_cache}, \texttt{partial}, \texttt{reduce}
    \item \texttt{re} --- regular expressions
    \item \texttt{logging} --- structured logging
    \item \texttt{typing} --- type hints
    \item \texttt{unittest} / \texttt{pytest} --- testing frameworks
    \item \texttt{hashlib} --- cryptographic hashing
    \item \texttt{secrets} --- secure random values
    \item \texttt{subprocess} --- running external commands
    \item \texttt{http.server} --- simple HTTP server
    \item \texttt{argparse} --- command-line argument parsing
\end{itemize}

\section{Virtual Environments and Package Management}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={Virtual environment workflow}]
# Create virtual environment
python3 -m venv venv

# Activate
source venv/bin/activate    # macOS/Linux
# venv\Scripts\activate     # Windows

# Install packages
pip install django==4.2 requests numpy

# Freeze dependencies
pip freeze > requirements.txt

# Install from requirements
pip install -r requirements.txt

# Deactivate
deactivate
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Project Hygiene: Structure, Dependencies, and Tooling}

Python mastery is not only about language features. It is also about keeping projects easy to run, review, test, and maintain.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={A clean Python project layout}]
my_project/
  app/
    __init__.py
    main.py
    services.py
    models.py
  tests/
    test_main.py
    test_services.py
  requirements.txt
  README.md
  .env.example
  .gitignore
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Good project hygiene means:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item dependencies are pinned and documented
    \item secrets are never committed; use \texttt{.env} files and environment variables
    \item tests live in a predictable place
    \item the project has a clear entry point
    \item a new developer can clone it and run it without guessing
\end{itemize}

\begin{warningbox}
Many junior developers underestimate environment problems. If your code ``works on my machine'' but nowhere else, the project is not truly working yet.
\end{warningbox}

\section{Python Best Practices}

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Follow \textbf{PEP 8} (Python style guide): 4-space indentation, snake\_case for functions/variables, PascalCase for classes.
    \item Write \textbf{docstrings} for every function and class.
    \item Use \textbf{type hints} for all function signatures.
    \item Use \textbf{virtual environments} for every project.
    \item Use \textbf{f-strings} (not \texttt{\%} formatting or \texttt{.format()}).
    \item Handle errors with specific exception types, never bare \texttt{except:}.
    \item Use \texttt{with} for file and resource management.
    \item Write \textbf{tests} (aim for at least 80\% coverage).
    \item Use a linter (\texttt{flake8}) and formatter (\texttt{black}).
    \item Keep functions short (under 20 lines ideally).
\end{enumerate}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Practice task:} take one of your older Python scripts and improve it using this checklist:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item add a virtual environment
    \item add a \texttt{requirements.txt}
    \item split one large function into smaller helpers
    \item add type hints to every function
    \item add at least three tests
    \item add a README explaining how to run it
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercisebox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Advanced Python is about clarity, maintainability, and leverage.
    \item Type hints, context managers, testing, and standard-library fluency raise code quality significantly.
    \item Python mastery directly supports backend, data engineering, and AI work.
    \item Real Python professionalism also includes environment discipline, traceback reading, and repeatable debugging habits.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Why are type hints useful even in a dynamically typed language?
    \item What problem does a context manager solve?
    \item When is asynchronous Python helpful?
    \item Why does Python's standard library matter for real engineering productivity?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now have deep Python knowledge covering advanced OOP, type hints, context managers, async, testing, the standard library, and best practices. Python is the foundation for Django, Flask, ML, and data engineering---everything that follows builds on this.}

% CHAPTER 6 — JAVASCRIPT MASTERY
% ============================================================
\chapter{JavaScript Mastery}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} understand JavaScript as the language of the browser and a major backend/runtime language. Focus on scope, objects, asynchronous execution, and the event-driven model.
\end{studybox}

JavaScript is the language of the web. Every browser runs it natively. It is also used server-side (Node.js), in mobile apps (React Native), and across your entire frontend stack.

\section{Why JavaScript Matters}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item The \textbf{only} language that runs natively in web browsers.
    \item Used in React (frontend), React Native (mobile), Node.js/Express (backend).
    \item Appears in nearly every project on your CV.
\end{itemize}

\section{Core Syntax}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={JavaScript variables and types}]
// Variables (use let and const, NEVER var)
let age = 24;             // mutable
const name = "Amzat";     // immutable binding
// var oldWay = "avoid";  // function-scoped, hoisted - avoid

// Primitive types
let str = "hello";        // string
let num = 42;             // number (int and float are same)
let bool = true;          // boolean
let nothing = null;       // intentional absence
let notDefined = undefined; // not yet assigned
let big = 9007199254740991n; // BigInt
let sym = Symbol("id");   // Symbol (unique identifier)

// typeof
typeof "hello"  // "string"
typeof 42       // "number"
typeof true     // "boolean"
typeof null     // "object" (historical bug!)
typeof undefined // "undefined"

// Template literals (like Python f-strings)
let greeting = `Hello, ${name}! You are ${age} years old.`;
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Functions}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={JavaScript functions}]
// Function declaration
function add(a, b) {
    return a + b;
}

// Function expression
const multiply = function(a, b) {
    return a * b;
};

// Arrow function (preferred in modern JS)
const subtract = (a, b) => a - b;
const square = x => x * x;  // single param: no parens needed
const greet = () => "Hello!"; // no params

// Default parameters
const greetUser = (name = "Guest") => `Hello, ${name}!`;

// Rest parameters (like Python *args)
const sum = (...numbers) => numbers.reduce((a, b) => a + b, 0);
sum(1, 2, 3, 4); // 10

// Destructuring
const user = { name: "Amzat", age: 24, city: "London" };
const { name: userName, age: userAge } = user;

const [first, second, ...rest] = [1, 2, 3, 4, 5];
// first=1, second=2, rest=[3,4,5]

// Spread operator
const arr1 = [1, 2, 3];
const arr2 = [...arr1, 4, 5]; // [1, 2, 3, 4, 5]
const merged = { ...user, email: "karim@example.com" };
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Scope and Closures}

JavaScript scope is one of the most important topics to understand well, because it affects callbacks, event handlers, React components, and async code.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Global scope:} available everywhere.
    \item \textbf{Function scope:} variables declared inside a function exist only there.
    \item \textbf{Block scope:} variables declared with \texttt{let} or \texttt{const} exist only inside that block.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Scope and closure basics}]
function outer() {
    let counter = 0;

    return function inner() {
        counter += 1;
        return counter;
    };
}

const increment = outer();
console.log(increment()); // 1
console.log(increment()); // 2
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why this works:} the inner function keeps access to variables from the outer function even after the outer function has finished running. That preserved access is called a \textbf{closure}.

\begin{intuitionbox}
A closure is not magic. It is simply a function carrying some remembered data with it. This pattern appears everywhere in JavaScript: event handlers, factories, middleware, hooks, and callback-driven code.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{Arrays and Array Methods}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Array methods}]
const nums = [1, 2, 3, 4, 5];

// map: transform each element
const doubled = nums.map(n => n * 2); // [2,4,6,8,10]

// filter: keep elements passing a test
const evens = nums.filter(n => n % 2 === 0); // [2, 4]

// reduce: combine all elements into one value
const total = nums.reduce((acc, n) => acc + n, 0); // 15

// find: first element matching condition
const found = nums.find(n => n > 3); // 4

// some / every
nums.some(n => n > 4);  // true (at least one)
nums.every(n => n > 0); // true (all of them)

// forEach: side-effect loop (no return value)
nums.forEach(n => console.log(n));

// includes
nums.includes(3); // true

// Chaining
const result = nums
    .filter(n => n % 2 !== 0)
    .map(n => n * 10)
    .reduce((acc, n) => acc + n, 0); // 90
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Objects}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Objects in JavaScript}]
const bike = {
    make: "Yamaha",
    model: "MT-07",
    year: 2024,
    getDisplayName() {
        return `${this.year} ${this.make} ${this.model}`;
    }
};

// Access
bike.make;          // "Yamaha"
bike["model"];      // "MT-07" (bracket notation)

// Computed property names
const key = "engine_cc";
const specs = { [key]: 689 }; // { engine_cc: 689 }

// Object methods
Object.keys(bike);    // ["make", "model", "year", ...]
Object.values(bike);  // ["Yamaha", "MT-07", 2024, ...]
Object.entries(bike); // [["make","Yamaha"], ...]

// Optional chaining
const email = user?.contact?.email; // undefined if missing
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Asynchronous JavaScript}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Promises and async/await}]
// Callback (old way - avoid "callback hell")
setTimeout(() => console.log("After 1s"), 1000);

// Promise
const fetchData = (url) => {
    return new Promise((resolve, reject) => {
        // simulate async work
        setTimeout(() => {
            if (url) resolve({ data: "success" });
            else reject(new Error("No URL"));
        }, 1000);
    });
};

fetchData("/api/users")
    .then(result => console.log(result))
    .catch(error => console.error(error))
    .finally(() => console.log("Done"));

// async/await (preferred - cleaner than .then chains)
async function loadUsers() {
    try {
        const response = await fetch("/api/users");
        const data = await response.json();
        return data;
    } catch (error) {
        console.error("Failed:", error);
    }
}

// Parallel execution
const [users, posts] = await Promise.all([
    fetch("/api/users").then(r => r.json()),
    fetch("/api/posts").then(r => r.json()),
]);
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{The Event Loop Mental Model}

JavaScript in the browser runs on a single main thread for most UI work. That raises an obvious question: how can it handle timers, user events, and network requests without freezing?

\textbf{High-level model:}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item JavaScript runs synchronous code on the call stack.
    \item Slow operations such as timers and network requests are handed off to browser APIs or the runtime.
    \item When those operations complete, callbacks or promise resolutions are queued.
    \item The event loop moves queued work back onto the call stack when it is free.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{warningbox}
Async JavaScript does not mean ``everything runs in parallel.'' It means the runtime can defer work and resume it later without blocking the main execution flow.
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Promise Combinators}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{\texttt{Promise.all}} fails fast if any promise fails.
    \item \textbf{\texttt{Promise.allSettled}} waits for all promises and reports each result.
    \item \textbf{\texttt{Promise.race}} resolves or rejects with the first completed promise.
\end{itemize}

\begin{interviewbox}
JavaScript async is one of the most commonly asked interview topics. Be ready to explain the event loop, the difference between callbacks/promises/async-await, and what \texttt{Promise.all} vs \texttt{Promise.allSettled} vs \texttt{Promise.race} do.
\end{interviewbox}

\section{Classes and OOP}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={JavaScript classes}]
class Vehicle {
    #vin; // private field (ES2022+)

    constructor(make, model, year) {
        this.make = make;
        this.model = model;
        this.year = year;
        this.#vin = null;
    }

    get displayName() {
        return `${this.year} ${this.make} ${this.model}`;
    }

    drive(km) {
        return `Drove ${km}km`;
    }

    static compare(a, b) {
        return a.year - b.year;
    }
}

class Motorcycle extends Vehicle {
    constructor(make, model, year, cc) {
        super(make, model, year);
        this.engineCC = cc;
    }

    wheelie() {
        return "Doing a wheelie!";
    }
}

const mt07 = new Motorcycle("Yamaha", "MT-07", 2024, 689);
console.log(mt07.displayName); // 2024 Yamaha MT-07
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Prototypes and Why Classes Are Syntax Sugar}

JavaScript is prototype-based. The \texttt{class} syntax is convenient, but under the hood objects inherit behaviour through prototypes.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Prototype intuition}]
function Vehicle(make) {
    this.make = make;
}

Vehicle.prototype.describe = function() {
    return `Vehicle made by ${this.make}`;
};

const bike = new Vehicle("Yamaha");
console.log(bike.describe());
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{What to remember:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item objects can inherit methods from another object
    \item \texttt{class} syntax makes this easier to read
    \item understanding prototypes helps explain \texttt{this}, inheritance, and method lookup
\end{itemize}

\section{Common JavaScript Mistakes}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item using \texttt{var} instead of \texttt{let} or \texttt{const}
    \item using loose equality \texttt{==} when strict equality \texttt{===} is safer
    \item forgetting that objects and arrays are reference values
    \item assuming async code runs in the order it is written without \texttt{await}
    \item mutating shared state carelessly
    \item misunderstanding \texttt{this} inside callbacks and methods
\end{itemize}

\section{Modules (ES6)}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={ES6 module system}]
// utils.js - Named exports
export const API_URL = "https://api.example.com";
export function formatDate(date) {
    return date.toISOString().split("T")[0];
}

// auth.js - Default export
export default class AuthService {
    login(email, password) { /* ... */ }
}

// main.js - Imports
import AuthService from "./auth.js";
import { API_URL, formatDate } from "./utils.js";
import * as Utils from "./utils.js"; // import all

// CommonJS (Node.js style - older)
const express = require("express");
module.exports = { myFunction };
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Error Handling}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Error handling in JS}]
try {
    JSON.parse("invalid json");
} catch (error) {
    console.error(error.message);
    console.error(error.stack); // stack trace
} finally {
    // always runs
}

// Custom errors
class ValidationError extends Error {
    constructor(field, message) {
        super(message);
        this.name = "ValidationError";
        this.field = field;
    }
}

throw new ValidationError("email", "Invalid email format");
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Key Differences: Python vs JavaScript}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Feature} & \textbf{Python} & \textbf{JavaScript} \\
\midrule
Indentation & Required (syntax) & Optional (style) \\
Blocks & Indentation & Curly braces \texttt{\{\}} \\
Types & Dynamic, strong & Dynamic, weak \\
Equality & \texttt{==} & \texttt{===} (strict, always use) \\
None/Null & \texttt{None} & \texttt{null} / \texttt{undefined} \\
Print & \texttt{print()} & \texttt{console.log()} \\
Lists/Arrays & \texttt{list} & \texttt{Array} \\
Dictionaries & \texttt{dict} & Object / \texttt{Map} \\
Async & \texttt{asyncio} & Native event loop \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item JavaScript powers both browser interactivity and server-side development through Node.js.
    \item Scope, closures, objects, and asynchronous execution are central to writing strong JavaScript.
    \item Good JavaScript understanding directly supports React, React Native, and Express.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What is a closure?
    \item Why is asynchronous thinking important in JavaScript?
    \item How are objects used differently from arrays?
    \item Why do modules improve maintainability?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now have deep JavaScript knowledge: variables, functions, arrays, objects, async/await, classes, modules, and error handling. This is the foundation for React, React Native, and Node.js.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 7 — TYPESCRIPT MASTERY
% ============================================================
\chapter{TypeScript Mastery}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} learn TypeScript as a tool for making larger JavaScript codebases safer and easier to reason about. The main value is not syntax; it is confidence, refactorability, and clearer contracts.
\end{studybox}

TypeScript is JavaScript with \textbf{static types}. It catches errors at compile time rather than runtime. It is used in your React Native (RevSync mobile), React, and modern frontend projects.

\section{Why TypeScript?}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Catches bugs \textbf{before} the code runs (type-checking at compile time).
    \item Better IDE support (auto-complete, refactoring, inline docs).
    \item Self-documenting: types serve as documentation.
    \item Industry standard for serious frontend and full-stack projects.
    \item Required for your RevSync mobile app.
\end{itemize}

\section{Core Types}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={TypeScript basic types}]
// Primitive types
let name: string = "Amzat";
let age: number = 24;
let isActive: boolean = true;
let nothing: null = null;
let notSet: undefined = undefined;

// Arrays
let numbers: number[] = [1, 2, 3];
let names: Array<string> = ["Amzat", "Karim"];

// Tuples (fixed-length, typed arrays)
let coord: [number, number] = [51.5074, -0.1278];

// Enums
enum UserRole {
    ADMIN = "admin",
    RIDER = "rider",
    TUNER = "tuner",
}
let role: UserRole = UserRole.RIDER;

// Union types
let id: string | number = "abc123";
id = 42; // also valid

// Literal types
let direction: "left" | "right" | "up" | "down" = "left";

// any (escape hatch - avoid when possible)
let flexible: any = "could be anything";

// unknown (safer than any - must narrow before use)
let input: unknown = getUserInput();
if (typeof input === "string") {
    console.log(input.toUpperCase()); // OK after narrowing
}
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Interfaces and Type Aliases}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Interfaces and types}]
// Interface (for object shapes)
interface User {
    id: number;
    name: string;
    email: string;
    age?: number;          // optional
    readonly createdAt: Date; // cannot be modified
}

// Extending interfaces
interface Rider extends User {
    bikeModel: string;
    licenseType: "A1" | "A2" | "A";
}

// Type alias (more flexible)
type TuneStatus = "draft" | "pending" | "approved" | "rejected";

type ApiResponse<T> = {
    data: T;
    status: number;
    message: string;
};

// Usage
const response: ApiResponse<User[]> = {
    data: [{ id: 1, name: "Amzat", email: "k@g.com",
             createdAt: new Date() }],
    status: 200,
    message: "OK",
};
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Interface vs Type Alias}

Learners often ask which one to use. The real answer is that both are useful, but they shine in slightly different places.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{|p{0.25\textwidth}|p{0.31\textwidth}|p{0.31\textwidth}|}
\hline
\textbf{Question} & \textbf{Interface} & \textbf{Type Alias} \\
\hline
Best for & object shapes and extension & unions, tuples, mapped and composed types \\
\hline
Can be extended & yes, very naturally & yes, through intersections \\
\hline
Great for API/domain models & yes & yes \\
\hline
Great for advanced type composition & less flexible & more flexible \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{Interface vs type alias}
\end{table}

\textbf{Practical rule:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item use \texttt{interface} for clear object contracts
    \item use \texttt{type} when you need unions, aliases, or type composition
\end{itemize}

\section{Generics}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={TypeScript generics}]
// Generic function
function getFirst<T>(arr: T[]): T | undefined {
    return arr[0];
}
getFirst<number>([1, 2, 3]);  // 1
getFirst<string>(["a", "b"]); // "a"
// Type inference: getFirst([1, 2, 3]) also works

// Generic interface
interface Repository<T> {
    findById(id: number): Promise<T | null>;
    findAll(): Promise<T[]>;
    create(item: Omit<T, "id">): Promise<T>;
    update(id: number, item: Partial<T>): Promise<T>;
    delete(id: number): Promise<void>;
}

// Constraints
interface HasId { id: number; }
function findById<T extends HasId>(items: T[], id: number): T | undefined {
    return items.find(item => item.id === id);
}
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Utility Types}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Built-in utility types}]
interface User {
    id: number;
    name: string;
    email: string;
    password: string;
}

// Partial: all properties optional
type UpdateUser = Partial<User>;

// Required: all properties required
type CompleteUser = Required<User>;

// Pick: select specific properties
type UserPreview = Pick<User, "id" | "name">;

// Omit: exclude specific properties
type PublicUser = Omit<User, "password">;

// Record: key-value map
type UserMap = Record<string, User>;

// Readonly: all properties readonly
type FrozenUser = Readonly<User>;

// ReturnType: extract function return type
type LoginResult = ReturnType<typeof loginUser>;
\end{lstlisting}

\section{TypeScript with Functions}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Typed functions}]
// Function with typed params and return
function calculateTax(amount: number, rate: number = 0.2): number {
    return amount * rate;
}

// Arrow function with types
const fetchUser = async (id: number): Promise<User | null> => {
    const response = await fetch(`/api/users/${id}`);
    if (!response.ok) return null;
    return response.json();
};

// Function type
type EventHandler = (event: MouseEvent) => void;
type Comparator<T> = (a: T, b: T) => number;
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Type Narrowing and Guards}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Type narrowing}]
// typeof guard
function processInput(input: string | number) {
    if (typeof input === "string") {
        return input.toUpperCase(); // TS knows it's string
    }
    return input.toFixed(2); // TS knows it's number
}

// in guard
interface Dog { bark(): void; }
interface Cat { meow(): void; }

function makeSound(animal: Dog | Cat) {
    if ("bark" in animal) {
        animal.bark();
    } else {
        animal.meow();
    }
}

// Custom type guard
function isUser(obj: any): obj is User {
    return obj && typeof obj.name === "string"
           && typeof obj.email === "string";
}
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Why TypeScript Helps in Real Projects}

TypeScript matters most when a codebase becomes large enough that human memory stops being reliable.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item API responses become clearer and safer.
    \item Refactoring becomes less risky because the compiler shows affected code.
    \item Navigation params, component props, and shared state become explicit contracts.
    \item New teammates can understand intent more quickly through types.
\end{itemize}

\begin{projectbox}
In a React Native app like RevSync, TypeScript is not just ``nice to have.'' It protects route params, API models, flash workflow state, entitlement objects, and legal acceptance data from drifting silently into runtime bugs.
\end{projectbox}

\section{Common TypeScript Mistakes}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item using \texttt{any} too quickly and disabling the point of TypeScript
    \item treating type errors as annoyances instead of design feedback
    \item over-modeling tiny code with unnecessarily complex generic types
    \item forgetting to narrow \texttt{unknown} values before using them
    \item defining types that no longer match backend reality
\end{itemize}

\begin{projectbox}
Your \textbf{RevSync mobile app} is built with TypeScript. Every screen, component, service, and API call uses typed interfaces. Understanding generics and utility types lets you define typed API responses, typed navigation params, and typed state management.
\end{projectbox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item TypeScript adds a type system on top of JavaScript.
    \item Interfaces, generics, utility types, and narrowing help large apps stay maintainable.
    \item TypeScript is especially valuable in React, React Native, and API-heavy frontend work.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What practical problem do interfaces solve?
    \item Why are generics useful?
    \item What is type narrowing?
    \item Why does TypeScript become more valuable as applications grow?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand TypeScript: types, interfaces, generics, utility types, type narrowing, and how to use TS with React/React Native. This gives you compile-time safety across your frontend and mobile code.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 8 — SQL MASTERY
% ============================================================
\chapter{SQL Mastery}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} learn SQL as the language of structured data retrieval and manipulation. Beyond syntax, focus on how joins, filters, grouping, constraints, and transactions support real application behaviour.
\end{studybox}

SQL (Structured Query Language) is the standard language for interacting with relational databases. Every backend application you build (Django, Flask, Express) talks to a database using SQL.

\section{Relational Database Concepts}

\subsection{What Is a Relational Database?}
A \textbf{relational database} stores data in \textbf{tables} (also called relations). Each table has:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Columns} (fields/attributes): Define what data is stored (name, email, age).
    \item \textbf{Rows} (records/tuples): Each row is one entry (one user, one order, one tune file).
    \item \textbf{Primary Key (PK):} A unique identifier for each row (usually \texttt{id}).
    \item \textbf{Foreign Key (FK):} A column that references the PK of another table, creating relationships.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Relationships}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{One-to-One:} One user has one profile. The profile table has a FK to user.
    \item \textbf{One-to-Many:} One user has many orders. The order table has a FK to user.
    \item \textbf{Many-to-Many:} Users can have many skills, and skills can belong to many users. Requires a \textbf{junction table} (e.g., \texttt{user\_skills}).
\end{itemize}

\section{SQL Syntax}

\subsection{CREATE, INSERT, SELECT}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=SQL, caption={Basic SQL operations}]
-- Create a table
CREATE TABLE users (
    id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    email VARCHAR(255) UNIQUE NOT NULL,
    age INTEGER CHECK (age >= 0),
    city VARCHAR(100) DEFAULT 'London',
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);

-- Insert data
INSERT INTO users (name, email, age)
VALUES ('Amzat Karim', 'karimamzat26@gmail.com', 24);

INSERT INTO users (name, email, age) VALUES
    ('Alice', 'alice@example.com', 28),
    ('Bob', 'bob@example.com', 32);

-- Select data
SELECT * FROM users;                    -- all columns
SELECT name, email FROM users;          -- specific columns
SELECT name FROM users WHERE age > 25;  -- filtered
SELECT DISTINCT city FROM users;        -- unique values
SELECT COUNT(*) FROM users;             -- count rows
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{WHERE, ORDER BY, LIMIT}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=SQL, caption={Filtering and sorting}]
-- WHERE with operators
SELECT * FROM users
WHERE age >= 21 AND city = 'London';

SELECT * FROM users
WHERE name LIKE 'A%';          -- starts with A

SELECT * FROM users
WHERE email IN ('a@b.com', 'c@d.com');

SELECT * FROM users
WHERE age BETWEEN 20 AND 30;

SELECT * FROM users
WHERE city IS NOT NULL;

-- ORDER BY
SELECT * FROM users ORDER BY age ASC;       -- ascending
SELECT * FROM users ORDER BY created_at DESC; -- descending

-- LIMIT and OFFSET (pagination)
SELECT * FROM users ORDER BY id LIMIT 10 OFFSET 20;
-- skip 20, take 10 (page 3 with 10 per page)
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{UPDATE and DELETE}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=SQL, caption={Updating and deleting}]
-- Update
UPDATE users SET city = 'Manchester' WHERE id = 1;
UPDATE users SET age = age + 1;  -- increment all ages

-- Delete
DELETE FROM users WHERE id = 5;

-- CAREFUL: without WHERE, this deletes ALL rows!
-- DELETE FROM users;  -- DANGER!
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Aggregate Functions and GROUP BY}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=SQL, caption={Aggregation}]
-- Aggregate functions
SELECT COUNT(*) FROM users;               -- total rows
SELECT AVG(age) FROM users;               -- average
SELECT MIN(age), MAX(age) FROM users;     -- min/max
SELECT SUM(amount) FROM orders;           -- sum

-- GROUP BY
SELECT city, COUNT(*) as user_count
FROM users
GROUP BY city
ORDER BY user_count DESC;

-- HAVING (filter on aggregated results)
SELECT city, COUNT(*) as user_count
FROM users
GROUP BY city
HAVING COUNT(*) > 5;  -- only cities with >5 users
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{JOINs}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=SQL, caption={SQL JOINs}]
-- Sample tables
-- users: id, name, email
-- orders: id, user_id (FK), product, amount

-- INNER JOIN: only matching rows from both tables
SELECT u.name, o.product, o.amount
FROM users u
INNER JOIN orders o ON u.id = o.user_id;

-- LEFT JOIN: all users, even without orders
SELECT u.name, o.product
FROM users u
LEFT JOIN orders o ON u.id = o.user_id;

-- RIGHT JOIN: all orders, even without matching user
SELECT u.name, o.product
FROM users u
RIGHT JOIN orders o ON u.id = o.user_id;

-- FULL OUTER JOIN: all rows from both tables
SELECT u.name, o.product
FROM users u
FULL OUTER JOIN orders o ON u.id = o.user_id;

-- Multiple joins
SELECT u.name, o.product, p.total
FROM users u
JOIN orders o ON u.id = o.user_id
JOIN payments p ON o.id = p.order_id;
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{interviewbox}
JOINs are the \textbf{most asked} SQL topic in interviews. Draw Venn diagrams to understand INNER, LEFT, RIGHT, and FULL joins. Practice writing queries that combine 3+ tables.
\end{interviewbox}

\subsection{Subqueries}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=SQL, caption={Subqueries}]
-- Scalar subquery
SELECT name FROM users
WHERE age = (SELECT MAX(age) FROM users);

-- IN subquery
SELECT name FROM users
WHERE id IN (
    SELECT user_id FROM orders WHERE amount > 100
);

-- EXISTS subquery
SELECT name FROM users u
WHERE EXISTS (
    SELECT 1 FROM orders o WHERE o.user_id = u.id
);

-- Subquery in FROM (derived table)
SELECT avg_city_age.city, avg_city_age.avg_age
FROM (
    SELECT city, AVG(age) as avg_age
    FROM users
    GROUP BY city
) as avg_city_age
WHERE avg_city_age.avg_age > 25;
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{CTEs (Common Table Expressions)}

CTEs let you name an intermediate query result and use it as a temporary logical step. They often make complex SQL easier to read than deeply nested subqueries.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=SQL, caption={CTE example}]
WITH monthly_sales AS (
    SELECT
        DATE_TRUNC('month', created_at) AS month,
        SUM(amount) AS total_sales
    FROM orders
    GROUP BY DATE_TRUNC('month', created_at)
)
SELECT month, total_sales
FROM monthly_sales
WHERE total_sales > 10000
ORDER BY month;
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why this matters:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item you can break a large query into named conceptual steps
    \item debugging becomes easier because each step has meaning
    \item many analytics-style queries become far more readable
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Window Functions}

Window functions are one of the most useful advanced SQL features. They let you compute values \emph{across related rows} without collapsing the result into one row per group.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=SQL, caption={Window function examples}]
-- Rank tunes by downloads within each bike model
SELECT
    bike_model,
    name,
    downloads,
    RANK() OVER (
        PARTITION BY bike_model
        ORDER BY downloads DESC
    ) AS popularity_rank
FROM tune_files;

-- Running total of monthly sales
SELECT
    month,
    total_sales,
    SUM(total_sales) OVER (
        ORDER BY month
    ) AS running_total
FROM monthly_sales;
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{examtipbox}
If you are asked for ``the top item in each group,'' a window function is often a better answer than a messy nested subquery. Learn the pattern of \texttt{ROW\_NUMBER()} or \texttt{RANK()} with \texttt{PARTITION BY}.
\end{examtipbox}

\section{Indexing}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=SQL, caption={Indexes}]
-- Create index for faster lookups
CREATE INDEX idx_users_email ON users(email);
CREATE INDEX idx_orders_user ON orders(user_id);

-- Composite index
CREATE INDEX idx_orders_user_date
ON orders(user_id, created_at);
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{notebox}
Indexes speed up \texttt{SELECT} queries but slow down \texttt{INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE} because the index must be updated too. Add indexes on columns you frequently filter or join on.
\end{notebox}

\section{Query Optimisation and Execution Thinking}

Many learners think SQL is only about correct answers. In production, it is also about efficient answers.

\textbf{Useful optimisation questions:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item am I scanning far more rows than necessary?
    \item am I joining before filtering?
    \item is the database able to use an index?
    \item am I selecting only the columns I need?
    \item should this aggregation be precomputed?
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Why \texttt{EXPLAIN} Matters}
Most relational databases can show you the execution plan for a query.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=SQL, caption={Inspecting a query plan}]
EXPLAIN ANALYZE
SELECT *
FROM orders
WHERE user_id = 42
ORDER BY created_at DESC
LIMIT 20;
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{What you are looking for conceptually:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item did it use an index or perform a full table scan?
    \item which step cost the most?
    \item did sorting happen efficiently?
    \item did row counts explode during joins?
\end{itemize}

\begin{warningbox}
Premature optimisation is unhelpful, but ignoring query shape is also a mistake. If a feature becomes slow at scale, SQL literacy is often the difference between a precise fix and random guessing.
\end{warningbox}

\section{Normalization}

\textbf{Normalization} is the process of organizing your database to reduce redundancy:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{1NF (First Normal Form):} Each column contains atomic (indivisible) values. No repeating groups.
    \item \textbf{2NF:} 1NF + every non-key column depends on the \emph{entire} primary key.
    \item \textbf{3NF:} 2NF + no non-key column depends on another non-key column (no transitive dependencies).
\end{itemize}

\section{Transactions and ACID}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=SQL, caption={Transactions}]
BEGIN TRANSACTION;
    UPDATE accounts SET balance = balance - 100 WHERE id = 1;
    UPDATE accounts SET balance = balance + 100 WHERE id = 2;
COMMIT;
-- If anything fails: ROLLBACK;
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{ACID properties:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Atomicity:} All or nothing---the entire transaction succeeds or rolls back.
    \item \textbf{Consistency:} The database moves from one valid state to another.
    \item \textbf{Isolation:} Concurrent transactions do not interfere with each other.
    \item \textbf{Durability:} Committed data survives crashes.
\end{itemize}

\section{ORM vs Raw SQL}

An \textbf{ORM (Object-Relational Mapper)} lets you interact with the database using your programming language (Python objects) instead of writing raw SQL.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Django ORM vs Raw SQL}]
# Django ORM
users = User.objects.filter(age__gte=21, city="London")
                    .order_by("-created_at")[:10]

# Equivalent raw SQL
SELECT * FROM users
WHERE age >= 21 AND city = 'London'
ORDER BY created_at DESC
LIMIT 10;
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{tipbox}
Use the ORM for 90\% of queries (safer, portable, cleaner). Use raw SQL for complex queries, performance-critical operations, or when the ORM cannot express what you need.
\end{tipbox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item SQL is the core language for querying and maintaining relational data.
    \item Joins, aggregations, subqueries, and transactions are central to real backend work.
    \item Understanding SQL makes ORMs more transparent and helps you debug performance and correctness issues.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item When would you use a JOIN instead of separate queries?
    \item Why do transactions matter in multi-step operations?
    \item What problem does an index solve?
    \item Why is ORM knowledge stronger when paired with real SQL understanding?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand relational databases, SQL syntax (CRUD, JOINs, subqueries, aggregation), indexing, normalization, transactions, and ORM vs raw SQL. This knowledge underpins every backend system you build.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 9 — JAVA MASTERY
% ============================================================
\chapter{Java Mastery}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} use Java to sharpen your understanding of strong typing, classes, interfaces, and disciplined object-oriented design. Even if Java is not your primary language, it improves engineering rigour.
\end{studybox}

Java is a statically typed, compiled, object-oriented language that runs on the JVM (Java Virtual Machine). It is used in enterprise software, Android apps (legacy), and is the standard language for teaching OOP and data structures.

\section{Why Java?}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Teaches strict OOP and strong typing discipline.
    \item Massive enterprise ecosystem (Spring, Hibernate).
    \item Standard for coding interviews at many companies.
    \item Your CV lists Java as a technical skill.
\end{itemize}

\section{Core Syntax}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Java basics}]
// Every Java file = one public class with same name as file
public class Main {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        // Variables
        int age = 24;
        double price = 19.99;
        boolean isActive = true;
        String name = "Amzat Karim";
        char grade = 'A';

        // Constants
        final double PI = 3.14159;

        // Type casting
        int x = (int) 3.7;  // 3 (explicit cast)
        double y = 5;       // 5.0 (implicit widening)

        // String operations
        String full = "Hello" + " " + name;
        int len = name.length();
        String upper = name.toUpperCase();
        boolean starts = name.startsWith("Am");

        // Print
        System.out.println("Age: " + age);
        System.out.printf("Name: %s, Age: %d%n", name, age);
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Control Flow and Collections}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Control flow and collections}]
// If-else
if (age < 18) {
    System.out.println("Minor");
} else if (age < 65) {
    System.out.println("Adult");
} else {
    System.out.println("Senior");
}

// For loop
for (int i = 0; i < 10; i++) {
    System.out.println(i);
}

// Enhanced for (for-each)
String[] fruits = {"apple", "banana", "cherry"};
for (String fruit : fruits) {
    System.out.println(fruit);
}

// ArrayList (dynamic array)
import java.util.ArrayList;
ArrayList<String> names = new ArrayList<>();
names.add("Amzat");
names.add("Karim");
names.get(0);       // "Amzat"
names.size();       // 2
names.remove(0);

// HashMap (dictionary)
import java.util.HashMap;
HashMap<String, Integer> scores = new HashMap<>();
scores.put("math", 95);
scores.put("cs", 98);
scores.get("math");          // 95
scores.containsKey("cs");   // true

for (Map.Entry<String, Integer> entry : scores.entrySet()) {
    System.out.println(entry.getKey() + ": " + entry.getValue());
}
\end{lstlisting}

\section{OOP in Java}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Java OOP}]
// Abstract class
public abstract class Vehicle {
    private String make;
    private String model;
    protected int mileage = 0;

    public Vehicle(String make, String model) {
        this.make = make;
        this.model = model;
    }

    // Getters/Setters
    public String getMake() { return make; }
    public void setMake(String make) { this.make = make; }

    // Abstract method (must be implemented by subclass)
    public abstract String getType();

    // Concrete method
    public String display() {
        return make + " " + model;
    }
}

// Interface
public interface Flashable {
    void flash(byte[] firmware);
    boolean verify();
}

// Inheritance + Interface implementation
public class Motorcycle extends Vehicle implements Flashable {
    private int engineCC;

    public Motorcycle(String make, String model, int cc) {
        super(make, model);
        this.engineCC = cc;
    }

    @Override
    public String getType() { return "Motorcycle"; }

    @Override
    public void flash(byte[] firmware) { /* ... */ }

    @Override
    public boolean verify() { return true; }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Exception Handling and Generics}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Java exceptions and generics}]
// Exception handling
try {
    int result = 10 / 0;
} catch (ArithmeticException e) {
    System.err.println("Error: " + e.getMessage());
} catch (Exception e) {
    System.err.println("General error: " + e.getMessage());
} finally {
    System.out.println("Cleanup");
}

// Custom exception
public class TuneValidationException extends Exception {
    public TuneValidationException(String message) {
        super(message);
    }
}

// Generics
public class Repository<T> {
    private ArrayList<T> items = new ArrayList<>();

    public void add(T item) { items.add(item); }
    public T get(int index) { return items.get(index); }
    public int size() { return items.size(); }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Feature} & \textbf{Python} & \textbf{Java} \\
\midrule
Typing & Dynamic & Static \\
Compilation & Interpreted & Compiled to bytecode \\
Syntax & Minimal, clean & Verbose, explicit \\
OOP & Optional & Mandatory \\
Access modifiers & Convention (\_) & public/private/protected \\
Null & \texttt{None} & \texttt{null} \\
Main entry point & Script-level & \texttt{main()} method \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Java reinforces strong typing, explicit structure, and disciplined OOP thinking.
    \item Collections, generics, and exception handling are practical foundations for serious software development.
    \item Learning Java improves how you reason about design even in other languages.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Why are generics valuable in Java?
    \item What does Java teach especially well about type discipline?
    \item Why are interfaces important in OOP design?
    \item How does exception handling improve robustness?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now have working Java knowledge: syntax, OOP, collections, generics, and exception handling. Java reinforces strong typing discipline and strict OOP that makes you a better engineer in any language.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 10 — PHP MASTERY
% ============================================================
\chapter{PHP Mastery}

PHP (PHP: Hypertext Preprocessor) is a server-side scripting language designed for web development. It powers roughly 77\% of websites with known server-side languages, including WordPress, Laravel, and Drupal.

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} treat PHP as an important part of backend literacy even if it is not your main language. It teaches server-side rendering patterns, request-driven execution, and the realities of working with mature production ecosystems.
\end{studybox}

\section{Core Syntax}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=PHP, caption={PHP basics}]
<?php
// Variables (always start with $)
$name = "Amzat Karim";
$age = 24;
$price = 19.99;
$isActive = true;
$nothing = null;

// String interpolation (double quotes only)
echo "Hello, $name! Age: $age\n";
echo 'No interpolation here: $name\n'; // literal $name

// String concatenation uses .
$full = "Hello" . " " . $name;

// Arrays (indexed)
$fruits = ["apple", "banana", "cherry"];
echo $fruits[0]; // apple

// Associative arrays (like Python dict)
$user = [
    "name" => "Amzat",
    "age" => 24,
    "city" => "London"
];
echo $user["name"]; // Amzat

// Type checking
var_dump($name);   // string(12) "Amzat Karim"
echo gettype($age); // integer
?>
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{intuitionbox}
PHP is easiest to understand if you picture a request arriving at the server, PHP code running to build a response, and that response being sent back immediately. Traditional PHP is highly request-oriented.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{Functions and OOP}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=PHP, caption={PHP functions and OOP}]
<?php
// Functions
function greet(string $name, string $greeting = "Hello"): string {
    return "$greeting, $name!";
}

// Arrow functions (PHP 7.4+)
$square = fn($x) => $x ** 2;

// OOP
class Vehicle {
    private string $make;
    protected int $mileage = 0;

    public function __construct(
        string $make,
        public readonly string $model // promoted property
    ) {
        $this->make = $make;
    }

    public function getMake(): string {
        return $this->make;
    }

    public function drive(int $km): string {
        $this->mileage += $km;
        return "Drove {$km}km";
    }
}

// Inheritance
class Motorcycle extends Vehicle {
    public function __construct(
        string $make, string $model,
        private int $engineCC
    ) {
        parent::__construct($make, $model);
    }
}

// Interfaces
interface Flashable {
    public function flash(string $firmware): bool;
}
?>
\end{lstlisting}

\section{PHP for Web}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=PHP, caption={PHP web basics}]
<?php
// GET parameters: example.com/page.php?name=Amzat
$name = $_GET["name"] ?? "Guest";

// POST form data
$email = $_POST["email"] ?? "";

// Sessions
session_start();
$_SESSION["user_id"] = 42;
echo $_SESSION["user_id"];

// Database (PDO - safe from SQL injection)
$pdo = new PDO("mysql:host=localhost;dbname=myapp", "user", "pass");
$stmt = $pdo->prepare("SELECT * FROM users WHERE id = ?");
$stmt->execute([$userId]);
$user = $stmt->fetch(PDO::FETCH_ASSOC);

// JSON API response
header("Content-Type: application/json");
echo json_encode(["status" => "ok", "data" => $user]);
?>
\end{lstlisting}

\section{When PHP Is Still Worth Learning}

PHP matters because:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item a huge number of real businesses still run on PHP
    \item frameworks such as Laravel provide excellent developer experience
    \item it teaches server-side rendering and request-response lifecycle clearly
    \item many maintenance, migration, and integration jobs still involve PHP systems
\end{itemize}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item PHP is a major server-side language with a large production footprint.
    \item It supports procedural code and modern OOP.
    \item It is especially relevant for web requests, sessions, form handling, and server-rendered applications.
    \item Even if you do not specialise in PHP, understanding it improves backend breadth and legacy-system confidence.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Why is PHP still relevant in professional engineering?
    \item How are associative arrays in PHP similar to dictionaries in Python?
    \item Why is PDO preferred over unsafe string-built SQL?
    \item What does session storage enable in web applications?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now have working PHP knowledge. While not your primary language, understanding PHP helps you work with legacy systems and understand request-driven server-side rendering patterns used in frameworks like Laravel.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 11 — MATLAB MASTERY
% ============================================================
\chapter{MATLAB Mastery}

MATLAB (MATrix LABoratory) is a numerical computing language used in engineering, signal processing, and academic research. It excels at matrix operations, data visualisation, and prototyping algorithms.

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} understand MATLAB as a problem-solving environment for numerical work rather than just another syntax to memorise. It is especially useful in engineering, simulation, control systems, and research-heavy workflows.
\end{studybox}

\section{Core Concepts}

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={MATLAB basics}]
% Variables (no declaration needed)
x = 42;
name = 'Amzat';
pi_val = 3.14159;

% Vectors
row_vec = [1, 2, 3, 4, 5];          % 1x5 row vector
col_vec = [1; 2; 3; 4; 5];          % 5x1 column vector
range_vec = 1:0.5:5;                 % 1, 1.5, 2, ..., 5
linspace_vec = linspace(0, 10, 100); % 100 points 0 to 10

% Matrices
A = [1, 2, 3; 4, 5, 6; 7, 8, 9]; % 3x3 matrix
I = eye(3);           % 3x3 identity matrix
Z = zeros(3, 4);      % 3x4 matrix of zeros
O = ones(2, 3);       % 2x3 matrix of ones
R = rand(3, 3);       % 3x3 random matrix

% Matrix operations
B = A';               % Transpose
C = A * B;            % Matrix multiplication
D = A .* A;           % Element-wise multiplication
det_A = det(A);       % Determinant
inv_A = inv(A);       % Inverse (if exists)
[V, D] = eig(A);     % Eigenvalues and eigenvectors
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Control Flow and Functions}

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={MATLAB control flow}]
% If-else
if x > 0
    disp('Positive');
elseif x < 0
    disp('Negative');
else
    disp('Zero');
end

% For loop
for i = 1:10
    fprintf('i = %d\n', i);
end

% While loop
count = 0;
while count < 5
    count = count + 1;
end

% Functions (saved as separate .m file OR local)
function result = quadratic(a, b, c, x)
    result = a*x^2 + b*x + c;
end
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Plotting}

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={MATLAB plotting}]
x = linspace(0, 2*pi, 100);
y = sin(x);

figure;
plot(x, y, 'b-', 'LineWidth', 2);
hold on;
plot(x, cos(x), 'r--', 'LineWidth', 2);
xlabel('x (radians)');
ylabel('Amplitude');
title('Sine and Cosine Waves');
legend('sin(x)', 'cos(x)');
grid on;
hold off;

% 3D plotting
[X, Y] = meshgrid(-5:0.5:5, -5:0.5:5);
Z = sin(sqrt(X.^2 + Y.^2));
figure;
surf(X, Y, Z);
colorbar;
title('3D Surface Plot');
\end{lstlisting}

\section{When MATLAB Is a Good Fit}

MATLAB is particularly strong when:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item you need fast numerical prototyping
    \item matrices and linear algebra dominate the problem
    \item you are doing simulation, control systems, or signal processing
    \item you are working in academic or engineering environments where MATLAB is standard
\end{itemize}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item MATLAB is built for matrix-heavy numerical work.
    \item Its syntax is strong for vectors, matrices, plots, and engineering workflows.
    \item It is especially common in simulation, scientific computing, and academic prototyping.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Why is MATLAB especially comfortable for matrix-centric problems?
    \item What is the difference between \texttt{*} and \texttt{.*} in MATLAB?
    \item Why is plotting such a central MATLAB capability?
    \item In what kinds of work would MATLAB often be preferred over a general-purpose language?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now have working MATLAB knowledge covering matrix operations, control flow, functions, and plotting. MATLAB is useful for prototyping algorithms, signal processing, and engineering simulations.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 12 — COMPUTER SCIENCE CORE
% ============================================================
\chapter{Computer Science Core}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} see computer science as the set of mental tools that let you reason about performance, structure, and trade-offs. Do not memorise definitions only; connect each structure to when and why you would use it.
\end{studybox}

 \begin{notebox}
\textbf{How to study this chapter well:} do not read it like a dictionary. Move in this order: (1) what problem the structure solves, (2) what operation it makes cheap, (3) what operation it makes expensive, and (4) a real programming situation where that trade-off matters.
\end{notebox}

This chapter covers the fundamental computer science knowledge that separates a coder from a software engineer. Data structures and algorithms are essential for writing efficient code and passing technical interviews.

\section{A Mental Model for This Chapter}

Most computer science fundamentals become easier when you stop seeing them as isolated definitions and start seeing them as \textbf{storage and access strategies}.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item An \textbf{array} is good when you want fast indexed access.
    \item A \textbf{linked list} is useful when structure changes often near the front.
    \item A \textbf{stack} is good when the most recent thing matters most.
    \item A \textbf{queue} is good when work must happen in arrival order.
    \item A \textbf{hash map} is good when fast lookup by key matters.
    \item A \textbf{tree} is good when data has hierarchy or sorted structure.
    \item A \textbf{graph} is good when relationships are network-shaped, not tree-shaped.
\end{itemize}

\begin{intuitionbox}
When you are asked to choose a data structure, the real question is usually: ``Which operations must be fast, and which costs can I tolerate?''
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{Memory, Stack, Heap, Processes, and Threads}

Before choosing data structures well, it helps to know where program data and execution contexts live conceptually.

\textbf{Stack:} typically stores function call frames, local variables, and return information. It is fast and structured.

\textbf{Heap:} typically stores objects and data that live longer or have dynamic size. It is flexible but less structured.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=2cm]
    \node[warnbox, minimum width=4cm, minimum height=5.4cm, label=above:{Program memory}] (mem) {};
    \node[flowbox, minimum width=2.8cm, minimum height=1.2cm] (stack1) at ($(mem.north)+(0,-1.0)$) {Call frame: main};
    \node[flowbox, minimum width=2.8cm, minimum height=1.2cm, below=0.15cm of stack1] (stack2) {Call frame: helper()};
    \node[flowbox, minimum width=2.8cm, minimum height=1.2cm, below=0.15cm of stack2] (stack3) {Local values};
    \node[storagebox, minimum width=2.8cm, minimum height=1.7cm, below=1.2cm of stack3] (heap) {Heap objects\\lists, dicts, class instances};

    \node[font=\small, left=1.8cm of stack2] {Stack};
    \node[font=\small, left=1.8cm of heap] {Heap};
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{A simplified memory intuition: stack frames above, heap objects below}
\label{fig:stack-heap-intuition}
\end{figure}

This figure matters because many debugging and performance issues involve lifetime and ownership. Local call information behaves differently from dynamically allocated objects.

\textbf{Process:} an independent running program with its own memory space.

\textbf{Thread:} a unit of execution within a process that shares the process memory with other threads in that same process.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=1.2cm]
    \node[warnbox, minimum width=10.8cm, minimum height=4.5cm, label=above:{One process}] (proc) {};
    \node[storagebox, minimum width=4.2cm, minimum height=1.0cm] (shared) at ($(proc.north)+(0,-1.0)$) {Shared memory / heap};
    \node[flowbox, minimum width=2.2cm, below left=1.2cm and 2.1cm of shared] (t1) {Thread 1};
    \node[flowbox, minimum width=2.2cm, below=1.2cm of shared] (t2) {Thread 2};
    \node[flowbox, minimum width=2.2cm, below right=1.2cm and 2.1cm of shared] (t3) {Thread 3};

    \draw[softarrow] (shared) -- (t1);
    \draw[softarrow] (shared) -- (t2);
    \draw[softarrow] (shared) -- (t3);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Threads share process memory, while separate processes do not}
\label{fig:process-thread-intuition}
\end{figure}

Use this picture to remember the practical trade-off: threads communicate more easily because they share memory, but that same sharing makes synchronization and safety harder.

\section{Arrays}

An \textbf{array} is a contiguous block of memory storing elements of the same type, accessed by index.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Access by index:} $O(1)$ --- instant.
    \item \textbf{Search (unsorted):} $O(n)$ --- must check each element.
    \item \textbf{Insert/Delete at end:} $O(1)$ amortised.
    \item \textbf{Insert/Delete at middle:} $O(n)$ --- must shift elements.
\end{itemize}

\section{Linked Lists}

A \textbf{linked list} stores elements in nodes, where each node contains a value and a pointer to the next node.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Linked list implementation}]
class Node:
    def __init__(self, val, next=None):
        self.val = val
        self.next = next

class LinkedList:
    def __init__(self):
        self.head = None

    def prepend(self, val):
        self.head = Node(val, self.head)

    def append(self, val):
        if not self.head:
            self.head = Node(val)
            return
        curr = self.head
        while curr.next:
            curr = curr.next
        curr.next = Node(val)

    def delete(self, val):
        if self.head and self.head.val == val:
            self.head = self.head.next
            return
        curr = self.head
        while curr and curr.next:
            if curr.next.val == val:
                curr.next = curr.next.next
                return
            curr = curr.next
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l c c}
\toprule
\textbf{Operation} & \textbf{Array} & \textbf{Linked List} \\
\midrule
Access by index & $O(1)$ & $O(n)$ \\
Search & $O(n)$ & $O(n)$ \\
Insert at start & $O(n)$ & $O(1)$ \\
Insert at end & $O(1)$* & $O(n)$ \\
Delete & $O(n)$ & $O(1)$** \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}
{\small *amortised. **if you have a reference to the previous node.}

\section{Stacks and Queues}

\textbf{Stack:} Last-In, First-Out (LIFO). Think of a stack of plates.
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \texttt{push(item)} --- add to top. $O(1)$.
    \item \texttt{pop()} --- remove from top. $O(1)$.
    \item \texttt{peek()} --- look at top without removing. $O(1)$.
    \item \textbf{Uses:} Undo functionality, expression parsing, DFS, call stack.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Queue:} First-In, First-Out (FIFO). Think of a queue at a shop.
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \texttt{enqueue(item)} --- add to back. $O(1)$.
    \item \texttt{dequeue()} --- remove from front. $O(1)$.
    \item \textbf{Uses:} Task scheduling, BFS, message queues, print queues.
\end{itemize}

\section{Hash Maps}

A \textbf{hash map} (dictionary in Python, object/Map in JS) stores key-value pairs with average $O(1)$ lookup.

\textbf{How it works:}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item A \textbf{hash function} converts the key into an integer (hash code).
    \item The hash code is mapped to an index in an internal array.
    \item The value is stored at that index.
    \item If two keys map to the same index (\textbf{collision}), handle it (chaining or open addressing).
\end{enumerate}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l c c}
\toprule
\textbf{Operation} & \textbf{Average} & \textbf{Worst} \\
\midrule
Insert & $O(1)$ & $O(n)$ \\
Lookup & $O(1)$ & $O(n)$ \\
Delete & $O(1)$ & $O(n)$ \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\section{Trees}

A \textbf{tree} is a hierarchical data structure with a root node and child nodes.

\subsection{Binary Search Tree (BST)}
Each node has at most two children. Left child $<$ parent $<$ right child.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={BST implementation}]
class TreeNode:
    def __init__(self, val):
        self.val = val
        self.left = None
        self.right = None

class BST:
    def __init__(self):
        self.root = None

    def insert(self, val):
        self.root = self._insert(self.root, val)

    def _insert(self, node, val):
        if not node:
            return TreeNode(val)
        if val < node.val:
            node.left = self._insert(node.left, val)
        else:
            node.right = self._insert(node.right, val)
        return node

    def search(self, val):
        return self._search(self.root, val)

    def _search(self, node, val):
        if not node or node.val == val:
            return node
        if val < node.val:
            return self._search(node.left, val)
        return self._search(node.right, val)

    # Traversals
    def inorder(self, node):
        if node:
            self.inorder(node.left)
            print(node.val, end=" ")
            self.inorder(node.right)
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Heaps}
A \textbf{min-heap} is a complete binary tree where each parent is smaller than its children. Used for priority queues.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Python heap usage}]
import heapq

nums = [5, 1, 8, 3, 2]
heapq.heapify(nums)        # Convert to min-heap
heapq.heappush(nums, 0)    # Add element
smallest = heapq.heappop(nums)  # Remove smallest
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Graphs}

A \textbf{graph} is a set of \textbf{nodes} (vertices) connected by \textbf{edges}. Graphs model networks, social connections, maps, and dependencies.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Graph representation and BFS/DFS}]
from collections import deque

# Adjacency list representation
graph = {
    "A": ["B", "C"],
    "B": ["A", "D", "E"],
    "C": ["A", "F"],
    "D": ["B"],
    "E": ["B", "F"],
    "F": ["C", "E"],
}

# BFS (Breadth-First Search) - uses queue
def bfs(graph, start):
    visited = set()
    queue = deque([start])
    visited.add(start)
    while queue:
        node = queue.popleft()
        print(node, end=" ")
        for neighbor in graph[node]:
            if neighbor not in visited:
                visited.add(neighbor)
                queue.append(neighbor)

# DFS (Depth-First Search) - uses stack/recursion
def dfs(graph, node, visited=None):
    if visited is None:
        visited = set()
    visited.add(node)
    print(node, end=" ")
    for neighbor in graph[node]:
        if neighbor not in visited:
            dfs(graph, neighbor, visited)
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Sorting Algorithms}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l c c c}
\toprule
\textbf{Algorithm} & \textbf{Best} & \textbf{Average} & \textbf{Worst} \\
\midrule
Bubble Sort & $O(n)$ & $O(n^2)$ & $O(n^2)$ \\
Selection Sort & $O(n^2)$ & $O(n^2)$ & $O(n^2)$ \\
Insertion Sort & $O(n)$ & $O(n^2)$ & $O(n^2)$ \\
Merge Sort & $O(n \log n)$ & $O(n \log n)$ & $O(n \log n)$ \\
Quick Sort & $O(n \log n)$ & $O(n \log n)$ & $O(n^2)$ \\
Heap Sort & $O(n \log n)$ & $O(n \log n)$ & $O(n \log n)$ \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Merge sort implementation}]
def merge_sort(arr):
    if len(arr) <= 1:
        return arr
    mid = len(arr) // 2
    left = merge_sort(arr[:mid])
    right = merge_sort(arr[mid:])
    return merge(left, right)

def merge(left, right):
    result = []
    i = j = 0
    while i < len(left) and j < len(right):
        if left[i] <= right[j]:
            result.append(left[i]); i += 1
        else:
            result.append(right[j]); j += 1
    result.extend(left[i:])
    result.extend(right[j:])
    return result
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Big O Notation}

\textbf{Big O} describes how an algorithm's runtime or space grows as the input size $n$ increases.

\begin{notebox}
\textbf{A calmer way to read Big O:} do not ask, ``What symbol should I memorise?'' Ask instead, ``If I double or massively increase the input, how fast does the work grow?'' Big O is about growth, not stopwatch timings on your laptop.
\end{notebox}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Big O} & \textbf{Name} & \textbf{Example} \\
\midrule
$O(1)$ & Constant & Hash table lookup \\
$O(\log n)$ & Logarithmic & Binary search \\
$O(n)$ & Linear & Linear search \\
$O(n \log n)$ & Linearithmic & Merge sort \\
$O(n^2)$ & Quadratic & Nested loops, bubble sort \\
$O(2^n)$ & Exponential & Recursive Fibonacci (naive) \\
$O(n!)$ & Factorial & Permutations \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{interviewbox}
Big O analysis is asked in \textbf{every} technical interview. For any solution you propose, you must state its time and space complexity. Practice analysing your own code.
\end{interviewbox}

\section{Common CS Mistakes Learners Make}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Memorising names without use cases.} Knowing that a heap exists is not the same as recognising a priority-queue problem.
    \item \textbf{Ignoring constant factors entirely.} Big O matters, but for small inputs a simpler solution can still be the right engineering choice.
    \item \textbf{Confusing tree problems with graph problems.} Trees are a special kind of graph, but graph problems often need cycle handling and visited sets.
    \item \textbf{Using recursion without tracing it.} If you cannot explain the base case and the shrinking step, the recursion is not yet clear.
    \item \textbf{Treating interview patterns as magic tricks.} Two pointers, BFS, DFS, and dynamic programming are reasoning tools, not memorised spells.
\end{itemize}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{CS revision drill:}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Pick three structures from this chapter and state one ideal use case for each.
    \item For each one, state one operation it is bad at.
    \item Explain one problem where a hash map changes an $O(n^2)$ idea into an $O(n)$ one.
    \item Explain when a queue is better than a stack using a real application example.
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercisebox}

\section{Design Patterns}

Key patterns every engineer should know:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Singleton:} Ensure a class has only one instance (e.g., database connection pool).
    \item \textbf{Factory:} Create objects without specifying the exact class (e.g., \texttt{create\_serializer()}).
    \item \textbf{Observer:} Objects subscribe to events and get notified of changes (e.g., event listeners).
    \item \textbf{Strategy:} Define a family of algorithms and make them interchangeable (e.g., sorting strategies).
    \item \textbf{Repository:} Abstract data access behind a clean interface (used in your Django models).
    \item \textbf{MVC (Model-View-Controller):} Separate data, presentation, and logic (Django's MTV pattern).
    \item \textbf{Middleware:} Chain of processing functions (Express middleware, Django middleware).
    \item \textbf{Decorator:} Add behaviour to objects dynamically (Python decorators).
\end{itemize}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Data structures exist because different workloads need different strengths.
    \item Big O helps you reason about how an approach scales.
    \item Strong CS fundamentals improve both interview performance and production engineering judgement.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item When is a hash map a better choice than a list?
    \item What kind of problem naturally suggests a stack or queue?
    \item Why does Big O matter even if inputs are small during testing?
    \item What is the practical difference between a tree and a graph?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand core CS: arrays, linked lists, stacks, queues, hash maps, trees, heaps, graphs, sorting algorithms, Big O complexity, and design patterns. This is the foundation for writing efficient code and passing technical interviews.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 13 — WEB DEVELOPMENT
% ============================================================
\chapter{Web Development}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} understand the web as a system, not just a pile of technologies. HTML gives structure, CSS controls presentation, JavaScript adds behaviour, and the browser coordinates rendering, events, networking, and accessibility.
\end{studybox}

\section{What Web Development Actually Means}

Web development is the discipline of building software that runs in the browser and communicates with backend services over the network. A learner often meets HTML, CSS, and JavaScript as separate topics, but in practice they form one pipeline:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item HTML defines the meaning and structure of content.
    \item CSS defines layout and presentation.
    \item JavaScript responds to user actions and updates the page.
    \item The browser turns all of that into pixels on the screen.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{intuitionbox}
The browser is a runtime environment. It is not just a viewer. It parses documents, runs JavaScript, builds an in-memory DOM tree, applies CSS rules, calculates layout, paints pixels, handles events, and makes network requests.
\end{intuitionbox}

The browser pipeline is easier to remember visually than as a paragraph list. This diagram shows how structure, style, and script combine before the user sees pixels.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=1.4cm]
    \node[databox] (html) {HTML};
    \node[databox, below=of html] (css) {CSS};
    \node[databox, below=of css] (js) {JavaScript};

    \node[flowbox, right=2.3cm of html] (dom) {DOM};
    \node[flowbox, right=2.3cm of css] (cssom) {CSS rules\\/ CSSOM};
    \node[flowbox, right=2.3cm of js] (events) {Events\\and updates};

    \node[storagebox, right=2.5cm of cssom] (render) {Render tree};
    \node[flowbox, right=of render] (layout) {Layout};
    \node[flowbox, right=of layout] (paint) {Paint\\to screen};

    \draw[flowarrow] (html) -- (dom);
    \draw[flowarrow] (css) -- (cssom);
    \draw[softarrow] (js) -- (events);
    \draw[flowarrow] (dom) -- (render);
    \draw[flowarrow] (cssom) -- (render);
    \draw[softarrow] (events) -- (render);
    \draw[flowarrow] (render) -- (layout);
    \draw[flowarrow] (layout) -- (paint);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Simplified browser rendering flow}
\label{fig:browser-rendering-flow}
\end{figure}

This matters because many frontend bugs are really pipeline bugs: wrong HTML structure, conflicting CSS, or JavaScript causing too many DOM updates and unnecessary layout work.

\section{HTML Fundamentals}
