HTML (HyperText Markup Language) defines the \textbf{structure} and \textbf{content} of web pages. It tells the browser \emph{what} each piece of content is: a heading, a paragraph, a navigation area, a button, a list, an image, or a form control.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=HTML, caption={HTML structure}]
<!DOCTYPE html>
<html lang="en">
<head>
    <meta charset="UTF-8">
    <meta name="viewport" content="width=device-width, initial-scale=1.0">
    <meta name="description" content="RevSync ECU Tuning Platform">
    <title>RevSync</title>
    <link rel="stylesheet" href="styles.css">
</head>
<body>
    <header>
        <nav>
            <a href="/">Home</a>
            <a href="/tunes">Tunes</a>
        </nav>
    </header>
    <main>
        <h1>Welcome to RevSync</h1>
        <p>The motorcycle ECU tuning ecosystem.</p>
        <section>
            <h2>Features</h2>
            <ul>
                <li>Flash ECU tunes</li>
                <li>Monitor diagnostics</li>
                <li>Marketplace for tunes</li>
            </ul>
        </section>
        <form action="/api/contact" method="POST">
            <label for="email">Email:</label>
            <input type="email" id="email" name="email" required>
            <button type="submit">Submit</button>
        </form>
    </main>
    <footer>
        <p>&copy; 2026 RevSync</p>
    </footer>
    <script src="app.js"></script>
</body>
</html>
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{How to read this example:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item the \texttt{<head>} contains metadata for the browser and search engines
    \item the \texttt{<body>} contains visible content
    \item landmarks such as \texttt{<header>}, \texttt{<nav>}, and \texttt{<main>} help structure the document
    \item the form declares what inputs are needed and what happens when the user submits
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Semantic HTML and Accessibility}
\textbf{Semantic elements} include \texttt{<header>}, \texttt{<nav>}, \texttt{<main>}, \texttt{<section>}, \texttt{<article>}, \texttt{<aside>}, \texttt{<footer>}, \texttt{<figure>}, and \texttt{<figcaption>}.

Use semantic HTML because it improves:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Accessibility:} screen readers understand the page better.
    \item \textbf{SEO:} search engines can infer page structure more accurately.
    \item \textbf{Maintainability:} your code communicates intent clearly.
\end{itemize}

\begin{warningbox}
A page that looks correct visually can still be poorly built semantically. This matters in real products: accessibility defects are usability defects, not optional polish.
\end{warningbox}

\section{CSS Fundamentals}

CSS (Cascading Style Sheets) controls the \textbf{visual presentation} of HTML elements.

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={CSS core concepts}]
/* Box Model: margin > border > padding > content */
.card {
    margin: 16px;
    padding: 24px;
    border: 1px solid #e0e0e0;
    border-radius: 8px;
    box-shadow: 0 2px 8px rgba(0,0,0,0.1);
}

/* Flexbox (1D layout) */
.navbar {
    display: flex;
    justify-content: space-between;  /* main axis */
    align-items: center;             /* cross axis */
    gap: 16px;
}

/* Grid (2D layout) */
.dashboard {
    display: grid;
    grid-template-columns: 250px 1fr 1fr;
    grid-template-rows: auto 1fr auto;
    gap: 16px;
    min-height: 100vh;
}

/* Responsive design */
@media (max-width: 768px) {
    .dashboard {
        grid-template-columns: 1fr;
    }
}

/* CSS Variables */
:root {
    --primary: #1a73e8;
    --bg-dark: #1e1e2e;
    --text: #e0e0e0;
}

.button {
    background: var(--primary);
    color: white;
    padding: 12px 24px;
    border: none;
    border-radius: 6px;
    cursor: pointer;
    transition: background 0.2s ease;
}
.button:hover {
    background: #1557b0;
}
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{The Box Model}
Every visible element on the page can be understood as a box:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{content} is the actual text or image
    \item \textbf{padding} is the internal space around the content
    \item \textbf{border} wraps the padding
    \item \textbf{margin} creates external space around the element
\end{itemize}

This one mental model explains a huge amount of layout confusion. If an element is ``too cramped'' or ``too far away,'' the answer is usually hidden somewhere in margin, padding, width, height, or layout context.

\subsection{Flexbox, Grid, and Responsive Layout}
\textbf{Flexbox} is best for one-dimensional layout: rows, columns, nav bars, toolbars, and alignment problems.

\textbf{Grid} is best for two-dimensional layout: dashboards, card grids, page regions, and more deliberate structural layout.

\textbf{Responsive design} means the layout adapts to different screen sizes rather than assuming a large desktop monitor. A \textbf{mobile-first} approach starts with the simplest narrow-screen version and progressively enhances for larger screens.

\begin{examtipbox}
When comparing Flexbox and Grid, avoid saying ``Grid is better.'' The correct answer is contextual. Flexbox excels at alignment and flow in one dimension; Grid excels at explicit structure in two dimensions.
\end{examtipbox}

\section{Browser Rendering Basics}

You do not need to become a browser engineer, but a little rendering knowledge helps with performance and debugging.

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item The browser parses HTML into the DOM.
    \item It parses CSS into style rules.
    \item It combines these into a render tree.
    \item It calculates layout: size and position of elements.
    \item It paints pixels to the screen.
\end{enumerate}

JavaScript can trigger new layout and paint work when it changes the DOM or styles. This is why unnecessary DOM updates can hurt performance.

\section{JavaScript in the Browser}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={DOM manipulation and events}]
// Select elements
const heading = document.getElementById("title");
const buttons = document.querySelectorAll(".btn");
const form = document.querySelector("form");

// Modify content
heading.textContent = "Updated Title";
heading.innerHTML = "<em>Styled</em> Title";
heading.style.color = "#1a73e8";
heading.classList.add("active");

// Event handling
form.addEventListener("submit", (e) => {
    e.preventDefault(); // stop page reload
    const formData = new FormData(form);
    const email = formData.get("email");
    console.log("Email:", email);
});

// Fetch API (call your backend)
async function loadTunes() {
    const response = await fetch("/api/tunes/", {
        headers: {
            "Authorization": "Bearer " + token,
            "Content-Type": "application/json",
        }
    });
    const data = await response.json();
    renderTunes(data);
}
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{DOM and Events}
The \textbf{DOM (Document Object Model)} is the browser's in-memory tree representation of the page. JavaScript reads and updates the DOM. Events are the browser's way of telling your code that something happened: a click, key press, scroll, input change, or form submission.

\textbf{Common event-driven pattern:}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item user interacts with the UI
    \item browser fires an event
    \item your event handler runs
    \item state changes
    \item the UI is updated
\end{enumerate}

\section{Forms, Validation, and Fetch}

Forms are one of the most important bridges between user input and application logic.

\textbf{Validation happens at multiple layers:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{HTML validation:} \texttt{required}, \texttt{type="email"}, min/max constraints
    \item \textbf{JavaScript validation:} richer client-side checks and UX
    \item \textbf{Backend validation:} final authority; must never be skipped
\end{itemize}

\begin{projectbox}
In a real product, a user registration form may validate email format in the browser for quick feedback, then validate uniqueness, password policy, and permission rules on the backend. Good engineering uses both layers for different reasons.
\end{projectbox}

\textbf{Fetch lifecycle:}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item initiate the request
    \item wait for the response
    \item check status codes
    \item parse JSON or text
    \item update UI state: loading, success, empty, or error
\end{enumerate}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item HTML provides structure and meaning.
    \item CSS controls layout, spacing, and presentation.
    \item JavaScript makes pages interactive through the DOM and events.
    \item The browser parses, lays out, and paints the page.
    \item Good web development includes accessibility, responsive design, validation, and careful UI state handling.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Why is semantic HTML important beyond SEO?
    \item What problem does the box model help you reason about?
    \item When should you choose Flexbox instead of Grid?
    \item What is the DOM?
    \item Why must backend validation still exist even if the browser validates a form?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Practice task:} build a simple contact page with semantic HTML, accessible labels, responsive CSS, and a JavaScript form submission handler that shows loading, success, and error states.
\end{exercisebox}

\checkpoint{You now understand the web stack at a practical level: semantic HTML, CSS layout, responsiveness, rendering intuition, DOM events, forms, validation, and fetch-based API integration. This is the foundation for React and all modern frontend work.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 14 — REACT + FRONTEND ENGINEERING
% ============================================================
\chapter{React + Frontend Engineering}

React is a JavaScript library for building user interfaces using reusable \textbf{components}. It uses a \textbf{declarative} approach: you describe \emph{what} the UI should look like for a given state, and React figures out \emph{how} to update the DOM efficiently.

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} understand why React became dominant in frontend engineering. Do not memorise hooks mechanically. Instead, learn the real problem React solves: keeping the user interface consistent with changing application state.
\end{studybox}

\begin{intuitionbox}
React is best understood as a \textbf{state-to-UI mapping system}. You keep data in state, and the component describes how the UI should look for that state. When the state changes, React recalculates the UI.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{Core Concepts}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={React component basics (JSX)}]
import React, { useState, useEffect } from "react";

// Functional component with props
function TuneCard({ tune, onSelect }) {
    return (
        <div className="tune-card">
            <h3>{tune.name}</h3>
            <p>Engine: {tune.engineCC}cc</p>
            <p>Author: {tune.author}</p>
            <button onClick={() => onSelect(tune.id)}>
                View Details
            </button>
        </div>
    );
}

// Component with state
function TuneList() {
    const [tunes, setTunes] = useState([]);
    const [loading, setLoading] = useState(true);
    const [error, setError] = useState(null);
    const [search, setSearch] = useState("");

    // Side effect: fetch data on mount
    useEffect(() => {
        async function fetchTunes() {
            try {
                const res = await fetch("/api/tunes/");
                const data = await res.json();
                setTunes(data);
            } catch (err) {
                setError(err.message);
            } finally {
                setLoading(false);
            }
        }
        fetchTunes();
    }, []); // empty deps = run once on mount

    // Derived state
    const filtered = tunes.filter(t =>
        t.name.toLowerCase().includes(search.toLowerCase())
    );

    if (loading) return <p>Loading...</p>;
    if (error) return <p>Error: {error}</p>;

    return (
        <div>
            <input
                type="text"
                value={search}
                onChange={e => setSearch(e.target.value)}
                placeholder="Search tunes..."
            />
            <div className="tune-grid">
                {filtered.map(tune => (
                    <TuneCard
                        key={tune.id}
                        tune={tune}
                        onSelect={id => console.log("Selected:", id)}
                    />
                ))}
            </div>
        </div>
    );
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Important ideas inside the example:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Component:} a reusable UI unit with its own logic.
    \item \textbf{Props:} inputs passed from parent to child.
    \item \textbf{State:} values that can change over time and trigger re-rendering.
    \item \textbf{Effect:} logic that interacts with the outside world such as fetching data or subscribing to timers.
\end{itemize}

\section{Why React Exists}

In plain JavaScript applications, you often end up manually selecting elements, updating the DOM, and coordinating state by hand. As the UI grows, this becomes brittle.

React improves this by making you think in three layers:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item what data the screen needs
    \item what components represent parts of the interface
    \item how the UI should look for each state
\end{enumerate}

This is why React scales well for dashboards, authenticated apps, complex forms, and live products with many interactive states.

\section{The Render Cycle and One-Way Data Flow}

React becomes much easier when you understand two rules:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item state changes trigger re-renders
    \item data normally flows from parent components down to child components through props
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{What a render means:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item React calls your component function again
    \item your component calculates what the UI should look like now
    \item React updates the real DOM efficiently based on what changed
\end{itemize}

\begin{intuitionbox}
In React, rendering is not a failure or an emergency. It is the normal mechanism by which UI stays in sync with state.
\end{intuitionbox}

The diagram below captures the normal React feedback loop. It is useful because beginners often treat state, props, and events as separate ideas rather than one flow.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=1.8cm]
    \node[storagebox] (state) {Parent state};
    \node[flowbox, right=of state] (render) {Render component};
    \node[databox, right=of render] (ui) {UI on screen};
    \node[warnbox, below=of ui] (event) {User event\\click, type, submit};
    \node[flowbox, left=of event] (update) {\texttt{setState}\\or handler logic};

    \draw[flowarrow] (state) -- node[above, font=\small]{props / values} (render);
    \draw[flowarrow] (render) -- (ui);
    \draw[flowarrow] (ui) -- (event);
    \draw[flowarrow] (event) -- (update);
    \draw[flowarrow] (update) -- node[left, font=\small]{new state} (state);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{React data flow and re-render loop}
\label{fig:react-data-flow}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Why this matters:} React becomes much easier once you stop thinking ``the DOM changed'' and start thinking ``an event changed state, so React recalculated the UI.'' That is the real engineering mental model.

\section{Key Hooks}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={React hooks}]
// useState: manage component state
const [count, setCount] = useState(0);

// useEffect: side effects (fetch, timers, subscriptions)
useEffect(() => {
    const timer = setInterval(() => tick(), 1000);
    return () => clearInterval(timer); // cleanup
}, [dependency]); // re-run when dependency changes

// useRef: persist values without re-rendering
const inputRef = useRef(null);
inputRef.current.focus();

// useMemo: memoize expensive computations
const expensive = useMemo(() => {
    return computeHeavy(data);
}, [data]);

// useCallback: memoize function references
const handleClick = useCallback(() => {
    doSomething(id);
}, [id]);

// useContext: access shared state without prop drilling
const ThemeContext = React.createContext("light");
const theme = useContext(ThemeContext);

// Custom hook
function useApi(url) {
    const [data, setData] = useState(null);
    const [loading, setLoading] = useState(true);
    useEffect(() => {
        fetch(url)
            .then(r => r.json())
            .then(setData)
            .finally(() => setLoading(false));
    }, [url]);
    return { data, loading };
}
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{When Hooks Help}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{\texttt{useState}} stores local component state such as input values or modal visibility.
    \item \textbf{\texttt{useEffect}} handles side effects such as fetching data, attaching listeners, or syncing with external systems.
    \item \textbf{\texttt{useRef}} stores mutable values that do not need re-rendering.
    \item \textbf{\texttt{useContext}} shares state across multiple components without passing props down many layers.
\end{itemize}

\begin{warningbox}
The most common beginner React mistake is putting everything into \texttt{useEffect}. If a value can be derived directly from existing props or state, compute it during render instead of syncing it with another piece of state.
\end{warningbox}

\section{Forms, Data Fetching, and UI States}

Real frontend work is not just rendering static cards. It is mostly state management:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item form input state
    \item validation state
    \item loading state
    \item error state
    \item empty state
    \item success state
\end{itemize}

\textbf{A strong frontend component handles all of these explicitly.}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={A practical React form pattern}]
function SignUpForm() {
  const [email, setEmail] = useState("");
  const [submitting, setSubmitting] = useState(false);
  const [error, setError] = useState("");
  const [success, setSuccess] = useState(false);

  async function handleSubmit(e) {
    e.preventDefault();
    setSubmitting(true);
    setError("");

    try {
      const res = await fetch("/api/signup", {
        method: "POST",
        headers: { "Content-Type": "application/json" },
        body: JSON.stringify({ email })
      });

      if (!res.ok) {
        throw new Error("Sign-up failed");
      }

      setSuccess(true);
    } catch (err) {
      setError(err.message);
    } finally {
      setSubmitting(false);
    }
  }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{A Better Data Fetching Mental Model}

When a component fetches data, ask four questions:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item what triggers the request?
    \item what should the UI show while loading?
    \item what should happen if the request fails?
    \item when should stale data be refreshed?
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{That is why real frontend engineering is not just ``call fetch.'' It is request lifecycle design.}

\section{Frontend Architecture Basics}

As applications grow, frontend structure matters.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Presentational components} focus on rendering.
    \item \textbf{Container or feature components} handle fetching, state, and orchestration.
    \item \textbf{Custom hooks} extract reusable stateful logic.
    \item \textbf{Shared UI primitives} keep buttons, inputs, modals, and layout patterns consistent.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{A practical mental model:}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item keep domain state close to where it is used
    \item lift state only when multiple children need it
    \item centralise only the state that is truly global
\end{enumerate}

\section{Common React Mistakes}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item storing derived values in state instead of computing them during render
    \item using array indexes as keys for dynamic lists
    \item putting too much logic into one component
    \item triggering effects unnecessarily with unstable dependencies
    \item forgetting loading, error, and empty states
    \item mixing UI concerns with domain/service logic carelessly
\end{itemize}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Practice task:} build a small React feature with:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item one list component
    \item one form component
    \item loading, empty, success, and error states
    \item one custom hook for data fetching
    \item TypeScript props and response types
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercisebox}

\section{React Best Practices}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Keep components \textbf{small and focused} (single responsibility).
    \item Lift state up to the nearest common ancestor.
    \item Use \textbf{composition} over inheritance.
    \item Always provide a unique \texttt{key} prop for lists.
    \item Handle loading, error, and empty states for every data fetch.
    \item Use TypeScript for all React projects.
    \item Extract reusable logic into \textbf{custom hooks}.
    \item Co-locate related files (component + styles + tests).
\end{enumerate}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item React maps application state to UI.
    \item Components, props, and state are the core building blocks.
    \item Hooks let functional components manage state, effects, references, and shared context.
    \item Real frontend engineering means explicitly handling loading, error, empty, and success states.
    \item Good architecture keeps components focused and shared logic reusable.
    \item One-way data flow and render-cycle thinking make complex interfaces easier to reason about.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What problem does React solve better than manual DOM manipulation?
    \item What is the difference between props and state?
    \item When should you use an effect?
    \item Why are custom hooks valuable?
    \item What UI states should a serious data-fetching component handle?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Practice task:} build a small product list page with search, loading, error, and empty states. Then refactor the fetching logic into a custom hook.
\end{exercisebox}

\checkpoint{You now understand React as a practical frontend engineering tool: components, JSX, props, state, hooks, data-fetching patterns, form state, and architectural habits that matter in real products.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 15 — REACT NATIVE + MOBILE ENGINEERING
% ============================================================
\chapter{React Native + Mobile Engineering}

React Native lets you build native mobile apps using React and JavaScript/TypeScript. Your RevSync mobile app is built with React Native and Expo.

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} understand how mobile engineering differs from web engineering even when the component model looks familiar. Mobile apps deal with navigation stacks, limited screen space, intermittent connectivity, device permissions, and more serious state persistence requirements.
\end{studybox}

\section{Core Differences from React Web}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Concept} & \textbf{React (Web)} & \textbf{React Native} \\
\midrule
Container & \texttt{<div>} & \texttt{<View>} \\
Text & \texttt{<p>}, \texttt{<span>} & \texttt{<Text>} \\
Image & \texttt{<img>} & \texttt{<Image>} \\
Scrolling & Native & \texttt{<ScrollView>} / \texttt{<FlatList>} \\
Styles & CSS files & \texttt{StyleSheet.create()} \\
Links & \texttt{<a>} & Navigation library \\
Input & \texttt{<input>} & \texttt{<TextInput>} \\
Click & \texttt{onClick} & \texttt{onPress} \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{intuitionbox}
React Native is not ``React for tiny websites.'' It uses the React mental model, but it renders to native platform components and lives inside mobile constraints such as battery, memory, gestures, backgrounding, and device permissions.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{Component Example}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={React Native screen}]
import React, { useState } from "react";
import {
    View, Text, TextInput, TouchableOpacity,
    FlatList, StyleSheet, ActivityIndicator
} from "react-native";

interface Tune {
    id: string;
    name: string;
    engineCC: number;
}

export default function TuneListScreen() {
    const [tunes, setTunes] = useState<Tune[]>([]);
    const [loading, setLoading] = useState(false);

    const renderTune = ({ item }: { item: Tune }) => (
        <TouchableOpacity style={styles.card}>
            <Text style={styles.title}>{item.name}</Text>
            <Text style={styles.sub}>{item.engineCC}cc</Text>
        </TouchableOpacity>
    );

    if (loading) return <ActivityIndicator size="large" />;

    return (
        <View style={styles.container}>
            <FlatList
                data={tunes}
                renderItem={renderTune}
                keyExtractor={item => item.id}
                ListEmptyComponent={
                    <Text>No tunes found</Text>
                }
            />
        </View>
    );
}

const styles = StyleSheet.create({
    container: { flex: 1, padding: 16, backgroundColor: "#1e1e2e" },
    card: { padding: 16, marginBottom: 8, backgroundColor: "#2a2a3e",
            borderRadius: 8 },
    title: { fontSize: 18, fontWeight: "bold", color: "#fff" },
    sub: { fontSize: 14, color: "#aaa", marginTop: 4 },
});
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why this matters:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item mobile lists must be efficient because devices have tighter resource limits
    \item styling is JavaScript-based rather than CSS-file based
    \item navigation is handled by a library, not the browser URL bar
\end{itemize}

\section{Navigation}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={React Navigation setup}]
import { NavigationContainer } from "@react-navigation/native";
import { createNativeStackNavigator } from
    "@react-navigation/native-stack";

type RootStackParams = {
    Home: undefined;
    TuneDetail: { tuneId: string };
    Profile: undefined;
};

const Stack = createNativeStackNavigator<RootStackParams>();

export default function App() {
    return (
        <NavigationContainer>
            <Stack.Navigator initialRouteName="Home">
                <Stack.Screen name="Home"
                    component={HomeScreen} />
                <Stack.Screen name="TuneDetail"
                    component={TuneDetailScreen} />
                <Stack.Screen name="Profile"
                    component={ProfileScreen} />
            </Stack.Navigator>
        </NavigationContainer>
    );
}
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Navigation Is Part of Product Architecture}

Mobile navigation is not just a UI detail. It shapes how users move through the product and how state is passed between screens.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Stack navigation:} good for flows where the user drills deeper, such as marketplace $\to$ tune detail $\to$ checkout.
    \item \textbf{Tab navigation:} good for major top-level areas such as Home, Garage, Marketplace, Profile.
    \item \textbf{Modal flows:} good for focused tasks or confirmations that should feel separate from the main stack.
\end{itemize}

\begin{warningbox}
Do not pass huge amounts of business state through navigation params. Use params for screen context, not as a substitute for proper state management or persistence.
\end{warningbox}

\section{Mobile-Specific Concepts}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{AsyncStorage:} Key-value storage (like localStorage for mobile).
    \item \textbf{Secure storage:} For JWT tokens and sensitive data.
    \item \textbf{FlatList vs ScrollView:} Use FlatList for large lists (virtualised rendering).
    \item \textbf{Platform-specific code:} \texttt{Platform.OS === "ios"} or \texttt{.android.tsx} files.
    \item \textbf{Expo:} Managed workflow that simplifies builds, OTA updates, and native APIs.
\end{itemize}

\section{State Persistence and Interruption Safety}

Mobile users switch apps, lose signal, lock screens, and sometimes run out of battery. This means mobile apps need a stronger attitude toward persistence than many simple web apps.

\textbf{Ask these questions for every important mobile flow:}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item if the app closes right now, what state would be lost?
    \item does that state matter enough to persist locally or remotely?
    \item can the user safely resume later?
    \item could partial progress create a misleading or dangerous state?
\end{enumerate}

\begin{projectbox}
In RevSync, flash workflows, backup status, entitlement state, and legal acceptance are not cosmetic state. They are product truth. That is why persistence and recovery logic matter so much more than in a casual content app.
\end{projectbox}

\section{Mobile Architecture and Real-World Concerns}

\subsection{Offline and Partial Connectivity}
Mobile apps must assume the network is sometimes slow, unstable, or absent. This changes how you design features:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item cache important data locally
    \item show explicit offline and retry states
    \item avoid pretending a server write succeeded if it only succeeded locally
    \item persist critical workflow state if the app is backgrounded or interrupted
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Secure Storage}
Sensitive data such as tokens, refresh credentials, and device secrets should not be treated like ordinary preferences. Use secure storage mechanisms rather than plain unencrypted local storage.

\subsection{List Performance}
\texttt{FlatList} is preferred over \texttt{ScrollView} for long datasets because it renders items lazily instead of mounting everything at once. This matters a lot for activity histories, catalog pages, logs, and message feeds.

\subsection{Permissions and Device APIs}
Mobile apps often need access to:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Bluetooth
    \item camera
    \item file system
    \item push notifications
    \item location
\end{itemize}

These permissions should be requested deliberately, explained clearly, and handled gracefully if denied.

\subsection{Backgrounding and App Lifecycle}
Apps do not run in a perfectly stable foreground session. They are paused, backgrounded, interrupted by phone calls, and killed by the operating system.

\textbf{Important implications:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item save critical state before it is too late
    \item expect network requests to be interrupted
    \item restore the right screen or workflow state when the user returns
    \item avoid assuming a long-running process will stay alive forever
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Mobile UI Performance}
Smooth mobile experiences require extra care because devices have tighter CPU, memory, and battery constraints.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item avoid unnecessary re-renders
    \item use \texttt{FlatList} for long lists
    \item avoid giant images and overly heavy component trees
    \item memoize expensive work only when profiling suggests it matters
    \item prefer perceived responsiveness: clear loading states, staged rendering, optimistic feedback where safe
\end{itemize}

\section{Common React Native Mistakes}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item treating mobile like a small web page
    \item ignoring offline or interrupted states
    \item storing sensitive data in insecure storage
    \item passing too much logic through navigation params
    \item using \texttt{ScrollView} for long dynamic lists
    \item failing to account for permission denial paths
    \item building flows that cannot recover after app backgrounding
\end{itemize}

\begin{examtipbox}
If asked about mobile architecture, mention not only screens and APIs, but also offline behaviour, lifecycle interruptions, secure storage, permissions, and performance. That is what makes the answer sound like mobile engineering rather than web knowledge reused on a phone.
\end{examtipbox}

\begin{projectbox}
In RevSync, mobile engineering is safety-relevant. BLE connection state, backup progress, recovery state, and flash readiness all need durable state handling. This is a good example of why mobile apps cannot rely only on pretty screens; they need careful workflow orchestration.
\end{projectbox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item React Native reuses React ideas but targets native mobile UI.
    \item Mobile development introduces navigation stacks, secure storage, performance constraints, permissions, and offline thinking.
    \item \texttt{FlatList}, navigation libraries, and storage APIs are central tools in real apps.
    \item Mobile workflows often need stronger persistence and recovery logic than equivalent web workflows.
    \item Production mobile engineering must account for interruption, backgrounding, and device-level constraints.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What are the main differences between React web and React Native?
    \item Why is \texttt{FlatList} usually better than \texttt{ScrollView} for long datasets?
    \item Why does mobile engineering require stronger offline thinking?
    \item Why should sensitive tokens use secure storage?
    \item What extra concerns appear in mobile apps that do not usually appear in basic web pages?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Practice task:} design a mobile screen that fetches a list of items, caches the last successful result locally, and shows the correct UI for loading, offline, empty, and error states.
\end{exercisebox}

\checkpoint{You now understand React Native as a real mobile engineering environment: native components, navigation, styling, secure storage, performance-aware lists, permissions, and the architectural mindset needed for production mobile apps.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 16 — BACKEND ENGINEERING
% ============================================================
\chapter{Backend Engineering}

This chapter covers backend fundamentals that apply regardless of framework (Django, Flask, Express). Read it as the chapter that teaches you \textbf{how a server thinks}. Framework-specific chapters later will make much more sense once this mental model is clear.

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{How to study this chapter:} first understand the request lifecycle in plain English, then attach framework vocabulary to it. If you try to memorise endpoints, middleware, serializers, and auth rules before seeing the whole flow, backend engineering will feel fragmented.
\end{studybox}

\begin{intuitionbox}
A backend is the \textbf{decision-making and record-keeping side} of an application. The frontend is what the user sees; the backend is what decides whether an action is allowed, how data is stored, how business rules are enforced, and what response should be returned.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{Core Backend Concepts}

When a learner first meets backend development, it often looks like a jumble of routes, database queries, and JSON responses. The easiest way to make it coherent is to think of the backend as a pipeline:

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item A client sends a request.
    \item The server receives it on a URL.
    \item Routing chooses the handler.
    \item Middleware applies cross-cutting rules such as logging or authentication.
    \item Business logic decides what should happen.
    \item The database is read from or written to.
    \item The result is serialized into a response and sent back.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Important backend terms:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Server:} A running program that listens for incoming requests and sends responses.
    \item \textbf{Route:} A mapping between a URL and a handler function. \texttt{/api/users/} might map to \texttt{list\_users()}.
    \item \textbf{Middleware:} Shared logic that runs before or after your route handler. Logging, authentication, CORS, rate limiting, and error handling usually live here.
    \item \textbf{Controller / View / Handler:} The function or class that contains the request-specific logic.
    \item \textbf{CRUD:} Create, Read, Update, Delete. Many backend APIs are built around these four operations.
    \item \textbf{REST:} A design convention for structuring APIs using resources, URLs, and HTTP methods.
\end{itemize}

\section{The Request Lifecycle}

\textbf{Toy example:} imagine a user presses ``Buy Tune'' in RevSync.

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item The mobile app sends \texttt{POST /api/purchases/}.
    \item The backend checks whether the user is logged in.
    \item The backend validates the body: which listing, which version, which payment state.
    \item The backend checks the database for availability and entitlement rules.
    \item The backend creates or rejects the purchase action.
    \item The backend returns JSON describing success, failure, or pending state.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{notebox}
Most backend bugs come from misunderstanding one of these stages. A route may exist, but validation may be missing. Validation may pass, but authorization may be wrong. The database write may succeed, but the response may lie. Thinking in stages helps you debug systematically.
\end{notebox}

\section{Business Logic vs Transport Logic}

One of the biggest backend maturity jumps is learning not to mix HTTP concerns with domain decisions.

\textbf{Transport logic} includes:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item reading headers and request bodies
    \item choosing status codes
    \item serializing JSON responses
    \item parsing query parameters
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Business logic} includes:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item whether a user is allowed to buy a tune
    \item whether a tune can move from draft to approved
    \item whether a backup is valid enough to restore from
    \item whether a payment event should create an entitlement
\end{itemize}

\begin{intuitionbox}
If business rules live directly inside route handlers, they become hard to reuse, hard to test, and hard to trust. Strong backend systems separate ``how the request arrived'' from ``what the domain allows.''
\end{intuitionbox}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={A service-layer mental model}]
def purchase_tune(user, tune_version):
    if not user.is_active:
        raise PermissionError("Inactive users cannot purchase")
    if tune_version.status != "published":
        raise ValueError("Only published versions can be purchased")

    order = create_order(user=user, tune_version=tune_version)
    create_audit_log(user=user, action="PURCHASE_CREATED")
    return order
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why this is better:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item the rule can be tested without HTTP
    \item a web API, admin tool, or background worker can all reuse it
    \item the business rule becomes explicit instead of being buried in controller code
\end{itemize}

\section{REST API Design}

REST becomes much easier when you think in terms of \textbf{resources} rather than random endpoints. If your resource is \texttt{tunes}, then your backend should expose clear actions on that resource.

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{HTTP Method} & \textbf{URL} & \textbf{Action} & \textbf{Status} \\
\midrule
GET & /api/tunes/ & List all tunes & 200 \\
GET & /api/tunes/42/ & Get tune 42 & 200 \\
POST & /api/tunes/ & Create tune & 201 \\
PUT & /api/tunes/42/ & Replace tune 42 & 200 \\
PATCH & /api/tunes/42/ & Partially update & 200 \\
DELETE & /api/tunes/42/ & Delete tune 42 & 204 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\textbf{Why this matters:} a predictable API is easier to learn, test, document, and maintain. It also makes frontend and backend teams collaborate more effectively because everyone knows what shape to expect.

\begin{examtipbox}
In written exams and take-home assessments, do not just list HTTP methods. Explain \textbf{why} \texttt{PATCH} differs from \texttt{PUT}, when to return \texttt{201 Created}, and why \texttt{DELETE} often returns \texttt{204 No Content}.
\end{examtipbox}

\section{API Design and Communication}

An API is not just a list of endpoints. It is a \textbf{communication contract} between systems. Good API design reduces confusion, simplifies debugging, and lowers the chance that frontend and backend teams build incompatible assumptions.

\subsection{HTTP Status Codes That Matter Most}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Code} & \textbf{Name} & \textbf{Typical Meaning} \\
\midrule
200 & OK & Successful read or update \\
201 & Created & New resource successfully created \\
204 & No Content & Successful operation with no body returned \\
400 & Bad Request & Invalid request format or missing fields \\
401 & Unauthorized & Not authenticated \\
403 & Forbidden & Authenticated, but not allowed \\
404 & Not Found & Resource does not exist \\
409 & Conflict & State conflict (duplicate, version clash) \\
422 & Unprocessable Entity & Validation failed on well-formed input \\
429 & Too Many Requests & Rate limit triggered \\
500 & Internal Server Error & Unexpected backend failure \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{intuitionbox}
Status codes are part of the teaching language of an API. A frontend should be able to understand whether it should retry, show a form error, ask the user to sign in again, or report a server failure based largely on the status code and response body.
\end{intuitionbox}

\subsection{Validation and Error Shape}

Good APIs validate both structure and meaning:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Structure validation:} required fields, types, formats
    \item \textbf{Business validation:} permissions, state transitions, domain rules
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Consistent API error response example}]
{
  "code": "VALIDATION_ERROR",
  "message": "One or more fields are invalid.",
  "errors": {
    "email": ["This field must be a valid email address."],
    "password": ["Password must be at least 12 characters."]
  }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why this matters:} frontends become much simpler when errors are predictable. Inconsistent error formats lead to fragile UI logic and confusing user feedback.

\subsection{Pagination, Filtering, and Sorting}

List endpoints should not assume the client wants every record at once.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Readable list API patterns}]
GET /api/tunes?page=2&page_size=20
GET /api/tunes?status=published
GET /api/tunes?vehicle_make=Yamaha&vehicle_model=MT-07
GET /api/tunes?ordering=-created_at
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why this matters in real systems:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item pagination prevents huge slow responses
    \item filtering reduces wasted data transfer
    \item sorting allows predictable UX and reporting
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Idempotency and Safe Retries}

\textbf{Idempotent} means a request can be repeated without changing the final result beyond the first successful application.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{GET} should be idempotent
    \item \textbf{DELETE} is usually idempotent
    \item \textbf{POST} often is not, unless you introduce idempotency keys
\end{itemize}

\begin{notebox}
Payment and order systems often use idempotency keys so a client can retry safely without accidentally charging the user twice.
\end{notebox}

\subsection{Versioning, Webhooks, and WebSockets}

\textbf{Versioning:} APIs change over time. If you break contracts carelessly, clients fail. Common pattern: \texttt{/api/v1/...}

\textbf{Webhooks:} the server pushes an event to another system when something happens. Example: Stripe calling your backend after payment completion.

\textbf{WebSockets:} persistent bidirectional connection for real-time systems such as chat, dashboards, or live device telemetry.

\begin{projectbox}
RevSync uses several API communication patterns at once: classic REST for core CRUD, signed download endpoints for secure packages, webhook-style payment confirmation, and live progress patterns that could eventually use WebSockets for flash status.
\end{projectbox}

\section{Authentication and Authorisation}

These are often confused, so it is worth repeating them clearly:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Authentication (AuthN):} ``Who are you?'' Example: validating an email/password or token.
    \item \textbf{Authorisation (AuthZ):} ``What are you allowed to do?'' Example: a tuner can upload tunes, but a normal rider cannot.
\end{itemize}

\begin{warningbox}
Many real security bugs happen because developers implement authentication but forget authorization. A user may be logged in correctly, but still should not be allowed to update another user's data.
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Authorisation by State, Not Just by Role}

Real systems often need more than role checks. They also need \textbf{state-based rules}.

\textbf{Examples:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item a tuner may edit a tune only while it is still a draft
    \item an admin may approve a package only after validation passes
    \item a rider may download only after entitlement exists
    \item a support agent may view logs but not edit payment status
\end{itemize}

\begin{examtipbox}
If asked about authorization in interviews, do not answer only with roles. Mention ownership, resource state, and domain-specific rules. That sounds much closer to real production systems.
\end{examtipbox}

\subsection{JWT (JSON Web Tokens)}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={JWT flow}]
# User logs in with email/password
# Server validates credentials
# Server creates JWT with user data and signs it
# Server returns: { "access": "eyJ...", "refresh": "eyJ..." }
# Client stores token and sends with every request:
#   Authorization: Bearer eyJ...
# Server validates the token signature on each request

# JWT structure: HEADER.PAYLOAD.SIGNATURE
# Header:  {"alg": "HS256", "typ": "JWT"}
# Payload: {"user_id": 42, "exp": 1709337600}
# Signature: HMAC-SHA256(header + payload, secret_key)
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{What this code means in practice:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item The client does not prove identity on every request with a password.
    \item Instead, it sends a signed token.
    \item The server verifies the token signature and expiration.
    \item If valid, the server trusts the identity claim inside it.
\end{itemize}

\section{Serialization and Validation}

\textbf{Serialization} means converting backend objects into a transport format such as JSON.

\textbf{Deserialization} means taking incoming JSON and turning it into validated backend data.

\textbf{Validation} exists to stop bad data from entering the system. Typical rules include:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item required fields must be present
    \item data types must be correct
    \item values must be in allowed ranges
    \item relationships must be valid
    \item uniqueness and business rules must hold
\end{itemize}

\begin{intuitionbox}
Validation is not just about ``nice error messages.'' It protects your system boundary. If invalid data enters once, every later layer becomes harder to trust and harder to debug.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{Architecture Patterns}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Monolith:} One codebase and one deployable unit. Easier to start, easier to reason about, often the correct choice for young products. Your RevSync backend fits this pattern well.
    \item \textbf{Microservices:} Multiple independent services. Useful when teams, scaling needs, or domain boundaries justify the extra operational complexity.
    \item \textbf{Layered architecture:} Separate concerns into layers so that request handling, business rules, and persistence logic do not all blur together.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Common layered split:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Presentation layer:} HTTP handling, request parsing, serialization, permissions.
    \item \textbf{Business logic layer:} Core rules, workflows, state transitions, domain decisions.
    \item \textbf{Data access layer:} Database queries, repositories, ORM usage, caching.
\end{itemize}

\section{Observability, Logging, and Operational Honesty}

A backend is not production-ready just because it returns the right JSON during happy-path demos. You also need visibility into what happened when things go wrong.

\textbf{Useful operational signals include:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item request logs
    \item structured error logs
    \item audit logs for sensitive actions
    \item metrics such as latency, error rate, and queue depth
    \item tracing for multi-step workflows
\end{itemize}

\begin{projectbox}
In a system like RevSync, actions such as purchase creation, package approval, backup registration, flash start, and recovery attempts should leave an audit trail. That is not just ``nice logging''; it is part of product trust and supportability.
\end{projectbox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item A backend is a request-processing pipeline, not just a collection of endpoints.
    \item REST gives APIs predictability through resources, URLs, and HTTP methods.
    \item Authentication proves identity; authorization enforces permissions.
    \item Serialization shapes outbound data; validation protects inbound data.
    \item Clean architecture reduces debugging pain because each layer has a clearer responsibility.
    \item Strong backends separate transport logic from domain logic and remain observable under failure, not just under success.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What are the main stages of a backend request lifecycle?
    \item Why is authorization a different concern from authentication?
    \item When would you choose \texttt{PATCH} over \texttt{PUT}?
    \item Why is validation a system-boundary concern rather than a UI-only concern?
    \item What is the practical advantage of layered architecture?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Practice task:} Design a simple backend for a notes app. Write down the routes, expected status codes, the authentication rule, the authorization rule, and the validation rules for creating a note.
\end{exercisebox}

\checkpoint{You now understand backend fundamentals: request flow, REST, CRUD, routing, middleware, auth, JWT, serialization, validation, and core architecture patterns.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 17 — DJANGO + DJANGO REST FRAMEWORK
% ============================================================
\chapter{Django + Django REST Framework}

Django is a high-level Python web framework. DRF (Django REST Framework) extends it for building REST APIs. Your RevSync backend is built entirely with Django + DRF.

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} learn Django as a full product framework, not just a model-view tutorial. Django becomes truly useful when you understand how models, views, templates, forms, admin, authentication, permissions, serializers, and tests work together as one system.
\end{studybox}

\begin{intuitionbox}
Django is often described as ``batteries included.'' That means it gives you strong defaults for the repetitive infrastructure most applications need: routing, ORM, forms, admin, auth, templating, settings, and project structure.
\end{intuitionbox}

The lifecycle diagram below shows how Django and DRF connect those pieces. It helps because many learners understand each file separately but not how a request travels through the whole stack.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=1.5cm]
    \node[databox] (client) {Browser / Mobile\\client};
    \node[flowbox, right=of client] (urls) {URL router};
    \node[flowbox, right=of urls] (middleware) {Middleware};
    \node[flowbox, right=of middleware] (view) {View / ViewSet};
    \node[storagebox, above=of view] (serializer) {Form / Serializer};
    \node[storagebox, below=of view] (model) {Model / ORM\\/ database};
    \node[databox, right=of view] (response) {HTML or JSON\\response};

    \draw[flowarrow] (client) -- (urls);
    \draw[flowarrow] (urls) -- (middleware);
    \draw[flowarrow] (middleware) -- (view);
    \draw[softarrow] (view) -- (serializer);
    \draw[softarrow] (serializer) -- (view);
    \draw[softarrow] (view) -- (model);
    \draw[softarrow] (model) -- (view);
    \draw[flowarrow] (view) -- (response);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Simplified Django / DRF request lifecycle}
\label{fig:django-request-lifecycle}
\end{figure}

Read this as a system, not as isolated files. The router decides where the request goes, middleware applies shared policies, the view orchestrates the workflow, serializers or forms validate shape, and models handle stored data.

\section{Django Project Structure}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={Django project setup}]
# Create project
pip install django djangorestframework
django-admin startproject revsync_backend .
python manage.py startapp tunes
python manage.py startapp users

# Project structure
revsync_backend/
    settings.py    # Configuration
    urls.py        # Root URL routing
    wsgi.py        # WSGI entry point
tunes/
    models.py      # Database models
    views.py       # Request handlers
    serializers.py # DRF serializers
    urls.py        # App URL routing
    admin.py       # Admin panel config
    tests.py       # Tests
users/
    models.py
    views.py
    serializers.py
    urls.py
manage.py          # CLI tool
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why this structure matters:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item the project package holds global configuration
    \item each app contains one domain area of your system
    \item models define your data
    \item views or viewsets handle requests
    \item serializers and forms validate and shape data
    \item tests protect the behaviour you care about
\end{itemize}

\section{Models}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Django models}]
from django.db import models
from django.contrib.auth.models import AbstractUser

class User(AbstractUser):
    bio = models.TextField(blank=True)
    avatar = models.ImageField(upload_to="avatars/", null=True)

    class Meta:
        ordering = ["-date_joined"]

class TuneFile(models.Model):
    STATUS_CHOICES = [
        ("draft", "Draft"),
        ("pending", "Pending Review"),
        ("approved", "Approved"),
        ("rejected", "Rejected"),
    ]

    name = models.CharField(max_length=200)
    description = models.TextField(blank=True)
    author = models.ForeignKey(
        User, on_delete=models.CASCADE, related_name="tunes"
    )
    engine_cc = models.IntegerField()
    bike_model = models.CharField(max_length=100)
    file = models.FileField(upload_to="tunes/")
    status = models.CharField(
        max_length=20, choices=STATUS_CHOICES, default="draft"
    )
    version = models.DecimalField(max_digits=4, decimal_places=2)
    downloads = models.IntegerField(default=0)
    created_at = models.DateTimeField(auto_now_add=True)
    updated_at = models.DateTimeField(auto_now=True)

    class Meta:
        ordering = ["-created_at"]

    def __str__(self):
        return f"{self.name} v{self.version}"
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Why Models Matter}
Models do more than define tables. They centralise domain structure:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item field types define expected data shape
    \item relationships encode how parts of the system connect
    \item model methods can express domain logic
    \item metadata controls ordering and admin behaviour
\end{itemize}

\section{Serializers (DRF)}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={DRF serializers}]
from rest_framework import serializers

class TuneSerializer(serializers.ModelSerializer):
    author_name = serializers.CharField(
        source="author.get_full_name", read_only=True
    )

    class Meta:
        model = TuneFile
        fields = [
            "id", "name", "description", "author",
            "author_name", "engine_cc", "bike_model",
            "status", "version", "downloads",
            "created_at", "updated_at",
        ]
        read_only_fields = ["author", "downloads", "status"]

    def validate_engine_cc(self, value):
        if value < 50 or value > 2000:
            raise serializers.ValidationError(
                "Engine CC must be between 50 and 2000"
            )
        return value
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Serializer responsibilities:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item convert model instances to JSON
    \item validate incoming request data
    \item control which fields are writable or read-only
    \item create a stable API contract for frontend clients
\end{itemize}

\section{Views and ViewSets}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={DRF views}]
from rest_framework import viewsets, permissions, filters
from rest_framework.decorators import action
from rest_framework.response import Response
from django_filters.rest_framework import DjangoFilterBackend

class TuneViewSet(viewsets.ModelViewSet):
    queryset = TuneFile.objects.select_related("author")
    serializer_class = TuneSerializer
    permission_classes = [permissions.IsAuthenticated]
    filter_backends = [
        DjangoFilterBackend,
        filters.SearchFilter,
        filters.OrderingFilter,
    ]
    filterset_fields = ["status", "engine_cc", "bike_model"]
    search_fields = ["name", "description"]
    ordering_fields = ["created_at", "downloads"]

    def perform_create(self, serializer):
        serializer.save(author=self.request.user)

    @action(detail=True, methods=["post"])
    def approve(self, request, pk=None):
        tune = self.get_object()
        tune.status = "approved"
        tune.save()
        return Response({"status": "approved"})
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Query Efficiency and ORM Discipline}

Django's ORM is powerful, but it is easy to write inefficient code accidentally.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Avoiding N+1 queries}]
# Inefficient: each tune access may trigger another query for author
tunes = TuneFile.objects.all()

# Better: fetch related author objects in the same query
tunes = TuneFile.objects.select_related("author").all()
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Important ORM habits:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item use \texttt{select\_related} for foreign-key relationships
    \item use \texttt{prefetch\_related} for many-to-many and reverse relations
    \item avoid doing database work inside loops if it can be batched
    \item inspect query counts in tests and debugging sessions
\end{itemize}

\begin{warningbox}
Django can make database access feel effortless. That convenience is powerful, but it can also hide expensive query patterns unless you develop deliberate ORM discipline.
\end{warningbox}

\section{Templates and Forms}

Even if you mainly build APIs, you should still understand Django's server-rendered side because it teaches excellent full-stack fundamentals.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Django form example}]
from django import forms
from .models import TuneFile

class TuneUploadForm(forms.ModelForm):
    class Meta:
        model = TuneFile
        fields = ["name", "description", "engine_cc", "bike_model", "file"]

    def clean_engine_cc(self):
        value = self.cleaned_data["engine_cc"]
        if value < 50:
            raise forms.ValidationError("Engine CC is unrealistically low.")
        return value
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=HTML, caption={Template example}]
{% extends "base.html" %}

{% block content %}
  <h1>Upload Tune</h1>
  <form method="post" enctype="multipart/form-data">
    {% csrf_token %}
    {{ form.as_p }}
    <button type="submit">Upload</button>
  </form>
{% endblock %}
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why learn this if you care about APIs?}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item forms teach validation clearly
    \item templates teach server-rendered response flow
    \item many real businesses still run successful server-rendered Django apps
\end{itemize}

\section{When to Use Forms, Serializers, APIView, or ViewSets}

Django and DRF give you several layers of abstraction. The right choice depends on the problem.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{|p{0.22\textwidth}|p{0.34\textwidth}|p{0.34\textwidth}|}
\hline
\textbf{Tool} & \textbf{Best for} & \textbf{Why} \\
\hline
Forms & server-rendered input handling & built-in validation and HTML form integration \\
\hline
Serializers & API input/output contracts & JSON transformation plus validation \\
\hline
APIView & custom request logic & maximum control over request flow \\
\hline
ViewSet & standard CRUD APIs & fast, consistent REST structure \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{Choosing common Django and DRF abstractions}
\end{table}

\begin{notebox}
Do not use a ViewSet just because it exists. If the workflow is highly custom, an APIView or a service layer called from one can be clearer.
\end{notebox}

\section{Authentication, Permissions, and Admin}

\subsection{Authentication and Permissions}
Django ships with a strong authentication system and DRF adds API-oriented permission classes.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={DRF permission example}]
from rest_framework.permissions import BasePermission, SAFE_METHODS

class IsAuthorOrReadOnly(BasePermission):
    def has_object_permission(self, request, view, obj):
        if request.method in SAFE_METHODS:
            return True
        return obj.author == request.user
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Mental model:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item authentication answers who the user is
    \item permissions answer what the user may do
    \item object-level permissions answer whether this user may act on this specific record
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Django Admin}
The admin site is one of Django's most valuable productivity features. It is not your public product UI, but it is excellent for:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item internal moderation tools
    \item operational inspection
    \item support workflows
    \item quick data management while a product is still maturing
\end{itemize}

\section{Migrations, Testing, and Deployment Basics}

\subsection{Migrations}
Migrations are version-controlled descriptions of schema changes.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={Migration workflow}]
python manage.py makemigrations
python manage.py migrate
python manage.py showmigrations
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Good migration habits:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item review generated migrations before applying them
    \item be careful with destructive changes in production
    \item keep data migrations separate and explicit when possible
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Testing}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Simple Django test example}]
from django.test import TestCase
from django.urls import reverse

class TuneTests(TestCase):
    def test_list_endpoint_returns_200(self):
        response = self.client.get(reverse("tune-list"))
        self.assertEqual(response.status_code, 200)
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{A stronger Django testing stack often includes:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item model tests for domain rules
    \item serializer tests for validation behaviour
    \item API tests for permissions and response shapes
    \item integration tests for critical workflows such as checkout or approval flows
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Deployment Basics}
At deployment time, you usually need:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item production settings via environment variables
    \item a real database such as PostgreSQL
    \item static file handling
    \item a WSGI/ASGI server such as Gunicorn or Uvicorn
    \item a reverse proxy such as Nginx
\end{itemize}

\section{URL Routing}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={DRF URL routing}]
from django.urls import path, include
from rest_framework.routers import DefaultRouter

router = DefaultRouter()
router.register(r"tunes", TuneViewSet)

urlpatterns = [
    path("api/", include(router.urls)),
    path("api/auth/", include("dj_rest_auth.urls")),
]
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Django Best Practices}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Use \textbf{custom user model} from the start (\texttt{AbstractUser}).
    \item Use \texttt{select\_related} and \texttt{prefetch\_related} to avoid N+1 queries.
    \item Write database migrations carefully; review before applying.
    \item Use environment variables for secrets (\texttt{django-environ}).
    \item Use \textbf{viewsets} and routers for standard CRUD; \textbf{APIView} for custom logic.
    \item Add \textbf{pagination} to all list endpoints.
    \item Write serializer-level \textbf{validation}.
    \item Use \texttt{permissions.py} for fine-grained access control.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Practice task:} design a Django API for a project approval workflow. Decide:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item which rules belong in the serializer
    \item which rules belong in permissions
    \item which rules belong in a service function or model method
    \item which queries need \texttt{select\_related} or \texttt{prefetch\_related}
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercisebox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Django gives you strong application structure from the start.
    \item Models, forms, serializers, and permissions are all ways of expressing system rules.
    \item DRF adds consistent API patterns on top of Django's existing strengths.
    \item The admin site, migrations, and test client make Django powerful for serious product development.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What is the role of a serializer in DRF?
    \item Why are migrations essential in team-based backend work?
    \item What does object-level permission mean?
    \item Why is Django admin so useful during product development?
    \item When would you use forms/templates even in a backend-focused handbook?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Practice task:} sketch a Django app for a support ticket system. Define the models, the API endpoints, the admin needs, the serializer validation rules, and one permission class.
\end{exercisebox}

\checkpoint{You now understand Django + DRF as a full application framework: project structure, models, forms, templates, serializers, viewsets, permissions, admin, migrations, testing, and deployment basics.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 18 — FLASK
% ============================================================
\chapter{Flask}

Flask is a lightweight Python web framework. Unlike Django (``batteries-included''), Flask is minimal and lets you choose your own tools. It is ideal for small APIs, microservices, and prototypes. Your Live Chat Bot project uses Flask.

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Read Flask as a lesson in backend minimalism.} Django teaches you what a full framework gives you. Flask teaches you what you must assemble deliberately when the framework does less for you.
\end{studybox}

\begin{intuitionbox}
Flask feels simple because it starts with almost nothing: one app object, a few routes, and explicit choices. That is powerful for learning because it exposes the moving parts instead of hiding them.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{Core Flask Application}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Flask application}]
from flask import Flask, request, jsonify
from functools import wraps

app = Flask(__name__)

# Simple route
@app.route("/")
def index():
    return jsonify({"message": "Welcome to the API"})

# Route with methods and parameters
@app.route("/api/users/<int:user_id>", methods=["GET"])
def get_user(user_id):
    user = find_user(user_id)
    if not user:
        return jsonify({"error": "Not found"}), 404
    return jsonify(user)

# POST with JSON body
@app.route("/api/users", methods=["POST"])
def create_user():
    data = request.get_json()
    if not data or "name" not in data:
        return jsonify({"error": "Name required"}), 400
    user = save_user(data)
    return jsonify(user), 201

# Auth decorator
def require_auth(f):
    @wraps(f)
    def decorated(*args, **kwargs):
        token = request.headers.get("Authorization")
        if not token:
            return jsonify({"error": "Unauthorized"}), 401
        # validate token...
        return f(*args, **kwargs)
    return decorated

@app.route("/api/protected")
@require_auth
def protected():
    return jsonify({"data": "secret"})

# Error handler
@app.errorhandler(404)
def not_found(error):
    return jsonify({"error": "Not found"}), 404

if __name__ == "__main__":
    app.run(debug=True, port=5000)
\end{lstlisting}

This example is short, but it contains the essential Flask mental model:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item the \texttt{app} object is the central application
    \item decorators such as \texttt{@app.route(...)} register URL handlers
    \item \texttt{request} gives access to inbound data
    \item \texttt{jsonify(...)} returns structured JSON responses
    \item decorators such as \texttt{@require\_auth} let you reuse logic cleanly
\end{itemize}

\section{When Flask Is a Good Choice}

Flask is a strong option when:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item you are building a small service with a narrow purpose
    \item you want full control over library choices
    \item you are prototyping quickly
    \item you want to understand backend concepts without a heavy framework abstraction layer
\end{itemize}

Flask is a weaker fit when:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item you need a built-in admin panel
    \item you want ORM, auth, forms, and project conventions provided out of the box
    \item your team needs stronger structural defaults to avoid inconsistency
\end{itemize}

\section{Flask Request Lifecycle}

Even in a minimal Flask app, every request still follows the same pattern:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item the client sends a request
    \item Flask matches the URL and method to a route
    \item decorators and middleware-like hooks run
    \item your function reads inputs and executes business logic
    \item a response object is returned
\end{enumerate}

\begin{notebox}
Flask makes the lifecycle visible. This is one reason it is excellent for teaching. You can see exactly where a request enters, where validation happens, where authentication runs, and where the response leaves.
\end{notebox}

\section{Blueprints and the App Factory Pattern}

As Flask projects grow, you need structure. Two core patterns help:

\textbf{Blueprints} group related routes and logic.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Flask Blueprint example}]
from flask import Blueprint, jsonify

tunes_bp = Blueprint("tunes", __name__, url_prefix="/api/tunes")

@tunes_bp.get("/")
def list_tunes():
    return jsonify({"items": []})
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{App factory pattern} creates the Flask app in a function instead of at import time.
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Flask app factory example}]
from flask import Flask

def create_app():
    app = Flask(__name__)
    app.config.from_object("config.Settings")

    from .routes import tunes_bp
    app.register_blueprint(tunes_bp)
    return app
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why these patterns matter:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item they make testing easier
    \item they stop all routes from living in one file
    \item they allow different app configurations for development, testing, and production
\end{itemize}

\section{A Practical Flask Project Structure}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={A maintainable Flask layout}]
my_flask_app/
  app/
    __init__.py
    routes/
      tunes.py
      auth.py
    services/
      tune_service.py
    models.py
    extensions.py
    config.py
  tests/
    test_auth.py
    test_tunes.py
  requirements.txt
  run.py
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why this helps:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item routes stay focused on HTTP concerns
    \item services can hold reusable business logic
    \item extensions are initialized in one place
    \item tests mirror the project structure more clearly
\end{itemize}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=1.35cm]
    \node[flowbox] (entry) {run.py /\\create\_app()};
    \node[storagebox, below left=1.2cm and 1.7cm of entry] (routes) {routes};
    \node[storagebox, below=1.2cm of entry] (services) {services};
    \node[storagebox, below right=1.2cm and 1.7cm of entry] (models) {models};
    \node[databox, below=1.5cm of services] (tests) {tests};

    \draw[flowarrow] (entry) -- (routes);
    \draw[flowarrow] (entry) -- (services);
    \draw[flowarrow] (entry) -- (models);
    \draw[softarrow] (routes) -- (services);
    \draw[softarrow] (services) -- (models);
    \draw[softarrow] (tests) -- (routes);
    \draw[softarrow] (tests) -- (services);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{A maintainable Flask application structure}
\label{fig:flask-structure}
\end{figure}

This figure is useful because Flask can feel too unstructured at first. A clean separation between routes, services, models, and tests helps small apps avoid growing into one-file tangles.

\section{SQLAlchemy, Testing, and Deployment}

Flask usually relies on extensions for common needs:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{SQLAlchemy} for ORM-style database access
    \item \textbf{Flask-Login} or token libraries for authentication
    \item \textbf{pytest} for testing
    \item \textbf{Gunicorn} or another production server for deployment
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Testing Flask with pytest}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Simple Flask pytest example}]
import pytest
from app import create_app

@pytest.fixture
def client():
    app = create_app()
    app.config["TESTING"] = True
    with app.test_client() as client:
        yield client

def test_index_returns_200(client):
    response = client.get("/")
    assert response.status_code == 200
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why this matters:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Flask stays small, so testing discipline matters even more
    \item app factories make test configuration easier
    \item focused tests stop small APIs from decaying into guesswork
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Choosing Flask Extensions Carefully}

Flask gives freedom, but too much ad-hoc freedom creates inconsistency.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item choose only the extensions you actually need
    \item document those choices in the README or architecture notes
    \item keep validation, auth, and database patterns consistent across the app
\end{itemize}

\begin{examtipbox}
If asked to compare Flask and Django, do not stop at ``Flask is lightweight.'' Explain the consequence: Flask gives more flexibility, but you must make more architectural decisions yourself.
\end{examtipbox}

\begin{projectbox}
Your \textbf{Live Chat Bot} uses Flask with Socket.IO for real-time WebSocket communication. Flask's simplicity makes it ideal for this kind of focused, single-purpose service.
\end{projectbox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Flask is minimal by design, which makes it flexible and easy to reason about.
    \item Routes, request objects, JSON responses, decorators, and error handlers form the core of most Flask APIs.
    \item Flask is often best for focused services, prototypes, and small-to-medium APIs where explicitness is helpful.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Why is Flask often described as ``minimal''?
    \item What does \texttt{@app.route(...)} actually do?
    \item Why are decorators useful for authentication?
    \item When would Django be a better choice than Flask?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand Flask: routing, request handling, JSON APIs, decorators for auth, error handling, and when Flask is the right tool.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 19 — NODE.JS + EXPRESS
% ============================================================
\chapter{Node.js + Express}

Node.js lets you run JavaScript on the server. Express is the most popular Node.js web framework for building APIs.

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} understand why Express feels different from Django and Flask. It is less opinionated, heavily middleware-driven, and closely tied to JavaScript's asynchronous execution model.
\end{studybox}

\section{Core Express Application}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Express application}]
const express = require("express");
const app = express();

// Middleware
app.use(express.json()); // parse JSON bodies
app.use((req, res, next) => {
    console.log(`${req.method} ${req.url}`);
    next(); // pass to next middleware/route
});

// Routes
app.get("/api/tunes", async (req, res) => {
    try {
        const tunes = await Tune.findAll();
        res.json(tunes);
    } catch (err) {
        res.status(500).json({ error: err.message });
    }
});

app.get("/api/tunes/:id", async (req, res) => {
    const tune = await Tune.findById(req.params.id);
    if (!tune) return res.status(404).json({ error: "Not found" });
    res.json(tune);
});

app.post("/api/tunes", async (req, res) => {
    const { name, engineCC } = req.body;
    if (!name) return res.status(400).json({ error: "Name required" });
    const tune = await Tune.create({ name, engineCC });
    res.status(201).json(tune);
});

// Auth middleware
const authMiddleware = (req, res, next) => {
    const token = req.headers.authorization?.split(" ")[1];
    if (!token) return res.status(401).json({ error: "No token" });
    try {
        const decoded = jwt.verify(token, process.env.JWT_SECRET);
        req.user = decoded;
        next();
    } catch {
        res.status(401).json({ error: "Invalid token" });
    }
};

app.get("/api/profile", authMiddleware, (req, res) => {
    res.json({ user: req.user });
});

// Error handling middleware (must have 4 params)
app.use((err, req, res, next) => {
    console.error(err.stack);
    res.status(500).json({ error: "Internal server error" });
});

app.listen(3000, () => console.log("Server running on port 3000"));
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{How to read this example:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \texttt{express()} creates the application object
    \item \texttt{app.use(...)} registers middleware that runs for many requests
    \item \texttt{app.get(...)} and \texttt{app.post(...)} define routes
    \item route handlers receive \texttt{req} and \texttt{res}
    \item the final error middleware centralizes failure handling
\end{itemize}

\section{Why Middleware Matters So Much in Express}

Express becomes much easier once you see middleware as a chain of responsibility:

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item parse the body
    \item log the request
    \item check authentication
    \item validate input
    \item run the main controller
    \item handle errors consistently
\end{enumerate}

\begin{intuitionbox}
