In Express, middleware is not a side feature. It is one of the main architectural tools. Good Express code feels like a clean pipeline of small steps rather than one huge route handler doing everything.
\end{intuitionbox}

That pipeline idea is easier to remember visually:

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=1.2cm]
    \node[databox] (req) {Incoming\\request};
    \node[flowbox, right=of req] (parse) {Parse body};
    \node[flowbox, right=of parse] (log) {Logger};
    \node[flowbox, right=of log] (auth) {Auth};
    \node[flowbox, right=of auth] (validate) {Validation};
    \node[flowbox, right=of validate] (controller) {Controller\\/ service};
    \node[databox, right=of controller] (res) {Response};
    \node[warnbox, below=1.5cm of controller] (error) {Global error\\handler};

    \draw[flowarrow] (req) -- (parse);
    \draw[flowarrow] (parse) -- (log);
    \draw[flowarrow] (log) -- (auth);
    \draw[flowarrow] (auth) -- (validate);
    \draw[flowarrow] (validate) -- (controller);
    \draw[flowarrow] (controller) -- (res);
    \draw[softarrow] (parse) -- (error);
    \draw[softarrow] (log) -- (error);
    \draw[softarrow] (auth) -- (error);
    \draw[softarrow] (validate) -- (error);
    \draw[softarrow] (controller) -- (error);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Express as a middleware pipeline}
\label{fig:express-middleware-flow}
\end{figure}

This matters because Express stays maintainable only when responsibilities are split cleanly. If all of these steps are collapsed into one handler, the codebase becomes hard to test and reason about.

\section{Common Express Mistakes}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Putting too much logic directly inside routes instead of separating controllers and services
    \item Forgetting to validate request bodies
    \item Returning inconsistent error shapes across endpoints
    \item Mixing authentication and authorization checks into random places
    \item Failing to call \texttt{next(err)} or centralize error handling
\end{itemize}

\section{Django vs Flask vs Express}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Feature} & \textbf{Django} & \textbf{Flask} & \textbf{Express} \\
\midrule
Language & Python & Python & JavaScript \\
Philosophy & Batteries-included & Minimal & Minimal \\
ORM & Built-in & Choose your own & Choose your own \\
Admin panel & Built-in & No & No \\
Best for & Full apps, APIs & Microservices & APIs, real-time \\
Learning curve & Medium & Low & Low \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Node.js lets backend engineers use JavaScript outside the browser.
    \item Express is simple because it provides a thin layer over HTTP rather than a full application framework.
    \item Middleware is the main structuring tool in Express apps.
    \item Express is strong for APIs, JavaScript-heavy teams, and systems that benefit from the Node ecosystem.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What role does middleware play in Express?
    \item Why is Express described as minimal?
    \item What belongs in middleware versus the route handler itself?
    \item How is Express structurally different from Django?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}



\section{Middleware Deep Dive}

\textbf{Middleware} functions are the backbone of Express. They have access to the request object (\texttt{req}), response object (\texttt{res}), and the next function (\texttt{next}). They execute in the order they are registered.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Express middleware patterns}]
// Logging middleware
const logger = (req, res, next) => {
    const start = Date.now();
    res.on("finish", () => {
        const duration = Date.now() - start;
        console.log(
            `${req.method} ${req.url} ${res.statusCode} ${duration}ms`
        );
    });
    next();
};
app.use(logger);

// Request validation middleware (with Zod)
const { z } = require("zod");

const validate = (schema) => (req, res, next) => {
    try {
        schema.parse(req.body);
        next();
    } catch (err) {
        res.status(400).json({
            error: "Validation failed",
            details: err.errors,
        });
    }
};

// Define schemas
const createTuneSchema = z.object({
    name: z.string().min(1).max(100),
    engineCC: z.number().int().min(50).max(2000),
    price: z.number().positive(),
    bikeModel: z.string().min(1),
});

// Use validation middleware
app.post("/api/tunes",
    authMiddleware,
    validate(createTuneSchema),
    async (req, res) => {
        // req.body is guaranteed to be valid here
        const tune = await Tune.create(req.body);
        res.status(201).json(tune);
    }
);
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Project Structure for Express}

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={Professional Express project structure}]
my_express_app/
  src/
    index.js              # entry point
    app.js                # Express app setup
    config/
      database.js         # DB connection
      env.js              # environment validation
    middleware/
      auth.js             # JWT auth
      validate.js         # request validation
      errorHandler.js     # global error handler
    routes/
      tuneRoutes.js       # /api/tunes
      userRoutes.js       # /api/users
    controllers/
      tuneController.js   # business logic
      userController.js
    models/
      Tune.js             # database model
      User.js
    services/             # complex business logic
      tuneService.js
    utils/
      helpers.js
  tests/
    tunes.test.js
    users.test.js
  package.json
  .env
  .env.example
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Error Handling Best Practices}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Robust Express error handling}]
// Custom error class
class AppError extends Error {
    constructor(message, statusCode) {
        super(message);
        this.statusCode = statusCode;
        this.isOperational = true;
    }
}

// Controller using custom errors
app.get("/api/tunes/:id", async (req, res, next) => {
    try {
        const tune = await Tune.findById(req.params.id);
        if (!tune) {
            throw new AppError("Tune not found", 404);
        }
        res.json(tune);
    } catch (err) {
        next(err);  // pass to error handler
    }
});

// Global error handler (MUST have 4 params)
app.use((err, req, res, next) => {
    console.error(err.stack);

    if (err.isOperational) {
        return res.status(err.statusCode).json({
            error: err.message,
        });
    }

    // Unexpected error -- don't leak details
    res.status(500).json({
        error: "Internal server error",
    });
});
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Chapter Review: Express}

\subsection{Key Takeaways}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Express uses \textbf{middleware} for everything: logging, auth, validation, error handling.
    \item Use \textbf{Zod} for request validation (type-safe, composable schemas).
    \item Custom \textbf{error classes} + a global error handler keeps error handling consistent.
    \item Follow MVC-style project structure: routes $\to$ controllers $\to$ models.
    \item Express is ideal for APIs and real-time apps (with Socket.IO or WebSockets).
\end{itemize}



% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 20 — DATABASES + SQL DESIGN
% ============================================================
\chapter{Databases + SQL Design}

This chapter focuses on practical database \textbf{design}---how to structure schemas for real products.

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} move beyond writing isolated SQL queries and learn how databases are designed as long-lived system components. Good schema design affects correctness, performance, developer productivity, and even product features.
\end{studybox}

\begin{notebox}
\textbf{How to study this chapter:} do not read it as a list of SQL features. Read it as a chapter about promises and trade-offs. A schema is promising what data exists, what relationships are valid, what values are allowed, and what operations will stay fast enough as the product grows.
\end{notebox}

\begin{intuitionbox}
A database schema is a set of promises about your system. It says what entities exist, how they relate, what values are allowed, and what the application can trust to be true.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{A Beginner-Friendly Way to Think About Schemas}

Many learners first imagine a database as ``a big spreadsheet.'' That is a useful starting point, but it becomes limiting quickly.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item A spreadsheet is mainly for human editing and visual inspection.
    \item A database is built for correctness, relationships, concurrency, and querying at scale.
    \item A table is not just a grid. It represents one kind of entity in your system.
    \item A row is not just a line of values. It represents one real record.
    \item A foreign key is not just another column. It is a formal relationship between records.
\end{itemize}

\begin{intuitionbox}
When designing a schema, think less about screens and more about the real-world things your product must remember correctly over time.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{Schema Design Process}

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Identify entities:} What ``things'' does your app manage? (Users, Tunes, Bikes, Orders)
    \item \textbf{Identify attributes:} What data does each entity have?
    \item \textbf{Identify relationships:} How do entities relate? (One user has many tunes)
    \item \textbf{Define primary keys:} Each table needs a unique identifier.
    \item \textbf{Define foreign keys:} Link related tables.
    \item \textbf{Normalise:} Remove redundancy (aim for 3NF).
    \item \textbf{Add indexes:} On columns used in WHERE, JOIN, and ORDER BY.
    \item \textbf{Add constraints:} NOT NULL, UNIQUE, CHECK, DEFAULT.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{examtipbox}
When asked to design a schema, start with entities and relationships before talking about column types. That produces a more mature answer than jumping straight into SQL syntax.
\end{examtipbox}

\section{Primary Keys, Foreign Keys, and Constraints}

\textbf{Primary key:} uniquely identifies each row.

\textbf{Foreign key:} points from one table to a row in another table.

\textbf{Constraints:} rules enforced by the database itself.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{NOT NULL} prevents missing required data
    \item \textbf{UNIQUE} prevents duplicate values where uniqueness matters
    \item \textbf{CHECK} restricts allowed ranges or categories
    \item \textbf{DEFAULT} provides a fallback value
\end{itemize}

\begin{warningbox}
Do not push every rule into application code. If a rule is fundamental to data integrity, the database should often enforce it too. Application validation is useful; database constraints are authoritative.
\end{warningbox}

\section{Normalisation and Denormalisation}

\subsection{Normalisation}
Normalisation means structuring tables to reduce duplication and update anomalies.

\textbf{Why it matters:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item repeated data becomes harder to keep consistent
    \item updates in one place may not propagate elsewhere
    \item duplicated facts produce subtle bugs in reporting and analytics
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Denormalisation}
Denormalisation means intentionally duplicating some data to improve performance or simplify certain read patterns.

\textbf{Trade-off:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item reads may become faster
    \item writes become more complex because duplicated data must stay in sync
\end{itemize}

\begin{examtipbox}
In interviews and exams, normalisation is not automatically the ``best'' answer. The better answer is: start from clean structure, then denormalise deliberately when workload patterns justify it.
\end{examtipbox}

\section{Example: RevSync Database Schema}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=SQL, caption={RevSync schema design}]
CREATE TABLE users (
    id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    email VARCHAR(255) UNIQUE NOT NULL,
    password_hash VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL,
    full_name VARCHAR(200) NOT NULL,
    role VARCHAR(20) DEFAULT 'rider'
        CHECK (role IN ('rider', 'tuner', 'admin')),
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT NOW()
);

CREATE TABLE bikes (
    id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    owner_id INTEGER REFERENCES users(id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
    make VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    model VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    year INTEGER,
    engine_cc INTEGER NOT NULL
);

CREATE TABLE tune_files (
    id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    author_id INTEGER REFERENCES users(id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
    target_bike_model VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    target_engine_cc INTEGER NOT NULL,
    name VARCHAR(200) NOT NULL,
    description TEXT,
    file_path VARCHAR(500) NOT NULL,
    status VARCHAR(20) DEFAULT 'draft',
    version DECIMAL(4,2) DEFAULT 1.0,
    downloads INTEGER DEFAULT 0,
    price DECIMAL(10,2) DEFAULT 0.00,
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT NOW(),
    updated_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT NOW()
);

CREATE TABLE flash_logs (
    id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    user_id INTEGER REFERENCES users(id),
    tune_id INTEGER REFERENCES tune_files(id),
    bike_id INTEGER REFERENCES bikes(id),
    status VARCHAR(20) NOT NULL,
    started_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT NOW(),
    completed_at TIMESTAMP
);

-- Indexes for common queries
CREATE INDEX idx_tunes_author ON tune_files(author_id);
CREATE INDEX idx_tunes_status ON tune_files(status);
CREATE INDEX idx_flash_user ON flash_logs(user_id);
\end{lstlisting}

\section{How to Read a Schema Without Getting Lost}

When you look at a schema, scan it in this order:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item identify the main business entities
    \item identify which table owns which relationship
    \item identify the keys and constraints that protect integrity
    \item identify the fields likely used in search, filtering, sorting, or joins
    \item identify which workflows will read and write these tables together
\end{enumerate}

\begin{notebox}
This reading order reduces overload. Instead of seeing one giant wall of SQL, you start seeing product concepts, safety rules, and workload patterns.
\end{notebox}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=2cm]
    \node[storagebox] (users) {users};
    \node[storagebox, below left=of users] (bikes) {bikes};
    \node[storagebox, below right=of users] (tunes) {tune\_files};
    \node[storagebox, below=2cm of users] (logs) {flash\_logs};

    \draw[flowarrow] (users) -- node[left, font=\small]{owns} (bikes);
    \draw[flowarrow] (users) -- node[right, font=\small]{authors} (tunes);
    \draw[flowarrow] (users) -- node[right, font=\small]{initiates} (logs);
    \draw[flowarrow] (bikes) -- node[left, font=\small]{target bike} (logs);
    \draw[flowarrow] (tunes) -- node[right, font=\small]{flashed tune} (logs);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Relationship map for the RevSync schema}
\label{fig:revsync-schema-relationships}
\end{figure}

What you are looking at here is not just database wiring. It is the domain model of the product: users own bikes, tuners author tune files, and flash logs connect a user, a bike, and a tune into one auditable event.

\subsection{Join Intuition}

Joins are easier when you stop seeing them as abstract SQL vocabulary and start seeing them as relationship assembly.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=1.7cm]
    \node[storagebox] (left) {users\\id, email};
    \node[storagebox, right=of left] (right) {orders\\user\_id, total};
    \node[flowbox, right=of right] (join) {INNER JOIN\\on users.id = orders.user\_id};
    \node[databox, right=of join] (result) {joined rows\\user + order};

    \draw[flowarrow] (left) -- (join);
    \draw[flowarrow] (right) -- (join);
    \draw[flowarrow] (join) -- (result);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{A simple mental model for an SQL join}
\label{fig:sql-join-intuition}
\end{figure}

The important learning point is that a join is not ``merging tables randomly.'' It is combining related rows using a shared key so the query can answer a business question.

\section{Indexes and Query Performance}

Indexes speed up reads, but they are not free.
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item they take additional storage
    \item inserts and updates become more expensive
    \item indexing the wrong columns may help little while increasing write cost
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Good candidates for indexes:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item foreign keys
    \item columns frequently used in WHERE clauses
    \item columns used in ORDER BY for large result sets
    \item frequently joined columns
\end{itemize}

\subsection{How to Think About Query Plans}

When a query feels slow, do not guess blindly. Ask:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item how many rows is the database examining?
    \item which filters are selective?
    \item is an index being used?
    \item is the slow part the join, sort, aggregation, or scan?
\end{enumerate}

\begin{notebox}
Professional database tuning is usually less about ``write clever SQL'' and more about understanding access patterns, row counts, index selectivity, and the workload you are actually serving.
\end{notebox}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=1.7cm]
    \node[storagebox] (table) {Large table\\many rows};
    \node[flowbox, right=of table] (scan) {Full scan\\check row by row};
    \node[warnbox, below=1.2cm of scan] (index) {Index lookup\\jump near target};
    \node[databox, right=of scan] (result1) {Matching rows};
    \node[databox, right=of index] (result2) {Matching rows};

    \draw[flowarrow] (table) -- (scan);
    \draw[flowarrow] (scan) -- (result1);
    \draw[softarrow] (table) -- (index);
    \draw[softarrow] (index) -- (result2);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Index intuition: scanning everything versus jumping closer to the needed rows}
\label{fig:index-intuition}
\end{figure}

This visual matters because indexing is a workload optimization, not a magic speed button. It helps when the index matches the way queries actually search or sort the data.

\section{Transactions and ACID}

A \textbf{transaction} groups several operations so they succeed or fail together.

\textbf{ACID:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Atomicity:} all or nothing
    \item \textbf{Consistency:} valid state before and after
    \item \textbf{Isolation:} concurrent work should not corrupt each other
    \item \textbf{Durability:} committed changes persist
\end{itemize}

\begin{projectbox}
In a marketplace product, entitlement creation, order status change, and audit log creation may need one transaction. If one part fails and the others succeed, the product can enter an inconsistent state that is difficult to repair later.
\end{projectbox}

\section{Migrations and ORM Mental Models}

Most modern applications do not edit the database manually every time a schema changes. They use \textbf{migrations} to describe schema evolution in version control.

\textbf{ORMs} such as Django ORM or SQLAlchemy let you work with database rows as objects, but they do not remove the need to understand SQL.

\begin{notebox}
The best engineers use ORMs comfortably \emph{and} still think in joins, indexes, query plans, and constraints. An ORM is a productivity layer, not a replacement for database literacy.
\end{notebox}

\section{Common Database Design Confusions}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{``If the app validates it, the database does not need to.''} Wrong. App validation can be bypassed; constraints protect truth at the data layer.
    \item \textbf{``More indexes always means faster.''} Wrong. Indexes improve some reads but increase write cost and storage.
    \item \textbf{``Normalisation is always best.''} Wrong. It is the clean starting point, but some read-heavy systems benefit from careful denormalisation.
    \item \textbf{``ORM means I do not need SQL knowledge.''} Wrong. ORMs help productivity, but performance and correctness still depend on database understanding.
    \item \textbf{``A schema only exists for today’s UI.''} Wrong. A schema should serve the domain, future workflows, analytics, and integrity constraints too.
\end{itemize}

\section{Schema Review Checklist}

Before you feel confident in a schema, ask:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What are the core entities?
    \item Which relationships are one-to-many or many-to-many?
    \item Which fields must be unique?
    \item Which fields need constraints for valid ranges or enums?
    \item Which queries will happen most often?
    \item Which indexes support those queries?
    \item Which operations need transactions?
    \item Which data should never be deleted silently?
\end{enumerate}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Database review drill:} take one of your projects and review the schema with the checklist above. Then write down:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item one missing constraint
    \item one missing index
    \item one place where denormalisation might help
    \item one workflow that should be wrapped in a transaction
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercisebox}

\section{Common Database Design Mistakes}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item storing several unrelated facts in one text field
    \item using no foreign keys for related data
    \item forgetting unique constraints on business-critical values
    \item indexing everything blindly
    \item letting application code be the only guardian of data integrity
    \item designing only for today's screen instead of the broader product domain
\end{itemize}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Schema design begins with entities, attributes, relationships, and rules.
    \item Primary keys, foreign keys, and constraints define trustworthy structure.
    \item Normalisation reduces duplication; denormalisation is a deliberate performance trade-off.
    \item Indexes help reads but cost writes and storage.
    \item Transactions and migrations are essential for safe long-term product development.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What is the difference between a primary key and a foreign key?
    \item Why are constraints important even when the application validates input?
    \item When is denormalisation reasonable?
    \item Why can too many indexes be harmful?
    \item What practical problem do transactions solve?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Practice task:} design the schema for an online course platform with users, courses, lessons, enrollments, and payments. Identify primary keys, foreign keys, constraints, and the indexes you would add first.
\end{exercisebox}

\checkpoint{You now understand practical database design: schema planning, keys, constraints, normalisation, denormalisation trade-offs, indexes, transactions, migrations, and ORM mental models for real systems.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 21 — ETL + DATA PIPELINES + ANALYTICS
% ============================================================
\chapter{ETL + Data Pipelines + Analytics}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} learn to think about data as a system with ingestion, validation, transformation, storage, and reporting stages. A strong data engineer does not only write Pandas scripts; they design reliable flows that survive bad inputs, retries, and scale changes.
\end{studybox}

\section{The Data Lifecycle}

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Ingestion:} Data enters the system (API calls, file uploads, database writes, streaming events).
    \item \textbf{Storage:} Data is stored (database, data lake, file system).
    \item \textbf{Processing/Transformation:} Raw data is cleaned, validated, and transformed into usable formats.
    \item \textbf{Analysis:} Processed data is queried, aggregated, and analysed for insights.
    \item \textbf{Reporting/Visualisation:} Results are presented via dashboards, reports, or API responses.
\end{enumerate}

\section{ETL vs ELT}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{ETL (Extract, Transform, Load):} Transform data \emph{before} loading it into the destination. Traditional approach.
    \item \textbf{ELT (Extract, Load, Transform):} Load raw data first, transform it inside the destination (e.g., in a data warehouse). Modern approach.
\end{itemize}

\begin{intuitionbox}
ETL and ELT are not only technical acronyms. They express different ideas about where transformation logic should live. ETL transforms before loading into the destination; ELT loads raw data first and transforms inside the warehouse or lakehouse.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{Building a Data Pipeline}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={ETL pipeline example}]
import pandas as pd
from datetime import datetime

class TuneAnalyticsPipeline:
    """ETL pipeline for RevSync tune download analytics."""

    def extract(self, source_path: str) -> pd.DataFrame:
        """Extract: read raw data from source."""
        raw = pd.read_csv(source_path)
        print(f"Extracted {len(raw)} rows")
        return raw

    def transform(self, df: pd.DataFrame) -> pd.DataFrame:
        """Transform: clean, validate, enrich data."""
        # Remove duplicates
        df = df.drop_duplicates(subset=["download_id"])

        # Handle missing values
        df["engine_cc"] = df["engine_cc"].fillna(0).astype(int)
        df["country"] = df["country"].fillna("Unknown")

        # Parse dates
        df["downloaded_at"] = pd.to_datetime(
            df["downloaded_at"], errors="coerce"
        )
        df = df.dropna(subset=["downloaded_at"])

        # Enrich: add computed columns
        df["download_month"] = df["downloaded_at"].dt.to_period("M")
        df["is_premium"] = df["price"] > 0

        # Validate
        df = df[df["engine_cc"].between(50, 2000)]

        print(f"Transformed: {len(df)} clean rows")
        return df

    def load(self, df: pd.DataFrame, dest: str) -> None:
        """Load: write processed data to destination."""
        df.to_parquet(dest, index=False)
        print(f"Loaded {len(df)} rows to {dest}")

    def run(self, source: str, dest: str) -> None:
        raw = self.extract(source)
        clean = self.transform(raw)
        self.load(clean, dest)

pipeline = TuneAnalyticsPipeline()
pipeline.run("raw_downloads.csv", "clean_downloads.parquet")
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why this example matters:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item extraction is about reliable source access
    \item transformation is where quality and meaning are enforced
    \item loading makes the processed data available for downstream use
\end{itemize}

\section{Data Quality Checks}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Completeness:} Are required fields present and non-null?
    \item \textbf{Uniqueness:} Are there duplicate records?
    \item \textbf{Validity:} Do values fall within expected ranges?
    \item \textbf{Consistency:} Do related fields agree (e.g., end date after start date)?
    \item \textbf{Timeliness:} Is the data recent enough?
\end{itemize}

\section{Pipeline Reliability and Operations}

Real pipelines fail in real ways:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item a source file arrives late
    \item a column changes shape unexpectedly
    \item a destination write partly succeeds
    \item the same batch is re-run accidentally
    \item downstream dashboards consume stale data
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Important production concepts:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Idempotency:} rerunning the same job should not duplicate records.
    \item \textbf{Scheduling:} jobs need dependable timing and dependency order.
    \item \textbf{Observability:} you need logs, metrics, and alerts so broken pipelines are visible quickly.
    \item \textbf{Data lineage:} know where data came from and how it was transformed.
\end{itemize}

\begin{projectbox}
Imagine RevSync analytics for tune downloads, flash success rates, and device errors. If raw event data is dirty or delayed, product and operational decisions become misleading. That is why pipeline quality is a product concern, not just a data-team concern.
\end{projectbox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Data engineering covers ingestion, transformation, storage, and reporting.
    \item ETL and ELT reflect different architectural choices.
    \item Data quality checks are essential because bad data silently breaks downstream decisions.
    \item Real pipelines require scheduling, idempotency, and observability.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What is the difference between ETL and ELT?
    \item Why is idempotency important in pipelines?
    \item What is observability in a data pipeline context?
    \item Why is data quality not just an analytics issue?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand data pipelines, ETL/ELT patterns, data cleaning, validation, data quality, scheduling, idempotency, and observability for production-oriented analytics work.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 22 — PANDAS + NUMPY + MATPLOTLIB
% ============================================================
\chapter{Pandas + NumPy + Matplotlib}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} learn the core Python data stack as a workflow: NumPy for numerical arrays, Pandas for tabular manipulation, and Matplotlib for visual explanation. These tools are central to analytics, feature engineering, and many ML pipelines.
\end{studybox}

\section{NumPy}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={NumPy essentials}]
import numpy as np

# Arrays
a = np.array([1, 2, 3, 4, 5])
b = np.zeros((3, 3))
c = np.ones((2, 4))
d = np.arange(0, 10, 0.5)
e = np.linspace(0, 1, 100)
f = np.random.randn(1000)  # normal distribution

# Operations (vectorised - no loops needed)
a * 2            # [2, 4, 6, 8, 10]
a ** 2           # [1, 4, 9, 16, 25]
np.sqrt(a)       # element-wise square root
np.mean(a)       # 3.0
np.std(a)        # standard deviation
np.sum(a)        # 15

# Matrix operations
A = np.array([[1, 2], [3, 4]])
B = np.array([[5, 6], [7, 8]])
A @ B            # matrix multiplication
np.linalg.inv(A) # inverse
np.linalg.det(A) # determinant

# Boolean indexing (filtering)
data = np.random.randn(100)
positives = data[data > 0]
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Pandas}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Pandas essentials}]
import pandas as pd

# Create DataFrame
df = pd.DataFrame({
    "name": ["Amzat", "Alice", "Bob"],
    "age": [24, 28, 32],
    "city": ["London", "London", "Manchester"],
})

# Read/Write
df = pd.read_csv("data.csv")
df = pd.read_json("data.json")
df.to_csv("output.csv", index=False)
df.to_parquet("output.parquet")

# Exploration
df.head()         # first 5 rows
df.info()         # column types, non-null counts
df.describe()     # statistics for numeric columns
df.shape          # (rows, cols)
df.columns        # column names
df.dtypes         # data types
df.isnull().sum() # count nulls per column

# Selection
df["name"]            # single column (Series)
df[["name", "age"]]   # multiple columns
df.loc[0]             # row by label
df.iloc[0:3]          # rows by position
df[df["age"] > 25]    # filter rows

# Modification
df["senior"] = df["age"] > 60
df["age_group"] = pd.cut(df["age"], bins=[0,18,30,65,100])
df = df.rename(columns={"name": "full_name"})
df = df.drop(columns=["temp_col"])

# Grouping and aggregation
df.groupby("city")["age"].mean()
df.groupby("city").agg(
    avg_age=("age", "mean"),
    count=("name", "count"),
)

# Merging (like SQL JOIN)
merged = pd.merge(users_df, orders_df, on="user_id", how="left")

# Pivot tables
pivot = df.pivot_table(
    values="downloads", index="month",
    columns="status", aggfunc="sum"
)
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Matplotlib}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Matplotlib essentials}]
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# Line plot
plt.figure(figsize=(10, 6))
plt.plot(x, y, color="blue", linewidth=2, label="Downloads")
plt.xlabel("Month")
plt.ylabel("Downloads")
plt.title("RevSync Monthly Downloads")
plt.legend()
plt.grid(True, alpha=0.3)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("downloads.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()

# Bar chart
categories = ["Draft", "Pending", "Approved", "Rejected"]
counts = [15, 8, 42, 5]
plt.bar(categories, counts, color=["gray", "orange", "green", "red"])
plt.title("Tune Status Distribution")

# Scatter plot
plt.scatter(df["engine_cc"], df["downloads"], alpha=0.6)

# Histogram
plt.hist(df["age"], bins=20, edgecolor="black")

# Subplots
fig, axes = plt.subplots(2, 2, figsize=(12, 8))
axes[0, 0].plot(x, y1)
axes[0, 1].bar(cats, vals)
axes[1, 0].scatter(x, y2)
axes[1, 1].hist(data)
plt.tight_layout()
\end{lstlisting}

\section{A Real Analysis Workflow}

These libraries become far more useful when you think in workflow stages instead of isolated functions:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item load the data
    \item inspect shape, types, and missingness
    \item clean obvious issues
    \item derive useful features
    \item aggregate or visualise patterns
    \item export a cleaned result for downstream use
\end{enumerate}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Mini end-to-end analysis workflow}]
import pandas as pd
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

df = pd.read_csv("flash_history.csv")

# 1. Inspect
print(df.head())
print(df.info())
print(df.isnull().sum())

# 2. Clean
df["created_at"] = pd.to_datetime(df["created_at"], errors="coerce")
df = df.dropna(subset=["created_at", "status"])
df["status"] = df["status"].str.upper().str.strip()

# 3. Derive
df["month"] = df["created_at"].dt.to_period("M").astype(str)

# 4. Aggregate
monthly = (
    df.groupby(["month", "status"])
      .size()
      .reset_index(name="count")
)

# 5. Visualise
pivot = monthly.pivot(index="month", columns="status", values="count").fillna(0)
pivot.plot(kind="bar", stacked=True, figsize=(12, 6))
plt.title("Flash Outcomes by Month")
plt.ylabel("Count")
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Common Data Analysis Mistakes}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item trusting data types without checking them
    \item forgetting that missing values can silently distort results
    \item mutating a filtered DataFrame and triggering chained-assignment confusion
    \item plotting without first asking what decision the chart should support
    \item leaking future information into training features
    \item skipping units, labels, and titles on plots
\end{itemize}

\begin{projectbox}
Use this stack for mini portfolio projects such as:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item tune marketplace trend analysis
    \item device failure pattern exploration
    \item flash success-rate dashboard prototype
    \item pricing, entitlement, or geography-based customer analysis
\end{itemize}
\end{projectbox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item NumPy provides fast numerical arrays and vectorised operations.
    \item Pandas provides practical data-cleaning and analysis workflows.
    \item Matplotlib helps you inspect and explain data visually.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Why are vectorised operations usually better than explicit Python loops?
    \item What kinds of tasks are especially natural in Pandas?
    \item Why is data visualisation useful before model building?
    \item When would you merge two DataFrames?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand NumPy (arrays, vectorised operations, linear algebra), Pandas (DataFrames, filtering, grouping, merging), and Matplotlib (line, bar, scatter, histograms). These are the core tools for data analysis and feature engineering.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 23 — POWER BI + TABLEAU
% ============================================================
\chapter{Power BI + Tableau}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} understand dashboarding as communication, not decoration. BI tools are valuable when they turn trustworthy data into decisions. They are weak when they produce flashy charts without context, clean modelling, or audience awareness.
\end{studybox}

\section{Power BI}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Microsoft's business intelligence tool for interactive dashboards.
    \item \textbf{Data sources:} Excel, CSV, SQL databases, REST APIs, cloud services.
    \item \textbf{DAX (Data Analysis Expressions):} Formula language for calculated columns and measures. Example: \texttt{Total Sales = SUM(Orders[Amount])}.
    \item \textbf{Key features:} Drag-and-drop visualisations, slicers/filters, drill-down, scheduled refresh, sharing via Power BI Service.
    \item \textbf{Workflow:} Connect to data $\to$ Transform in Power Query $\to$ Model relationships $\to$ Build visuals $\to$ Publish and share.
\end{itemize}

\section{Tableau}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Industry-leading data visualisation platform.
    \item \textbf{Key concepts:} Dimensions (categorical) vs.\ Measures (numerical), Marks (encoding data visually), Sheets/Dashboards/Stories.
    \item \textbf{Calculated fields:} Create custom metrics. Example: \texttt{IF [Status] = "Approved" THEN 1 ELSE 0 END}.
    \item \textbf{Strengths:} Beautiful visualisations, fast exploration, LOD (Level of Detail) expressions.
\end{itemize}

\section{Choosing the Right Tool}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Question} & \textbf{Power BI} & \textbf{Tableau} \\
\midrule
Best organisational fit & Microsoft-heavy stack & Analytics-heavy culture \\
Strength & Integrated modelling + sharing & Exploratory visual analysis \\
Typical audience & Business teams, ops, finance & Analysts, data storytellers \\
Important language & DAX & Calculated fields / LOD expressions \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\section{Dashboard Design Principles}

Good dashboards:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item start from a decision question
    \item use a small number of meaningful KPIs
    \item keep filters understandable
    \item show trends and comparisons, not isolated numbers
    \item clearly label definitions and time ranges
\end{itemize}

Common mistakes:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item too many charts on one screen
    \item inconsistent colour meanings
    \item mixing different grains of data carelessly
    \item hiding metric definitions from the reader
    \item presenting stale data without warning
\end{itemize}

\begin{tipbox}
Both tools are used in business analytics roles. Power BI is more common in Microsoft-centric organisations; Tableau is favoured in data-driven companies. Learn both at a basic level.
\end{tipbox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Power BI and Tableau are both serious dashboarding tools.
    \item Tool choice depends on organisational context, workflow, and audience.
    \item Good dashboards communicate trustworthy decisions, not just pretty visuals.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What is the role of Power Query in Power BI?
    \item Why do dashboard definitions and time ranges matter?
    \item When might Tableau be preferred over Power BI?
    \item What makes a dashboard useful rather than merely attractive?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand Power BI and Tableau as business analytics tools: workflow, modelling ideas, comparison points, dashboard design principles, and common reporting mistakes.}




% ============================================================
% CHAPTER: MATHEMATICS FOR AI AND MACHINE LEARNING
% ============================================================
\chapter{Mathematics for AI and Machine Learning}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} build usable mathematical intuition for ML and AI. You are not aiming to become a pure mathematician here. You are aiming to understand what the symbols mean, why the formulas matter, and how they connect to code and model behaviour.
\end{studybox}

This chapter covers the essential mathematics you need to understand AI and machine learning. You do not need a maths degree, but you do need to understand \emph{why} these concepts matter and \emph{how} they connect to real ML systems. We will build intuition first, then formalise.

\begin{notebox}
\textbf{How to study this chapter without getting overwhelmed:}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item read the plain-English explanation first
    \item work through the tiny numerical example
    \item only then look at the formula
    \item finally connect it to the Python code
\end{enumerate}
If the symbols feel heavy, slow down and return to the meaning of each part rather than pushing ahead mechanically.
\end{notebox}

\section{Why Maths Matters in ML}

Every machine learning algorithm is, at its core, a mathematical optimisation problem: find the parameters that minimise a loss function. To understand what your model is doing, debug it when it fails, and choose the right algorithm, you need these mathematical foundations.

\begin{notebox}
You do not need to memorise proofs. You need to understand what each concept \emph{means}, how to \emph{read} formulas, and how to \emph{connect} the maths to the code you write.
\end{notebox}

\section{How to Read a Formula Without Panicking}

Many learners freeze when they see notation. Use this sequence every time:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item identify the symbols
    \item say what each symbol represents in words
    \item ask what the formula is trying to measure or compute
    \item test it on a tiny example
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example:}
\[
\hat{y} = w_1x_1 + w_2x_2 + b
\]

\textbf{Read it like this:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item $\hat{y}$ means the model's prediction
    \item $x_1$ and $x_2$ are input features
    \item $w_1$ and $w_2$ are learned weights
    \item $b$ is a bias term
    \item the formula says: ``multiply each input by its importance, add them together, then shift by a bias''
\end{itemize}

\begin{intuitionbox}
Most formulas become less scary once you translate them back into a sentence. Do that before worrying about memorisation.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{Linear Algebra}

Linear algebra is the mathematics of vectors and matrices. It is the language of data: every dataset is a matrix, every data point is a vector, and every ML operation is a matrix transformation.

\subsection{Scalars, Vectors, and Matrices}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Scalar:} A single number. Example: temperature = 20, price = 99.99. Notation: $x$.
    \item \textbf{Vector:} An ordered list of numbers. Example: a data point with 3 features = $\mathbf{x} = [170, 70, 25]$ (height, weight, age). Notation: $\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{R}^n$.
    \item \textbf{Matrix:} A 2D grid of numbers (rows $\times$ columns). Example: a dataset of 100 people with 3 features = $\mathbf{X} \in \mathbb{R}^{100 \times 3}$. Notation: $\mathbf{X}$.
    \item \textbf{Tensor:} A generalisation to any number of dimensions. A 3D tensor could be a colour image: height $\times$ width $\times$ 3 (RGB channels). Deep learning frameworks (TensorFlow, PyTorch) are named after tensors.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Vectors and matrices in NumPy}]
import numpy as np

# Scalar
x = 5

# Vector (1D array)
v = np.array([170, 70, 25])  # height, weight, age
print(v.shape)  # (3,) -- 3-dimensional vector

# Matrix (2D array): 3 people x 3 features
X = np.array([
    [170, 70, 25],  # person 1
    [160, 55, 30],  # person 2
    [180, 85, 22],  # person 3
])
print(X.shape)  # (3, 3) -- 3 rows, 3 columns

# Tensor (3D array): e.g., 2 colour images of 4x4 pixels
images = np.random.rand(2, 4, 4, 3)
print(images.shape)  # (2, 4, 4, 3) -- 2 images, 4x4, RGB
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Vector Operations}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Addition:} $\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} = [a_1 + b_1, a_2 + b_2, \ldots]$. Add corresponding elements.
    \item \textbf{Scalar multiplication:} $c \cdot \mathbf{a} = [c \cdot a_1, c \cdot a_2, \ldots]$. Scale every element.
    \item \textbf{Dot product:} $\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} = a_1 b_1 + a_2 b_2 + \ldots + a_n b_n$. Returns a single number. \textbf{This is the most important operation in ML.} Every neuron in a neural network computes a dot product of weights and inputs.
    \item \textbf{Magnitude (norm):} $\|\mathbf{a}\| = \sqrt{a_1^2 + a_2^2 + \ldots + a_n^2}$. The ``length'' of a vector.
    \item \textbf{Cosine similarity:} $\cos(\theta) = \frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \cdot \|\mathbf{b}\|}$. Measures how similar two vectors are (used in embeddings and search). Range: $-1$ to $1$. Value of $1$ means identical direction.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Vector operations}]
a = np.array([1, 2, 3])
b = np.array([4, 5, 6])

# Dot product: this is what a neuron does!
dot = np.dot(a, b)  # 1*4 + 2*5 + 3*6 = 32

# Magnitude (L2 norm)
mag = np.linalg.norm(a)  # sqrt(1+4+9) = 3.74

# Cosine similarity
cos_sim = np.dot(a, b) / (np.linalg.norm(a) * np.linalg.norm(b))
print(f"Cosine similarity: {cos_sim:.4f}")  # 0.9746
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Matrix Operations}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Matrix multiplication:} $\mathbf{C} = \mathbf{A} \times \mathbf{B}$ where each entry $C_{ij} = \sum_k A_{ik} B_{kj}$. \textbf{Rule:} columns of $\mathbf{A}$ must equal rows of $\mathbf{B}$. If $\mathbf{A}$ is $m \times n$ and $\mathbf{B}$ is $n \times p$, result is $m \times p$.
    \item \textbf{Transpose:} $\mathbf{A}^T$ swaps rows and columns. If $\mathbf{A}$ is $3 \times 2$, $\mathbf{A}^T$ is $2 \times 3$.
    \item \textbf{Identity matrix:} $\mathbf{I}$ is the matrix equivalent of 1. $\mathbf{A} \times \mathbf{I} = \mathbf{A}$.
    \item \textbf{Inverse:} $\mathbf{A}^{-1}$ is the matrix that ``undoes'' $\mathbf{A}$: $\mathbf{A} \times \mathbf{A}^{-1} = \mathbf{I}$. Not all matrices have inverses.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Why matrix multiplication matters in ML:} A neural network layer is literally a matrix multiplication: $\mathbf{output} = \text{activation}(\mathbf{W} \cdot \mathbf{x} + \mathbf{b})$ where $\mathbf{W}$ is the weight matrix, $\mathbf{x}$ is the input vector, and $\mathbf{b}$ is the bias vector.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Matrix multiplication --- what a neural network layer does}]
# Simulate a neural network layer
X = np.array([[1, 2, 3],
              [4, 5, 6]])              # 2 samples, 3 features

W = np.random.randn(3, 4)              # weights: 3 inputs -> 4 outputs
b = np.random.randn(4)                 # bias: 4 values

# Forward pass (matrix multiplication + bias)
output = X @ W + b  # @ is matrix multiplication in Python
print(output.shape)  # (2, 4) -- 2 samples, 4 output features

# Apply ReLU activation
output = np.maximum(0, output)  # element-wise max(0, x)
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Calculus for Machine Learning}

Calculus answers the question: how does the output change when we change the input? In ML, this tells us how to update weights to reduce error.

\subsection{Derivatives}

\textbf{What is a derivative?} It measures the \emph{rate of change}---the slope of a function at a point.

\textbf{Intuition:} If $f(x) = x^2$ (a parabola), the derivative $f'(x) = 2x$ tells you:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item At $x = 3$: slope is $6$ (steep uphill)
    \item At $x = 0$: slope is $0$ (flat---this is the minimum!)
    \item At $x = -2$: slope is $-4$ (downhill)
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Key rules:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Power rule: $\frac{d}{dx}(x^n) = n \cdot x^{n-1}$
    \item Constant rule: $\frac{d}{dx}(c) = 0$
    \item Sum rule: $\frac{d}{dx}(f + g) = f' + g'$
    \item Chain rule: $\frac{d}{dx}f(g(x)) = f'(g(x)) \cdot g'(x)$
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Gradients}

When you have a function with multiple inputs (e.g., loss depends on many weights), the \textbf{gradient} is a vector of all partial derivatives:

$$\nabla f = \left[\frac{\partial f}{\partial w_1}, \frac{\partial f}{\partial w_2}, \ldots, \frac{\partial f}{\partial w_n}\right]$$

The gradient points in the direction of steepest increase. To \textbf{minimise} the loss, move in the \emph{opposite} direction---this is \textbf{gradient descent}.

\subsection{Gradient Descent}

\textbf{Gradient descent} is the core algorithm for training all neural networks:

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Start with random weights.
    \item Compute the loss (how wrong the predictions are).
    \item Compute the gradient (which direction reduces loss).
    \item Update weights: $w_{\text{new}} = w_{\text{old}} - \eta \cdot \nabla L$ where $\eta$ (eta) is the learning rate.
    \item Repeat until loss is small enough.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Gradient descent from scratch}]
# Simple gradient descent: find the minimum of f(x) = (x - 3)^2
# The minimum is at x = 3 (where the derivative = 0)

x = 10.0            # start far from minimum
learning_rate = 0.1

for step in range(50):
    gradient = 2 * (x - 3)  # derivative of (x-3)^2
    x = x - learning_rate * gradient  # update step
    loss = (x - 3) ** 2
    if step % 10 == 0:
        print(f"Step {step}: x = {x:.4f}, loss = {loss:.6f}")

# Output: x converges to 3.0, loss converges to 0.0
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Backpropagation (The Chain Rule in Action)}

\textbf{Backpropagation} is just the chain rule applied to compute gradients through many layers:

If your network computes $y = f_3(f_2(f_1(x)))$ (three layers), the gradient of the loss with respect to the first layer's weights is:

$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial w_1} = \frac{\partial L}{\partial f_3} \cdot \frac{\partial f_3}{\partial f_2} \cdot \frac{\partial f_2}{\partial f_1} \cdot \frac{\partial f_1}{\partial w_1}$$

Each term is computed during the backward pass. TensorFlow and PyTorch compute this automatically---you do not need to derive gradients by hand.

\section{Probability and Statistics}

Probability helps you reason about uncertainty. ML models make \emph{probabilistic} predictions: ``this tumour is 92\% likely to be malignant'' rather than ``this is definitely malignant.''

\subsection{Probability Basics}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Probability:} A number between 0 and 1 representing the likelihood of an event. $P(\text{heads}) = 0.5$.
    \item \textbf{Joint probability:} $P(A \text{ and } B)$. If independent: $P(A \cap B) = P(A) \cdot P(B)$.
    \item \textbf{Conditional probability:} $P(A | B) = \frac{P(A \cap B)}{P(B)}$. ``Probability of A \emph{given} B has occurred.''
    \item \textbf{Bayes' Theorem:} $P(A|B) = \frac{P(B|A) \cdot P(A)}{P(B)}$. Central to many ML algorithms (Naive Bayes, Bayesian inference).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Distributions}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Normal (Gaussian) distribution:} Bell curve. Most natural phenomena follow this. Defined by mean $\mu$ and standard deviation $\sigma$. Central to many ML assumptions.
    \item \textbf{Bernoulli distribution:} Binary outcome (coin flip). $P(\text{success}) = p$.
    \item \textbf{Uniform distribution:} All outcomes equally likely. Used for random initialisation.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Key Statistics Concepts}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Mean (average):} $\bar{x} = \frac{1}{n}\sum x_i$. Central tendency.
    \item \textbf{Variance:} $\sigma^2 = \frac{1}{n}\sum (x_i - \bar{x})^2$. Measures spread.
    \item \textbf{Standard deviation:} $\sigma = \sqrt{\text{variance}}$. Same units as the data.
    \item \textbf{Correlation:} Measures linear relationship between two variables ($-1$ to $1$). Correlation does \emph{not} imply causation.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Statistics in Python}]
import numpy as np

data = np.array([170, 165, 180, 175, 190, 168, 172])

print(f"Mean: {data.mean():.1f}")      # 174.3
print(f"Std: {data.std():.1f}")        # 7.7
print(f"Variance: {data.var():.1f}")   # 59.4
print(f"Median: {np.median(data):.1f}")  # 172.0
print(f"Min: {data.min()}, Max: {data.max()}")

# Normal distribution: generate random data
samples = np.random.normal(loc=170, scale=10, size=1000)
# loc=mean, scale=std, size=number of samples
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Loss Functions}

\textbf{A loss function} measures how wrong your model's predictions are. Training = finding weights that minimise the loss.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Mean Squared Error (MSE):} $L = \frac{1}{n}\sum(y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2$. For regression. Penalises large errors heavily.
    \item \textbf{Binary Cross-Entropy:} $L = -\frac{1}{n}\sum[y_i \log(\hat{y}_i) + (1-y_i)\log(1-\hat{y}_i)]$. For binary classification. Measures how far predicted probabilities are from true labels.
    \item \textbf{Categorical Cross-Entropy:} Generalisation of binary CE to multiple classes. Used with softmax output.
\end{itemize}

\begin{interviewbox}
\textbf{Common interview question:} ``What loss function would you use for a binary classification problem?'' Answer: Binary cross-entropy. ``What about multi-class?'' Categorical cross-entropy with softmax. ``Regression?'' MSE or MAE (mean absolute error). Always explain \emph{why}---MSE penalises outliers more; MAE is more robust.
\end{interviewbox}

\section{Common Maths Confusions in ML}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Derivative vs gradient:} a derivative is for one variable; a gradient is the vector of partial derivatives for many variables.
    \item \textbf{Vector vs matrix:} one data point is often a vector; a full dataset is often a matrix.
    \item \textbf{Loss vs accuracy:} loss is the training objective; accuracy is one evaluation metric.
    \item \textbf{Probability vs confidence:} model probabilities are estimates, not proof.
    \item \textbf{Formula recognition vs understanding:} knowing the shape of an equation is not the same as understanding what it is doing.
\end{itemize}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Revision drill:} pick one formula from this chapter and do four things:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item define every symbol
    \item explain the formula in one sentence
    \item compute a tiny numerical example
    \item state where it appears in code or model training
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercisebox}

\begin{tipbox}
\textbf{Key takeaway:} Linear algebra gives you the language of data. Calculus gives you the method to learn (gradient descent). Probability gives you the framework for uncertainty. Loss functions define what ``correct'' means. Together, these form the mathematical backbone of every ML system.
\end{tipbox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Linear algebra gives you the language of data and model parameters.
    \item Calculus explains how models learn by following gradients.
    \item Probability and statistics help you reason about uncertainty, error, and evaluation.
    \item Loss functions define what the model is trying to optimise.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Why is matrix multiplication so central in machine learning?
    \item What does a derivative tell you intuitively?
    \item Why does gradient descent move in the opposite direction of the gradient?
    \item What role do probability and variance play in ML thinking?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand the essential maths for AI: vectors, matrices, matrix multiplication, derivatives, gradients, gradient descent, backpropagation, probability, distributions, statistics, and loss functions. You know how these connect to real ML code and why they matter.}



% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 24 — MACHINE LEARNING WITH SCIKIT-LEARN
% ============================================================
\chapter{Machine Learning with Scikit-learn}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{How to study this chapter:} do not treat the algorithms as isolated recipes. First build the mathematical intuition, then connect each algorithm to the kind of pattern it is trying to learn, and only then memorise library usage.
\end{studybox}

\begin{notebox}
\textbf{Recommended study order for this chapter:}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item understand the task type (classification, regression, clustering)
    \item learn what kind of pattern each algorithm assumes
    \item compare two or three baseline models
    \item then learn tuning, pipelines, and evaluation
\end{enumerate}
This chapter is much easier if you think in decisions rather than in a giant list of algorithms.
\end{notebox}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=1.2cm]
    \node[databox] (data) {Raw data};
    \node[flowbox, right=of data] (split) {Train / val / test};
    \node[flowbox, right=of split] (prep) {Preprocess};
    \node[flowbox, right=of prep] (train) {Train model};
    \node[flowbox, right=of train] (eval) {Evaluate};
    \node[storagebox, below=1.45cm of train] (deploy) {Deploy\\and monitor};

    \draw[flowarrow] (data) -- (split);
    \draw[flowarrow] (split) -- (prep);
    \draw[flowarrow] (prep) -- (train);
    \draw[flowarrow] (train) -- (eval);
    \draw[flowarrow] (eval) -- (deploy);
    \draw[softarrow] (deploy) -| (prep);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{A practical machine learning workflow}
\label{fig:ml-workflow}
\end{figure}

This diagram matters because ML is not one step called ``train a model.'' It is a pipeline where preprocessing, evaluation, and monitoring are as important as the algorithm itself.

\section{Mathematics Foundations for AI and Machine Learning}

Many learners fear the mathematics of AI because they meet formulas before intuition. That is the wrong order. The right order is:

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item understand the idea in plain English
    \item see a tiny numerical example
    \item connect the idea to model training
    \item then read the formula
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Vectors}

\textbf{What they are:} ordered lists of numbers. In ML, a vector usually represents one data point or one set of learned weights.

\textbf{Example:} a bike listing might be represented as:
\[
\mathbf{x} = [689,\ 2,\ 1,\ 0]
\]
where the features could mean engine size, stage number, whether it is published, and whether it includes dyno verification.

\textbf{Why vectors matter:} models do not ``see'' text labels such as ``Yamaha'' or ``Stage 2''. They see numerical feature vectors.

\subsection{Matrices}

\textbf{What they are:} a grid of numbers. If one vector represents one example, then a matrix often represents an entire dataset.

\[
X =
\begin{bmatrix}
689 & 1 & 1 \\
998 & 2 & 0 \\
847 & 1 & 1
\end{bmatrix}
\]

Each row is one training example. Each column is one feature.

\textbf{Mental model:} a matrix is just a compact way of storing many vectors at once.

\subsection{Matrix Multiplication Intuition}

When a model makes a prediction, it often multiplies an input vector by a weight vector or weight matrix.

\[
\hat{y} = w_1x_1 + w_2x_2 + \cdots + w_nx_n + b
\]

\textbf{Plain-English meaning:} each feature contributes some amount to the prediction, and the weight controls how important that feature is.

\textbf{Tiny example:}
\[
\hat{y} = 0.2(689) + 12(2) + 30(1) + 5
\]

The model is combining weighted evidence from different features.

\subsection{Derivatives and Gradients}

\textbf{Derivative:} tells you how much an output changes when an input changes a little.

\textbf{In ML terms:} if you change a weight slightly, does the loss go up or down? The derivative tells you the direction and sensitivity.

\textbf{Gradient:} the collection of all partial derivatives for a multivariable function. If the loss depends on many weights, the gradient tells you how each weight should move.

\begin{intuitionbox}
The gradient is like standing on a hill in fog. It tells you which direction is uphill. To minimize loss, you move in the opposite direction.
\end{intuitionbox}

\subsection{Loss Functions}

\textbf{Loss} measures how wrong the model is.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Mean Squared Error (MSE):} common for regression
    \item \textbf{Cross-Entropy Loss:} common for classification
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Why loss matters:} training is the process of adjusting model parameters to reduce loss on the training data without overfitting.

\subsection{Gradient Descent}

\textbf{Gradient descent} is the optimization method used to reduce loss:

\[
w \leftarrow w - \eta \frac{\partial L}{\partial w}
\]

where:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item $w$ is a weight
    \item $L$ is the loss
    \item $\frac{\partial L}{\partial w}$ is the derivative of loss with respect to that weight
    \item $\eta$ is the learning rate
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Interpretation:} move each weight a small amount in the direction that reduces error.

\subsection{Probability, Expectation, and Variance}

\textbf{Probability} gives a language for uncertainty. ML models often output probabilities rather than certainties.

\textbf{Expectation} is the long-run average value of a random variable.

\textbf{Variance} measures how spread out values are around the mean.

\textbf{Why this matters in ML:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item classification models output estimated probabilities
    \item uncertainty matters for threshold decisions
    \item variance helps explain unstable models and overfitting
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Train, Validation, and Test Thinking}

Mathematically, a model is not useful because it memorizes the training set. It is useful because it generalizes to unseen data.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Training set:} used to fit parameters
    \item \textbf{Validation set:} used to choose models and hyperparameters
    \item \textbf{Test set:} used once at the end to estimate real performance
\end{itemize}

\begin{warningbox}
If you repeatedly tune against the test set, it stops being a real test set. You have leaked information from evaluation back into development.
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{What You Need to Remember Mathematically}

You do \textbf{not} need to be a pure mathematician to become strong at ML. But you do need these durable ideas:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item features are represented as vectors
    \item datasets are often represented as matrices
    \item models learn weights
    \item predictions are produced by combining features and weights
    \item loss measures error
    \item gradients show how to reduce error
    \item probability helps express uncertainty
    \item good evaluation depends on data splits and honest measurement
\end{itemize}

\section{What Is Machine Learning?}

\textbf{Machine learning (ML)} is a branch of artificial intelligence where computers learn to make predictions or decisions from data, rather than being explicitly programmed with rules. Instead of writing \texttt{if price > 500: category = "expensive"}, you give the computer thousands of examples and it \emph{learns} the pattern itself.

\textbf{Formal definition:} A computer program is said to \emph{learn} from experience $E$ with respect to some task $T$ and performance measure $P$, if its performance on $T$ as measured by $P$ improves with experience $E$. (Tom Mitchell, 1997)

\textbf{Analogy:} Imagine teaching a child to recognise cats. You do not give them a rulebook (``cats have pointed ears, whiskers, fur\ldots''). Instead, you show them thousands of pictures of cats and not-cats, and they learn the pattern naturally. ML works the same way.

\subsection{Types of Machine Learning}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Supervised learning:} Learn from \emph{labelled} examples. You provide input $X$ and the correct output $y$, and the algorithm learns a function $f$ such that $f(X) \approx y$.
    \begin{itemize}
        \item \textbf{Classification:} Predict a discrete category. Examples: spam/not spam, malignant/benign tumour, cat/dog image.
        \item \textbf{Regression:} Predict a continuous number. Examples: house price, temperature, stock price.
    \end{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Unsupervised learning:} Learn from \emph{unlabelled} data---find hidden patterns without being told what to look for.
    \begin{itemize}
        \item \textbf{Clustering:} Group similar data points together (K-Means, DBSCAN). Example: grouping customers by purchasing behaviour.
        \item \textbf{Dimensionality reduction:} Reduce the number of features while preserving important information (PCA, t-SNE). Useful for visualisation and denoising.
        \item \textbf{Anomaly detection:} Identify outliers/unusual data points. Example: fraud detection, server failure detection.
    \end{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Semi-supervised learning:} A mix---small amount of labelled data + large amount of unlabelled data. Common in practice because labelling is expensive.
    \item \textbf{Reinforcement learning:} An agent learns by interacting with an environment, receiving rewards for good actions and penalties for bad ones. Used in: game AI, robotics, self-driving cars.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Type} & \textbf{Data} & \textbf{Goal} & \textbf{Example} \\
\midrule
Supervised & Labelled & Predict known target & Spam detection \\
Unsupervised & Unlabelled & Discover structure & Customer clustering \\
Reinforcement & Reward signal & Learn action policy & Game-playing agent \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{interviewbox}
ML type questions are extremely common. You should be able to: (1) classify any problem as supervised/unsupervised/reinforcement, (2) distinguish classification from regression, (3) name 3 algorithms for each type, (4) explain bias-variance trade-off.
\end{interviewbox}

\section{Core ML Algorithms Explained}

Before diving into each algorithm, keep one framing question in mind:
\begin{center}
\textit{``What kind of pattern is this model trying to learn, and what kind of mistake is it likely to make?''}
\end{center}

\subsection{Linear Regression}

\textbf{What it does:} Fits a straight line through data to predict a continuous value.

\textbf{Equation:} $\hat{y} = w_1 x_1 + w_2 x_2 + \ldots + w_n x_n + b$ where $w_i$ are weights (slopes) and $b$ is the bias (intercept).

\textbf{When to use:} When you expect a roughly linear relationship between inputs and output. Example: predicting house price from square footage.

\textbf{Limitation:} Cannot capture non-linear relationships.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Linear regression with scikit-learn}]
from sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression
from sklearn.metrics import mean_squared_error, r2_score

# Create and train
model = LinearRegression()
model.fit(X_train, y_train)

# Predict
y_pred = model.predict(X_test)

# Evaluate
mse = mean_squared_error(y_test, y_pred)
rmse = mse ** 0.5  # root mean squared error
r2 = r2_score(y_test, y_pred)  # 1.0 = perfect, 0 = useless
print(f"RMSE: {rmse:.2f}, R2: {r2:.4f}")

# Inspect: what did it learn?
print(f"Coefficients: {model.coef_}")
print(f"Intercept: {model.intercept_}")
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Logistic Regression}

\textbf{What it does:} Despite the name, this is a \emph{classification} algorithm. It predicts the probability that an input belongs to a particular class.

\textbf{How it works:} Applies a sigmoid function $\sigma(z) = \frac{1}{1 + e^{-z}}$ to a linear combination of features, squeezing the output between 0 and 1.

\textbf{When to use:} Binary classification with roughly linearly separable data. Fast, interpretable, good baseline.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Logistic regression}]
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression

model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000)
model.fit(X_train, y_train)

# Predict class
y_pred = model.predict(X_test)

# Predict probability
y_proba = model.predict_proba(X_test)[:, 1]  # P(class=1)
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Decision Trees}

\textbf{What it does:} Builds a tree of yes/no questions to classify or predict. At each node, it asks a question about one feature (``Is age > 30?''), and splits the data based on the answer.

\textbf{How it decides which question to ask:} It picks the question that best separates the classes, measured by \textbf{Gini impurity} (classification) or \textbf{mean squared error} (regression).

\textbf{Strengths:} Easy to understand, no feature scaling needed, handles mixed data types.

\textbf{Weaknesses:} Prone to overfitting (memorises training data), sensitive to small changes in data.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Decision tree with visualisation}]
from sklearn.tree import DecisionTreeClassifier
from sklearn import tree
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

model = DecisionTreeClassifier(
    max_depth=5,           # limit depth to prevent overfitting
    min_samples_leaf=10,   # at least 10 samples per leaf
    random_state=42
)
model.fit(X_train, y_train)

# Visualise the tree
plt.figure(figsize=(20, 10))
tree.plot_tree(model, feature_names=feature_names,
               class_names=["Benign", "Malignant"],
               filled=True, rounded=True)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("decision_tree.png", dpi=150)
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Random Forest}

\textbf{What it does:} Builds many decision trees (a ``forest'') and averages their predictions. Each tree is trained on a random subset of data and features.

\textbf{Why it works:} Individual trees overfit in different ways. By averaging many diverse trees, the errors cancel out---this is called \textbf{ensemble learning}.

\textbf{When to use:} Excellent default choice for most tabular problems. Robust, accurate, handles non-linear relationships.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Random forest with feature importance}]
from sklearn.ensemble import RandomForestClassifier
import numpy as np

model = RandomForestClassifier(
    n_estimators=100,      # number of trees
    max_depth=10,          # max depth per tree
    min_samples_leaf=5,
    random_state=42,
    n_jobs=-1              # use all CPU cores
)
model.fit(X_train, y_train)

# Feature importance: which features matter most?
importances = model.feature_importances_
indices = np.argsort(importances)[::-1]
for i in range(min(10, len(indices))):
    print(f"{feature_names[indices[i]]}: "
          f"{importances[indices[i]]:.4f}")
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Support Vector Machine (SVM)}

\textbf{What it does:} Finds the hyperplane (decision boundary) that maximally separates two classes. The ``support vectors'' are the data points closest to this boundary.

\textbf{Kernel trick:} SVMs can handle non-linear data by projecting it into a higher-dimensional space using kernel functions (RBF, polynomial). In this higher space, the data may become linearly separable.

\textbf{When to use:} Small to medium datasets, high-dimensional data, text classification. Less effective on very large datasets (slow training).

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={SVM classifier}]
from sklearn.svm import SVC

model = SVC(
    kernel="rbf",     # radial basis function (non-linear)
    C=1.0,            # regularisation: higher = less regularisation
    gamma="scale",    # kernel coefficient
    probability=True  # enable predict_proba
)
model.fit(X_train_scaled, y_train)
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{K-Nearest Neighbours (KNN)}

\textbf{What it does:} Classifies a new data point by looking at the $k$ closest training examples and taking a majority vote. No actual ``training'' happens---it just stores the data.

\textbf{Distance metric:} Usually Euclidean distance $\sqrt{\sum (x_i - y_i)^2}$.

\textbf{When to use:} Small datasets, when you want simplicity. Poor for high-dimensional data (curse of dimensionality).

\begin{warningbox}
KNN is \textbf{very sensitive to feature scaling}. Always standardise your features before using KNN. A feature ranging 0--1000 will dominate one ranging 0--1.
\end{warningbox}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={KNN classifier}]
from sklearn.neighbors import KNeighborsClassifier

model = KNeighborsClassifier(
    n_neighbors=5,         # try odd numbers: 3, 5, 7
    weights="distance",    # closer neighbours have more influence
    metric="euclidean"
)
model.fit(X_train_scaled, y_train)
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Gradient Boosting (XGBoost, LightGBM)}

\textbf{What it does:} Builds trees \emph{sequentially}---each new tree tries to correct the errors of the previous ones. This ``boosting'' process is extremely powerful.

\textbf{XGBoost (Extreme Gradient Boosting):} The most popular implementation. Dominates Kaggle competitions and widely used in industry.

\textbf{When to use:} When you need maximum accuracy on tabular data. Often the best-performing algorithm for structured datasets.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={XGBoost classifier}]
# pip install xgboost
from xgboost import XGBClassifier

model = XGBClassifier(
    n_estimators=200,
    max_depth=6,
    learning_rate=0.1,     # step size
    subsample=0.8,         # fraction of data per tree
    colsample_bytree=0.8,  # fraction of features per tree
    random_state=42,
    eval_metric="logloss"
)
model.fit(
    X_train, y_train,
    eval_set=[(X_test, y_test)],
    verbose=False
)
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{K-Means Clustering (Unsupervised)}

\textbf{What it does:} Partitions data into $k$ clusters by iteratively assigning each point to the nearest cluster centre (centroid) and updating the centroids.

\textbf{How to choose $k$:} Use the Elbow Method---plot inertia vs.\,$k$ and look for the ``elbow'' where improvement slows.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={K-Means clustering with elbow method}]
from sklearn.cluster import KMeans
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# Elbow method to find optimal k
inertias = []
for k in range(1, 11):
    km = KMeans(n_clusters=k, random_state=42, n_init=10)
    km.fit(X_scaled)
    inertias.append(km.inertia_)

plt.plot(range(1, 11), inertias, marker="o")
plt.xlabel("Number of clusters (k)")
plt.ylabel("Inertia")
plt.title("Elbow Method")
plt.show()

# Train with chosen k
kmeans = KMeans(n_clusters=3, random_state=42, n_init=10)
labels = kmeans.fit_predict(X_scaled)
\end{lstlisting}

\section{How to Choose a First Model}

Beginners often ask, ``Which algorithm should I use?'' The honest answer is: pick a strong baseline that matches the data type and business constraint, then compare.

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l l l l}
\toprule
\textbf{Situation} & \textbf{Good first choice} & \textbf{Why} & \textbf{Watch out for} \\
\midrule
Small tabular classification & Logistic Regression & Fast, interpretable baseline & Linear boundary limits \\
General tabular classification & Random Forest & Strong default, robust & Less interpretable \\
High-accuracy tabular work & XGBoost & Excellent performance & More tuning complexity \\
Small high-dimensional text & Linear / SVM & Strong on sparse features & Scaling, tuning \\
Distance-based intuition & KNN & Simple to explain & Poor scaling, feature sensitivity \\
Unlabelled grouping task & K-Means & Simple clustering baseline & Must choose \(k\), spherical clusters \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{intuitionbox}
Model selection is rarely about finding the fanciest algorithm. It is about matching the algorithm to the data, the cost of errors, interpretability requirements, and the engineering constraints of the project.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{How to Revise ML Effectively}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item group models by task type instead of memorising them randomly
    \item always pair an algorithm with: best use case, limitation, and metric
    \item compare baseline vs stronger model rather than studying one model in isolation
    \item explain one full pipeline aloud: split $\to$ preprocess $\to$ train $\to$ evaluate
\end{itemize}

\section{Feature Engineering}

\textbf{Feature engineering} is the process of creating, transforming, or selecting features to improve model performance. Often more important than the choice of algorithm.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Feature engineering techniques}]
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
from sklearn.preprocessing import (
    StandardScaler, MinMaxScaler, LabelEncoder,
    OneHotEncoder, PolynomialFeatures
)

# 1. Handle missing values
df["age"].fillna(df["age"].median(), inplace=True)
df["category"].fillna("unknown", inplace=True)
df.dropna(subset=["critical_column"], inplace=True)

# 2. Encode categorical variables
# Label encoding (ordinal: low < medium < high)
le = LabelEncoder()
df["size_encoded"] = le.fit_transform(df["size"])

# One-hot encoding (nominal: red, blue, green)
df = pd.get_dummies(df, columns=["colour"], drop_first=True)

# 3. Scale numerical features (CRITICAL for SVM, KNN, neural nets)
scaler = StandardScaler()  # mean=0, std=1
X_scaled = scaler.fit_transform(X)

# 4. Create new features (domain knowledge!)
df["price_per_sqft"] = df["price"] / df["sqft"]
df["age_group"] = pd.cut(df["age"], bins=[0,18,35,65,100],
                         labels=["child","young","adult","senior"])

# 5. Log transform skewed distributions
df["log_price"] = np.log1p(df["price"])  # log(1+x) handles zeros
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{tipbox}
The golden rule of preprocessing: \textbf{fit on training data, transform on both.} Never fit your scaler/encoder on the test set---this causes data leakage.
\end{tipbox}

\section{Cross-Validation}

\textbf{Problem:} A single train/test split can give misleading results.

\textbf{Solution:} \textbf{K-Fold Cross-Validation} splits data into $k$ folds. For each fold, train on $k-1$ folds and test on the remaining one. Repeat $k$ times and average.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Cross-validation}]
from sklearn.model_selection import cross_val_score, StratifiedKFold

# Basic cross-validation
scores = cross_val_score(
    model, X, y,
    cv=5,              # 5-fold CV
    scoring="accuracy"  # or "f1", "roc_auc"
)
print(f"CV Accuracy: {scores.mean():.4f} (+/- {scores.std():.4f})")

# Stratified K-Fold (preserves class distribution)
skf = StratifiedKFold(n_splits=5, shuffle=True, random_state=42)
scores = cross_val_score(model, X, y, cv=skf, scoring="f1")
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Hyperparameter Tuning}

\textbf{Hyperparameters} are settings you choose \emph{before} training. The model does not learn these---you must tune them.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Hyperparameter tuning}]
from sklearn.model_selection import GridSearchCV

# Grid search: try every combination
param_grid = {
    "n_estimators": [50, 100, 200],
    "max_depth": [5, 10, 15, None],
    "min_samples_leaf": [1, 5, 10],
}

grid_search = GridSearchCV(
    RandomForestClassifier(random_state=42),
    param_grid,
    cv=5,
    scoring="f1",
    n_jobs=-1,
    verbose=1
)
grid_search.fit(X_train, y_train)

print(f"Best params: {grid_search.best_params_}")
print(f"Best F1: {grid_search.best_score_:.4f}")
best_model = grid_search.best_estimator_
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Complete ML Pipeline}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Production ML pipeline}]
from sklearn.pipeline import Pipeline
from sklearn.compose import ColumnTransformer

numeric_features = ["age", "income", "score"]
categorical_features = ["category", "region"]

preprocessor = ColumnTransformer(
    transformers=[
