    \item \textbf{Weaviate:} Open-source, supports hybrid search (vector + keyword).
    \item \textbf{pgvector:} PostgreSQL extension for vector storage. Use your existing Postgres database.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={RAG with ChromaDB}]
# pip install chromadb
import chromadb

# 1. Create a collection (like a table)
client = chromadb.Client()
collection = client.create_collection("docs")

# 2. Add documents (automatically creates embeddings)
collection.add(
    documents=[
        "ECU tuning increases engine horsepower by modifying "
        "fuel injection and ignition timing maps.",
        "RevSync supports Yamaha MT-07, Kawasaki Z650, "
        "and Honda CB650R motorcycles.",
        "Always back up the stock ECU map before flashing "
        "a custom tune to avoid bricking the ECU.",
    ],
    ids=["doc1", "doc2", "doc3"],
    metadatas=[
        {"source": "tuning_guide"},
        {"source": "compatibility"},
        {"source": "safety"},
    ]
)

# 3. Query (find relevant documents)
results = collection.query(
    query_texts=["Which bikes does RevSync support?"],
    n_results=2  # return top 2 most relevant
)
print(results["documents"])
# [['RevSync supports Yamaha MT-07, Kawasaki Z650...',
#   'ECU tuning increases engine horsepower...']]
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Document Chunking Strategies}

\textbf{Chunking} is how you split documents before embedding them. This is critical---bad chunking ruins retrieval quality.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Fixed-size chunks:} Split every $N$ characters/tokens. Simple but may cut sentences mid-thought.
    \item \textbf{Sentence-based:} Split on sentence boundaries. Preserves semantic units.
    \item \textbf{Paragraph-based:} Each paragraph is a chunk. Good for well-structured documents.
    \item \textbf{Recursive character splitting:} Try to split on paragraphs first, then sentences, then words. The LangChain default.
    \item \textbf{Semantic chunking:} Use embeddings to detect topic boundaries. Most sophisticated.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Document chunking}]
# Using LangChain's text splitter
# pip install langchain
from langchain.text_splitter import RecursiveCharacterTextSplitter

splitter = RecursiveCharacterTextSplitter(
    chunk_size=500,       # max characters per chunk
    chunk_overlap=50,     # overlap between chunks (context bridge)
    separators=["\n\n", "\n", ". ", " ", ""]  # try these in order
)

text = open("motorcycle_tuning_guide.txt").read()
chunks = splitter.split_text(text)
print(f"Split into {len(chunks)} chunks")
for i, chunk in enumerate(chunks[:3]):
    print(f"\nChunk {i}: ({len(chunk)} chars)")
    print(chunk[:100] + "...")
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{tipbox}
\textbf{Chunk size guidelines:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item 200--500 tokens: Good for precise Q\&A retrieval.
    \item 500--1000 tokens: Good for summarisation and broader context.
    \item Add 10--20\% overlap to avoid losing context at chunk boundaries.
\end{itemize}
\end{tipbox}

\section{Hybrid Retrieval and Reranking}

Basic vector retrieval is useful, but production systems often need more than ``top-\(k\) nearest embeddings.''

\subsection{Hybrid Retrieval}
Hybrid retrieval combines:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{keyword search} for exact terms, IDs, product names, and rare phrases
    \item \textbf{vector search} for semantic meaning and paraphrases
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Why hybrid helps:} a user asking for ``MT-07 ECU checksum issue'' may need exact token matching on ``MT-07'' plus semantic similarity on the rest of the phrase.

\subsection{Reranking}
Retrieval often returns several plausible chunks. A \textbf{reranker} scores the shortlisted chunks more precisely and reorders them.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Conceptual reranking flow}]
question = "What must I check before flashing an ECU?"

# Step 1: broad recall
retrieved_chunks = vector_db.search(question, top_k=10)

# Step 2: precise reranking
ranked_chunks = reranker.rank(question, retrieved_chunks)

# Step 3: pass best chunks to the LLM
context = ranked_chunks[:3]
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why this matters:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item first-stage retrieval optimises speed and broad recall
    \item reranking improves precision before generation
    \item this often produces more grounded answers without increasing context-window waste
\end{itemize}

\section{How to Reason About a Weak RAG System}

When a RAG application performs badly, ask where the failure happened:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item was the source content weak or poorly structured?
    \item was chunking too coarse or too fragmented?
    \item did retrieval miss the right evidence?
    \item did reranking fail to prioritize the best chunk?
    \item did the prompt misuse or ignore the retrieved context?
    \item did the product trust the output without verification?
\end{enumerate}

\begin{warningbox}
Many teams say ``the model hallucinated'' when the real problem was retrieval failure, poor chunking, weak prompts, or lack of output validation. Diagnose the pipeline, not just the model.
\end{warningbox}

\section{Complete RAG Pipeline}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Production RAG pipeline}]
import chromadb
import openai

client_ai = openai.OpenAI()
client_db = chromadb.Client()

# 1. Index documents
collection = client_db.create_collection("knowledge_base")

documents = [
    "ECU stands for Engine Control Unit. It is the computer "
    "that controls engine operation including fuel injection, "
    "ignition timing, and emission controls.",
    "Flashing a tune involves writing new calibration data "
    "to the ECU's memory. This modifies engine behaviour.",
    "Always perform a health check before flashing. Verify "
    "battery voltage is above 12.5V and engine is cool.",
    # ... hundreds more chunks from your knowledge base
]

collection.add(
    documents=documents,
    ids=[f"doc_{i}" for i in range(len(documents))]
)

# 2. RAG query function
def rag_query(question, n_results=3):
    # Step 1: Retrieve relevant chunks
    results = collection.query(
        query_texts=[question],
        n_results=n_results
    )
    context = "\n\n".join(results["documents"][0])

    # Step 2: Generate answer using retrieved context
    response = client_ai.chat.completions.create(
        model="gpt-4",
        messages=[
            {"role": "system",
             "content": f"""Answer the user's question using ONLY
the provided context. If the context doesn't contain enough
information, say "I don't have enough information to answer that."
Always cite which part of the context supports your answer.

Context:
{context}"""},
            {"role": "user", "content": question}
        ],
        temperature=0.1  # low temperature for factual answers
    )
    return response.choices[0].message.content

# 3. Use it
answer = rag_query("What should I check before flashing an ECU?")
print(answer)
# "Before flashing an ECU, you should perform a health check.
#  Specifically, verify that the battery voltage is above 12.5V
#  and that the engine is cool. [Source: doc_2]"
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Tool Use and Agent Patterns}

Not every LLM application should be a free-form chat response. Some should call tools, query databases, or trigger deterministic business logic.

\subsection{Tool Use}
\textbf{Tool use} means the model can request that the system perform an action such as:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item searching documentation
    \item calling a weather API
    \item looking up account state
    \item running a calculator
    \item querying a database
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Conceptual tool-call flow}]
user_question = "How many premium tune purchases happened today?"

# 1. Model recognises this requires structured data
# 2. Model requests a tool call, e.g. query_sales(date=today)
# 3. System runs the tool safely
# 4. Tool result is returned to the model
# 5. Model formats the final answer for the user
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Agent Patterns}
An \textbf{agent} is a system where the model can make or suggest multiple decisions across several steps. In practice, many agentic systems are just careful workflow orchestration:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item understand the goal
    \item choose a tool or retrieval step
    \item inspect the result
    \item decide whether another tool call is needed
    \item produce a final answer or action
\end{enumerate}

\begin{warningbox}
``Agent'' does not automatically mean ``better.'' Many tasks are more reliable with a simpler pipeline: retrieve context, call one tool, enforce structured output, then validate the result. Use agents only when multi-step decision-making is actually needed.
\end{warningbox}

\section{Verification and Hallucination Detection}

\textbf{Hallucination} is when an LLM generates information that sounds correct but is fabricated. This is a critical problem in production AI systems.

\textbf{Your VeriRAG project} addresses this with a \textbf{verify-repair loop}:

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Claim decomposition:} Break the LLM's response into individual factual claims.
    \item \textbf{Evidence retrieval:} For each claim, find supporting evidence in the knowledge base.
    \item \textbf{Verification:} Check if each claim is supported, contradicted, or unverifiable.
    \item \textbf{Repair:} Replace or remove unsupported claims. Regenerate if needed.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Claim verification pattern (VeriRAG concept)}]
def verify_response(response_text, knowledge_base):
    # 1. Decompose into claims
    claims = decompose_claims(response_text)

    verified_claims = []
    for claim in claims:
        # 2. Find supporting evidence
        evidence = knowledge_base.search(claim, top_k=3)

        # 3. Verify claim against evidence
        verification = verify_claim(claim, evidence)

        if verification.supported:
            verified_claims.append({
                "claim": claim,
                "status": "SUPPORTED",
                "evidence": verification.evidence_text,
                "confidence": verification.score
            })
        elif verification.contradicted:
            verified_claims.append({
                "claim": claim,
                "status": "CONTRADICTED",
                "correction": verification.correct_info
            })
        else:
            verified_claims.append({
                "claim": claim,
                "status": "UNVERIFIABLE",
                "note": "No evidence found in knowledge base"
            })

    return verified_claims
\end{lstlisting}

\section{RAG Evaluation Metrics}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Faithfulness:} Is the generated answer factually consistent with the retrieved context? Does it only say things supported by the sources?
    \item \textbf{Relevance (Answer):} Does the answer actually address the user's question?
    \item \textbf{Relevance (Context):} Are the retrieved chunks actually relevant to the question?
    \item \textbf{Groundedness:} Is every claim in the answer traceable to a specific source?
    \item \textbf{Recall:} Did the retrieval step find all relevant documents?
    \item \textbf{Precision:} Were the retrieved documents actually relevant (not noise)?
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={RAG evaluation with RAGAS}]
# pip install ragas
from ragas import evaluate
from ragas.metrics import (
    faithfulness, answer_relevancy,
    context_precision, context_recall
)

# Prepare evaluation data
eval_data = {
    "question": ["What is ECU tuning?"],
    "answer": ["ECU tuning modifies engine parameters..."],
    "contexts": [["ECU stands for Engine Control Unit..."]],
    "ground_truth": ["ECU tuning modifies fuel injection..."
                     "and ignition timing maps in the ECU."]
}

result = evaluate(
    eval_data,
    metrics=[faithfulness, answer_relevancy,
             context_precision, context_recall]
)
print(result)
# faithfulness: 0.95  (answer sticks to retrieved facts)
# answer_relevancy: 0.88  (answer addresses the question)
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Common Failure Modes in LLM Systems}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Weak chunking:} relevant evidence gets split awkwardly or buried in noisy chunks.
    \item \textbf{Poor retrieval recall:} the right source is never found.
    \item \textbf{Poor retrieval precision:} too much irrelevant context is passed to the model.
    \item \textbf{Prompt drift:} instructions become too long, vague, or contradictory.
    \item \textbf{Unvalidated tool output:} the model receives bad or partial structured data and treats it as truth.
    \item \textbf{Overtrusting the model:} product logic assumes the model is authoritative where deterministic checks should own the decision.
\end{itemize}

\begin{examtipbox}
If asked how to improve an LLM application, answer systematically: better retrieval, better chunking, reranking, stricter prompting, structured outputs, tool validation, and evaluation against labeled examples. That is stronger than saying only ``use a better model.''
\end{examtipbox}

\section{Common LLM and RAG Confusions}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{``A better model removes the need for retrieval.''} Usually false when the data is private, changing, or evidence-sensitive.
    \item \textbf{``If the answer sounds fluent, it is probably right.''} Fluency is not proof. Grounding and verification matter.
    \item \textbf{``RAG means the answer is automatically trustworthy.''} Retrieval helps, but irrelevant or weak context still produces weak answers.
    \item \textbf{``Agents are always more advanced and therefore better.''} Simpler pipelines are often more reliable.
    \item \textbf{``Prompt engineering is just wording tricks.''} Good prompting is task design, constraint design, and output-shape design.
\end{itemize}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{LLM systems revision drill:}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Explain the difference between prompting, retrieval, tool use, and verification.
    \item Name one scenario where prompting alone is enough.
    \item Name one scenario where RAG is necessary.
    \item Name one scenario where deterministic business rules should override the model completely.
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercisebox}

\section{AI Ethics and Safety}

As an AI engineer, you have a responsibility to build systems that are safe, fair, and transparent:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Bias:} LLMs can reflect and amplify biases present in training data (gender, racial, cultural). Always test for bias in your outputs.
    \item \textbf{Hallucination:} Never deploy LLM responses without verification for high-stakes applications (medical, legal, financial).
    \item \textbf{Privacy:} Do not send sensitive/personal data to external LLM APIs without consent and proper data handling agreements.
    \item \textbf{Transparency:} Users should know when they are interacting with AI. Label AI-generated content.
    \item \textbf{Guardrails:} Implement content filters, output validation, and rate limiting to prevent misuse.
    \item \textbf{Human oversight:} For critical decisions, AI should assist humans, not replace them. Keep a human in the loop.
\end{itemize}

\begin{cautionbox}
In your RevSync project, ECU firmware modifications can affect vehicle safety. An AI system that recommends or generates tune files \textbf{must} have rigorous verification to ensure tunes are safe and will not damage engines or compromise vehicle control.
\end{cautionbox}

\begin{projectbox}
Your \textbf{VeriRAG} project is a direct implementation of these concepts: it builds a RAG pipeline with claim-level verification to reduce hallucination. The verify-repair loop ensures that generated responses are grounded in source documents, making it suitable for research and high-stakes applications where accuracy is critical.
\end{projectbox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Building with LLMs requires more than prompting; it requires grounding and evaluation.
    \item RAG improves factuality by retrieving relevant context.
    \item Verification, reranking, structured outputs, and tool use make systems more reliable and usable.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Why do LLMs hallucinate?
    \item What does chunking influence in a RAG pipeline?
    \item Why is retrieval quality as important as generation quality?
    \item What is the role of verification in a trustworthy LLM system?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand LLM integration (API calls, prompt engineering patterns, structured output), RAG (retrieval-augmented generation with embeddings, vector databases, and document chunking), verification and hallucination detection, RAG evaluation metrics, and AI ethics. This is directly applicable to your VeriRAG and CareerForge AI projects.}



% CHAPTER 28 — GIT + GITHUB
% ============================================================
\chapter{Git + GitHub}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} understand Git from first principles, not as a list of memorised commands. Git is a history-management system for code. Once you see commits as snapshots and branches as pointers, the workflow becomes much easier to reason about.
\end{studybox}

\section{Core Git Workflow}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={Essential Git commands}]
# Setup
git init                      # initialise repository
git clone <url>               # clone remote repository

# Daily workflow
git status                    # check what's changed
git add .                     # stage all changes
git add file.py               # stage specific file
git commit -m "Add user auth" # commit with message
git push origin main          # push to remote

# Branches
git branch feature/auth       # create branch
git checkout feature/auth     # switch to branch
git checkout -b feature/auth  # create AND switch
git merge feature/auth        # merge into current branch
git branch -d feature/auth    # delete branch

# Viewing history
git log --oneline -10         # last 10 commits, compact
git log --graph --oneline     # visual branch graph
git diff                      # unstaged changes
git diff --staged             # staged changes

# Undoing
git stash                     # temporarily save changes
git stash pop                 # restore stashed changes
git reset --soft HEAD~1       # undo last commit, keep changes
git revert <commit>           # create reverse commit

# Pull requests (GitHub workflow)
# 1. Create feature branch
# 2. Make changes and commit
# 3. Push branch to GitHub
# 4. Open Pull Request on GitHub
# 5. Code review by teammates
# 6. Merge PR
# 7. Delete feature branch
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Mental Model: Snapshots, Not Magic}

Git stores project history as a chain of snapshots.
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item a \textbf{repository} is the full history and working tree
    \item a \textbf{commit} is a named snapshot with a message
    \item a \textbf{branch} is a movable pointer to a line of commits
    \item a \textbf{merge} combines lines of history
\end{itemize}

\begin{intuitionbox}
If Git feels confusing, it is usually because the commands are being learned without the snapshot model. Once you understand that commits are history points and branches are labels pointing at them, the workflow becomes much clearer.
\end{intuitionbox}

This diagram makes that snapshot model visible:

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=1.8cm]
    \node[circle, draw, fill=primaryblue!15, inner sep=2.5pt, label=below:{A}] (a) {};
    \node[circle, draw, fill=primaryblue!15, inner sep=2.5pt, right=of a, label=below:{B}] (b) {};
    \node[circle, draw, fill=primaryblue!15, inner sep=2.5pt, right=of b, label=below:{C}] (c) {};
    \node[circle, draw, fill=tipgreen!15, inner sep=2.5pt, above right=1.1cm and 1.3cm of c, label=above:{D}] (d) {};
    \node[circle, draw, fill=tipgreen!15, inner sep=2.5pt, right=of d, label=above:{E}] (e) {};
    \node[circle, draw, fill=warnyellow!18, inner sep=2.5pt, right=1.8cm of c, label=below:{M}] (m) {};

    \draw[softarrow] (a) -- (b) -- (c);
    \draw[softarrow] (c) -- (d) -- (e);
    \draw[softarrow] (c) -- (m);
    \draw[softarrow] (e) -- (m);

    \node[font=\small, below=0.65cm of c] {main};
    \node[font=\small, above=0.65cm of e] {feature branch};
    \node[font=\small, below=0.65cm of m] {merge commit};
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{A simple branch and merge history}
\label{fig:git-branch-merge}
\end{figure}

Seeing the graph helps because a branch is not a second copy of the project. It is a pointer into history, and merge combines two lines of snapshots into one shared line again.

\section{Working Tree, Staging Area, and Repository}

Many Git mistakes come from not knowing \emph{where} a change currently lives.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Working tree:} the files currently on your machine, including unstaged edits.
    \item \textbf{Staging area (index):} the set of changes you have selected for the next commit.
    \item \textbf{Repository history:} the commits that are already recorded.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={How changes move through Git}]
# 1. Edit files in your working tree
git status

# 2. Stage the changes you want in the next commit
git add app.py

# 3. Confirm what is staged
git diff --staged

# 4. Create a snapshot
git commit -m "Refactor authentication flow"
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{intuitionbox}
Think of the staging area as a draft of your next snapshot. It lets you decide \emph{which} changes belong together. That is why careful engineers do not always use \texttt{git add .} blindly.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{A Real Collaboration Workflow}

In professional teams, Git is not only about storing history. It is about coordinating work safely.

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Pull the latest main branch.
    \item Create a focused feature branch.
    \item Make one coherent change at a time.
    \item Commit in small logical units with clear messages.
    \item Push the branch and open a pull request.
    \item Respond to review comments thoughtfully.
    \item Re-run tests and checks after changes.
    \item Merge only when the branch is understood and stable.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{projectbox}
On a real team, a pull request should answer three questions quickly:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item what problem does this change solve?
    \item what files or behaviours are affected?
    \item what should the reviewer pay special attention to?
\end{enumerate}
\end{projectbox}

\section{Code Review, Rebasing, and Conflict Resolution}

\textbf{Pull requests} are not only a gate to merge code. They are a collaboration tool:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item explain intent
    \item show changes in context
    \item surface risks and edge cases
    \item leave a reviewable decision record
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Rebasing} rewrites commit history so your branch sits on top of a newer base. It can keep history cleaner, but it should be used carefully, especially on shared branches.

\textbf{Conflict resolution} happens when Git cannot automatically reconcile two sets of changes. The right response is not panic. Read both sides, decide the intended final state, edit deliberately, and retest.

\subsection{Merge vs Rebase}

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{|p{0.26\textwidth}|p{0.31\textwidth}|p{0.31\textwidth}|}
\hline
\textbf{Question} & \textbf{Merge} & \textbf{Rebase} \\
\hline
What it does & Combines histories with a merge commit & Replays commits on top of a new base \\
\hline
History shape & Preserves the branch structure & Produces a cleaner linear history \\
\hline
Safety on shared branches & Safer for shared public history & Riskier if history is already shared \\
\hline
When to use & Team branches, preserving context, simple integration & Cleaning local history before opening or updating a PR \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{Merge vs rebase}
\end{table}

\begin{warningbox}
Do not casually rebase commits that other people may already be using. Rebasing rewrites history. It is excellent on your own local branch, but dangerous on shared branches if the team is not aligned.
\end{warningbox}

\section{Safe Undo Strategies}

Beginners often fear Git because they think one wrong command will destroy everything. Git can be dangerous, but there is a safe order of operations if you stay calm.

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Inspect first:} run \texttt{git status}, \texttt{git log --oneline}, and \texttt{git diff}.
    \item \textbf{Prefer reversible actions first:} use \texttt{git restore}, \texttt{git stash}, or \texttt{git revert} before more destructive commands.
    \item \textbf{Use \texttt{git revert} for public history:} it creates a new commit that undoes a previous commit without rewriting shared history.
    \item \textbf{Use reset carefully:} \texttt{--soft}, \texttt{--mixed}, and \texttt{--hard} have very different consequences.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={Safer ways to recover from common mistakes}]
# Unstage a file without losing edits
git restore --staged app.py

# Discard local changes in one file
git restore app.py

# Save unfinished work temporarily
git stash

# Undo a public commit safely
git revert <commit_hash>
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{examtipbox}
In interviews or assessments, saying ``I would check Git status, inspect the diff, and choose the least destructive recovery step'' sounds far stronger than naming random undo commands.
\end{examtipbox}

\section{Best Practices}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Write clear, imperative commit messages: ``Add user authentication'' not ``added stuff''.
    \item Commit often, push regularly.
    \item One feature per branch.
    \item Always use pull requests for team projects.
    \item Never commit secrets (passwords, API keys, \texttt{.env} files).
    \item Add a \texttt{.gitignore} file to exclude build artifacts, \texttt{node\_modules}, \texttt{venv}, etc.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Common Git Mistakes}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item committing too many unrelated changes together
    \item opening large pull requests that are hard to review
    \item writing vague commit messages such as ``fix'' or ``update''
    \item committing generated files or secrets
    \item pulling without understanding whether the team expects merge or rebase
    \item force-pushing shared history carelessly
    \item resolving conflicts without retesting the application afterward
\end{itemize}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Practice drill:} create a small test repository and deliberately practice these operations:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item create a branch
    \item make two commits
    \item edit the same line in two branches to trigger a conflict
    \item resolve the conflict
    \item rebase one branch onto another
    \item revert one commit
\end{enumerate}
The goal is to remove fear by gaining controlled experience.
\end{exercisebox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Git tracks project history through commits and branches.
    \item Pull requests and code reviews are collaboration tools, not just merge rituals.
    \item Rebasing and merging solve different history-management problems.
    \item Good commit hygiene improves debugging, review quality, and release confidence.
    \item Understanding the working tree, staging area, and safe undo strategies makes Git much less intimidating.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What is a commit conceptually?
    \item What problem does branching solve?
    \item Why are pull requests valuable even in small teams?
    \item What should you do when a merge conflict appears?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand Git from both the command level and the conceptual level: commits, branches, merges, rebasing, pull requests, review flow, and conflict resolution. Git is used in every professional software project.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 29 — DOCKER + CI/CD + JENKINS
% ============================================================
\chapter{Docker + CI/CD + Jenkins}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} understand deployment tooling as a chain of reproducibility. Containers make environments consistent. CI validates changes automatically. CD moves tested changes towards production. Jenkins and GitHub Actions are ways of expressing that automation.
\end{studybox}

\section{Docker}

\textbf{Docker} packages your application and all its dependencies into a \textbf{container}---a lightweight, portable, reproducible environment.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={Dockerfile for Django}]
# Dockerfile
FROM python:3.11-slim

WORKDIR /app

# Install dependencies
COPY requirements.txt .
RUN pip install --no-cache-dir -r requirements.txt

# Copy application code
COPY . .

# Collect static files
RUN python manage.py collectstatic --noinput

# Expose port
EXPOSE 8000

# Run the application
CMD ["gunicorn", "revsync.wsgi:application",
     "--bind", "0.0.0.0:8000", "--workers", "3"]
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={Docker Compose for multi-service setup}]
# docker-compose.yml
version: "3.8"
services:
  db:
    image: postgres:15
    environment:
      POSTGRES_DB: revsync
      POSTGRES_PASSWORD: secret
    volumes:
      - pgdata:/var/lib/postgresql/data

  backend:
    build: ./backend
    ports:
      - "8000:8000"
    environment:
      DATABASE_URL: postgres://postgres:secret@db:5432/revsync
    depends_on:
      - db

  redis:
    image: redis:7-alpine

volumes:
  pgdata:
\end{lstlisting}

\section{How to Think About Containers}

\begin{intuitionbox}
A container is not a virtual machine. It is a packaged process with its dependencies, running in isolation on the host operating system. Docker makes the runtime environment easier to reproduce across laptops, CI, and servers.
\end{intuitionbox}

\textbf{Important ideas:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Image:} a built template
    \item \textbf{Container:} a running instance of that image
    \item \textbf{Dockerfile:} the recipe for building the image
    \item \textbf{Compose:} a way to run multiple services together
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Why Docker Helps in Real Projects}

Without containers, developers often fight environment drift:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item one laptop has Python 3.11, another has 3.13
    \item one machine has the right PostgreSQL version, another does not
    \item a package works locally but fails in CI or production
\end{itemize}

Docker reduces that drift by packaging the application together with the runtime assumptions it needs.

\begin{intuitionbox}
The deepest value of Docker is not ``containers are modern.'' The value is \textbf{reproducibility}. If the same image runs in development, CI, staging, and production, debugging becomes much more honest.
\end{intuitionbox}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=1.6cm]
    \node[warnbox, minimum width=11.6cm, minimum height=5cm, label=above:{Host machine}] (host) {};
    \node[flowbox] (docker) at ($(host.north)+(0,-1.0)$) {Docker engine};
    \node[storagebox, below left=1.4cm and 2.6cm of docker] (app) {app\\container};
    \node[storagebox, below=1.4cm of docker] (db) {database\\container};
    \node[storagebox, below right=1.4cm and 2.6cm of docker] (cache) {cache / queue\\container};

    \draw[flowarrow] (docker) -- (app);
    \draw[flowarrow] (docker) -- (db);
    \draw[flowarrow] (docker) -- (cache);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{A simple multi-container application on one host}
\label{fig:docker-layout}
\end{figure}

This picture matters because it separates three things learners often blur together: the host machine, the Docker engine, and the individual containers that run separate services.

\section{Dockerfile Thinking and Image Layers}

Each Dockerfile instruction creates a \textbf{layer}. Layers are cached, so ordering matters.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={Layer-aware Dockerfile thinking}]
FROM python:3.11-slim

WORKDIR /app

# Copy dependency file first so dependency install can be cached
COPY requirements.txt .
RUN pip install --no-cache-dir -r requirements.txt

# Copy application code after dependencies
COPY . .
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Why this order matters:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item if only your code changes, Docker can reuse the dependency-install layer
    \item if you copy the whole project first, small code edits may force a full rebuild
\end{itemize}

\begin{warningbox}
Do not treat Dockerfiles as random shell scripts. Poor ordering leads to slow builds, large images, and more painful CI runs.
\end{warningbox}

\section{Development vs Production Containers}

The same application usually needs slightly different behaviour in development and production.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{|p{0.26\textwidth}|p{0.31\textwidth}|p{0.31\textwidth}|}
\hline
\textbf{Concern} & \textbf{Development} & \textbf{Production} \\
\hline
Code changes & live reload / mounted volume & immutable built image \\
\hline
Logs & verbose, easy to inspect & structured and centralised \\
\hline
Security & speed and convenience matter more & least privilege and smaller images matter more \\
\hline
Debug tools & shell access is often acceptable & extra tools should be minimized \\
\hline
Performance & local feedback is priority & predictable, stable runtime is priority \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{Development vs production containers}
\end{table}

\textbf{Production container habits:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item keep images small
    \item avoid running as root if possible
    \item pass config through environment variables
    \item do not bake secrets into images
    \item use explicit image tags rather than ambiguous ``latest''
\end{itemize}

\section{CI/CD}

\textbf{CI (Continuous Integration):} Automatically run tests every time code is pushed.

\textbf{CD (Continuous Delivery/Deployment):} Automatically deploy tested code to production.

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={GitHub Actions CI pipeline}]
# .github/workflows/ci.yml
name: CI Pipeline
on: [push, pull_request]
jobs:
  test:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - uses: actions/setup-python@v5
        with:
          python-version: "3.11"
      - run: pip install -r requirements.txt
      - run: python manage.py test
      - run: flake8 .
\end{lstlisting}

\section{How to Think About a CI Pipeline}

A healthy CI pipeline is a quality gate, not just a ceremony.

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Install dependencies} in a reproducible way.
    \item \textbf{Run fast checks first:} formatting, linting, static analysis.
    \item \textbf{Run tests:} unit tests first, then heavier integration tests.
    \item \textbf{Build artifacts:} Docker image, compiled frontend, packages.
    \item \textbf{Publish or deploy} only if the earlier stages pass.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{examtipbox}
If asked what CI should run, answer in layers: style checks, static analysis, unit tests, integration tests, build, then deployment. That sounds much stronger than ``run the tests.''
\end{examtipbox}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=1.15cm]
    \node[databox] (push) {Git push\\or PR};
    \node[flowbox, right=of push] (lint) {Lint / format};
    \node[flowbox, right=of lint] (tests) {Tests};
    \node[flowbox, right=of tests] (build) {Build};
    \node[storagebox, right=of build] (staging) {Staging};
    \node[databox, right=of staging] (prod) {Production};
    \node[warnbox, below=1.4cm of staging] (obs) {Metrics, alerts,\\rollback};

    \draw[flowarrow] (push) -- (lint);
    \draw[flowarrow] (lint) -- (tests);
    \draw[flowarrow] (tests) -- (build);
    \draw[flowarrow] (build) -- (staging);
    \draw[flowarrow] (staging) -- (prod);
    \draw[softarrow] (staging) -- (obs);
    \draw[softarrow] (prod) -- (obs);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{A practical CI/CD pipeline}
\label{fig:cicd-flow}
\end{figure}

The point of this flow is not decoration. It shows deployment as a sequence of confidence-building checks rather than one risky jump from laptop to production.

\subsection{Continuous Delivery vs Continuous Deployment}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Continuous Delivery:} every change that passes checks is ready to deploy, but a human may still press the final button.
    \item \textbf{Continuous Deployment:} every passing change automatically goes live without manual approval.
\end{itemize}

\begin{warningbox}
Do not confuse ``automated'' with ``safe.'' Safety comes from tests, rollback plans, observability, and release discipline, not from automation alone.
\end{warningbox}

\section{Jenkins}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Open-source automation server for CI/CD.
    \item Uses \textbf{Jenkinsfile} (pipeline as code) or UI-based configuration.
    \item Supports \textbf{stages}: Build $\to$ Test $\to$ Deploy.
    \item Integrates with Docker, Git, AWS, Kubernetes.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={Conceptual Jenkins pipeline}]
pipeline {
  agent any
  stages {
    stage('Install') {
      steps { sh 'pip install -r requirements.txt' }
    }
    stage('Test') {
      steps { sh 'pytest' }
    }
    stage('Build Image') {
      steps { sh 'docker build -t myapp:${BUILD_NUMBER} .' }
    }
    stage('Deploy') {
      steps { sh './deploy.sh' }
    }
  }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Deployment Strategies and Rollback Thinking}

Shipping code is not only about getting a new version out. It is also about recovering safely if something goes wrong.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Rolling deployment:} replace instances gradually.
    \item \textbf{Blue-green deployment:} keep old and new environments side by side, then switch traffic.
    \item \textbf{Canary deployment:} send a small percentage of traffic to the new version first.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Rollback readiness means:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item the previous version is still available
    \item migrations are planned carefully
    \item metrics and alerts can detect a bad release quickly
    \item deployment is observable, not blind
\end{itemize}

\section{Secrets, Environments, and Deployment Hygiene}

Serious delivery pipelines also need:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item environment variables for configuration
    \item secret storage for API keys and credentials
    \item separate dev, staging, and production environments
    \item clear rollback strategies if deployment fails
\end{itemize}

\section{Common Container and CI/CD Mistakes}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item building huge images with unnecessary tools and files
    \item storing secrets in Dockerfiles, images, or source control
    \item making CI slow enough that the team stops trusting it
    \item running only unit tests and assuming deployment is therefore safe
    \item having no staging environment or rollback plan
    \item using ``works on my machine'' as a substitute for reproducible builds
\end{itemize}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Practice task:} take one of your projects and define:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item a Dockerfile
    \item a Compose file for local dependencies
    \item a CI pipeline with lint + test + build
    \item one deployment strategy and one rollback strategy
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercisebox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Docker improves environment consistency.
    \item Compose helps you run multiple dependent services together.
    \item CI checks quality automatically; CD moves validated code toward deployment.
    \item Jenkins and GitHub Actions are both ways to express build-test-deploy workflows.
    \item Real deployment systems also require careful secret and environment management.
    \item Strong delivery pipelines are built around reproducibility, visibility, and safe rollback.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What is the difference between an image and a container?
    \item Why is CI valuable even before a product has many users?
    \item What problem does Docker Compose solve?
    \item Why should secrets never be hard-coded into pipelines?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand Docker (images, containers, Dockerfiles, Compose), CI/CD (GitHub Actions, automated testing/deployment), Jenkins basics, and the role of secrets and environment management in delivery pipelines.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 30 — AWS + KUBERNETES BASICS
% ============================================================
\chapter{AWS + Kubernetes Basics}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} build cloud intuition rather than memorising service names. AWS gives you managed building blocks. Kubernetes gives you a model for running and scaling containers. You do not need to master every feature; you need to understand what problems these tools solve.
\end{studybox}

\section{AWS Core Services}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{EC2:} Virtual servers in the cloud. Run your Django app here.
    \item \textbf{S3:} Object storage (files, images, tune files, backups).
    \item \textbf{RDS:} Managed relational databases (PostgreSQL, MySQL).
    \item \textbf{Lambda:} Serverless functions (run code without managing servers).
    \item \textbf{CloudFront:} CDN (Content Delivery Network) for fast global content delivery.
    \item \textbf{IAM:} Identity and Access Management (users, roles, permissions).
    \item \textbf{VPC:} Virtual Private Cloud (network isolation).
    \item \textbf{ECS/EKS:} Container orchestration (run Docker containers at scale).
\end{itemize}

\section{A Practical AWS Mental Model}

AWS can feel overwhelming because there are so many services. A better way to learn it is by grouping services by the kind of problem they solve:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Compute:} EC2, Lambda, ECS, EKS
    \item \textbf{Storage:} S3, EBS, EFS
    \item \textbf{Databases:} RDS, DynamoDB, ElastiCache
    \item \textbf{Networking:} VPC, subnets, route tables, load balancers, CloudFront
    \item \textbf{Security and identity:} IAM, Security Groups, KMS, Secrets Manager
    \item \textbf{Observability:} CloudWatch, logs, alarms, tracing tools
\end{itemize}

\begin{intuitionbox}
Cloud architecture is easier once you stop memorising product names and start asking: where does code run, where is data stored, how does traffic enter, who is allowed to access what, and how do we observe failures?
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{A Typical Cloud Layout}

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item users hit a domain name
    \item DNS routes traffic to a load balancer
    \item the load balancer forwards requests to app servers or containers
    \item the application talks to a managed database, cache, and object storage
    \item logs, metrics, and alerts tell the team whether the system is healthy
\end{enumerate}

\begin{projectbox}
For a product like RevSync, a realistic early cloud setup could be:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item EC2 or ECS for the backend API
    \item RDS PostgreSQL for relational data
    \item S3 for tune packages, manifests, and backups
    \item ElastiCache/Redis for caching and queues
    \item CloudFront for static asset delivery
    \item IAM roles and Secrets Manager for controlled access
\end{itemize}
\end{projectbox}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=1.25cm]
    \node[databox] (users) {Users};
    \node[flowbox, right=of users] (dns) {DNS / CDN};
    \node[flowbox, right=of dns] (lb) {Load balancer};
    \node[storagebox, right=of lb] (app) {App servers\\or containers};
    \node[storagebox, below left=1.4cm and 1.1cm of app] (db) {RDS};
    \node[storagebox, below=1.4cm of app] (cache) {Redis};
    \node[storagebox, below right=1.4cm and 1.1cm of app] (s3) {S3};

    \draw[flowarrow] (users) -- (dns);
    \draw[flowarrow] (dns) -- (lb);
    \draw[flowarrow] (lb) -- (app);
    \draw[softarrow] (app) -- (db);
    \draw[softarrow] (app) -- (cache);
    \draw[softarrow] (app) -- (s3);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{A typical cloud application layout}
\label{fig:cloud-layout}
\end{figure}

This picture reduces cloud overload by showing the core path first: traffic enters through networking components, reaches compute, and compute talks to storage and stateful services.

\section{Kubernetes (K8s) Basics}

Kubernetes automates deployment, scaling, and management of containerised applications.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Pod:} Smallest deployable unit. One or more containers running together.
    \item \textbf{Deployment:} Declarative updates for pods. Ensures desired number of replicas.
    \item \textbf{Service:} Network endpoint that exposes pods (load balancing).
    \item \textbf{ConfigMap:} Non-sensitive configuration data.
    \item \textbf{Secret:} Sensitive configuration data (passwords, tokens).
    \item \textbf{Ingress:} HTTP routing rules (like a reverse proxy).
\end{itemize}

\section{How Kubernetes Objects Relate}

Kubernetes becomes much easier when you see the relationships clearly:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item a \textbf{Pod} runs containers
    \item a \textbf{Deployment} manages pods and keeps the desired replica count alive
    \item a \textbf{Service} gives stable networking to a changing set of pods
    \item an \textbf{Ingress} exposes HTTP routes into the cluster
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={Conceptual Kubernetes flow}]
Internet -> Ingress -> Service -> Pods managed by Deployment
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=yaml, caption={Minimal Kubernetes deployment example}]
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: api-deployment
spec:
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: api
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: api
    spec:
      containers:
        - name: api
          image: myapp:1.0.0
          ports:
            - containerPort: 8000
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{notebox}
Kubernetes is declarative. You usually tell it the desired state, such as ``three replicas of this image,'' and the control plane works to maintain that state.
\end{notebox}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=1.2cm]
    \node[databox] (ing) {Ingress};
    \node[flowbox, right=of ing] (svc) {Service};
    \node[storagebox, right=of svc] (dep) {Deployment};
    \node[flowbox, below right=1.2cm and 0.8cm of dep] (pod1) {Pod 1};
    \node[flowbox, above right=1.2cm and 0.8cm of dep] (pod2) {Pod 2};
    \node[flowbox, right=of pod2] (pod3) {Pod 3};
    \node[warnbox, below=1.4cm of svc] (cfg) {ConfigMap /\\Secret};

    \draw[flowarrow] (ing) -- (svc);
    \draw[flowarrow] (svc) -- (dep);
    \draw[softarrow] (dep) -- (pod1);
    \draw[softarrow] (dep) -- (pod2);
    \draw[softarrow] (dep) -- (pod3);
    \draw[softarrow] (cfg) -- (pod1);
    \draw[softarrow] (cfg) -- (pod2);
    \draw[softarrow] (cfg) -- (pod3);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{How core Kubernetes objects relate}
\label{fig:kubernetes-objects}
\end{figure}

What you are looking at here is the operational structure of a typical Kubernetes app: ingress handles incoming HTTP, the service gives stable networking, the deployment manages replicas, and pods run the actual containers with configuration injected from config objects.

\section{Containers, Load Balancers, and Production Placement}

In a typical production system:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item a domain points to a load balancer
    \item the load balancer forwards traffic to application instances or pods
    \item those instances talk to databases, caches, queues, and object storage
    \item metrics, logs, and alerts provide \textbf{observability}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Observability} means making a system understandable from the outside through logs, metrics, traces, and alerts.

\section{Container Orchestration: When Kubernetes Helps}

You do not need Kubernetes for every project. It becomes more useful when:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item you run many services
    \item you need self-healing and automatic restarts
    \item you want rolling deployments and service discovery
    \item you need stronger scheduling and scaling across many nodes
\end{itemize}

\textbf{It may be overkill when:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item you have one or two services only
    \item the team is small and operations time is limited
    \item ECS, Docker Compose, or a simple VM setup would solve the problem more cheaply
\end{itemize}

\section{Common Cloud and Kubernetes Mistakes}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item exposing services publicly when they should stay private
    \item granting overly broad IAM permissions
    \item confusing containers with persistent storage
    \item deploying without health checks, probes, or alerts
    \item choosing Kubernetes because it sounds impressive rather than because it is needed
    \item ignoring cost while scaling architecture complexity
\end{itemize}

\begin{examtipbox}
When asked about cloud or Kubernetes, avoid listing tool names without purpose. Always connect each service to a problem: storage, compute, networking, isolation, scaling, configuration, or observability.
\end{examtipbox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item AWS is best understood as a set of managed building blocks for compute, storage, networking, security, and observability.
    \item Kubernetes manages containerized workloads declaratively through pods, deployments, services, and ingress.
    \item Good cloud design connects components to concrete problems rather than listing services at random.
    \item Production readiness depends on permissions, health checks, rollout discipline, and observability.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What different problems do EC2, S3, and RDS solve?
    \item What is the relationship between a pod, a deployment, and a service?
    \item When is Kubernetes helpful, and when is it unnecessary complexity?
    \item Why are IAM design and observability as important as raw compute?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Architecture drill:} design a simple cloud deployment for one of your projects. Specify:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item where the frontend runs
    \item where the backend runs
    \item where files are stored
    \item where the database lives
    \item how traffic reaches the app
    \item how logs and alerts will be collected
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercisebox}

\checkpoint{You now understand the cloud and orchestration mental model: AWS services grouped by purpose, how traffic and data flow through a typical cloud system, how Kubernetes objects relate, when orchestration is valuable, and which operational mistakes to avoid.}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item AWS provides managed cloud building blocks such as compute, storage, networking, identity, and databases.
    \item Kubernetes orchestrates containerised workloads through pods, deployments, services, and ingress.
    \item Production systems also need observability, configuration management, and careful traffic routing.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What is the role of EC2, S3, and RDS?
    \item What does a Kubernetes Deployment manage?
    \item What is the difference between a Secret and a ConfigMap?
    \item Why is observability a production requirement rather than an optional extra?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand AWS core services, Kubernetes concepts, load-balancing placement, configuration, and observability. These are the foundations of production cloud deployment.}


% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 31 — SECURITY FUNDAMENTALS
% ============================================================
\chapter{Security Fundamentals}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{How to study this chapter:} do not rush through the vulnerability list. For each risk, ask four things: what is the attack, why does it work, what damage can it cause, and what specific coding or infrastructure practice prevents it?
\end{studybox}

\begin{notebox}
\textbf{A useful reading pattern for security:} trace every issue as a path. Start with an input, follow the trust boundary it crosses, identify the unsafe assumption, then name the damage and the defensive control. This makes security easier to remember because it becomes causal rather than abstract.
\end{notebox}

\section{Security Mindset}

\textbf{The first rule of security:} Never trust user input. Every piece of data that comes from outside your system---form fields, URL parameters, headers, file uploads, API requests---must be treated as potentially malicious.

\textbf{Defence in depth:} Security is not a single wall but multiple layers. If one layer fails, the next should catch the attack. Implement security at every level: input validation, authentication, authorisation, encryption, monitoring.

\begin{intuitionbox}
Security is really the discipline of asking, ``What happens if someone uses this system in the worst possible way?'' Good security engineering does not assume users, networks, or clients behave ideally.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{A Repeatable Security Thinking Loop}

When you meet a new system, run through this sequence:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Identify the asset.} What are you protecting: passwords, money, tokens, firmware, personal data?
    \item \textbf{Identify the entry point.} Which form, endpoint, file upload, cookie, or service accepts external input?
    \item \textbf{Identify the trust mistake.} What is the system assuming without proof?
    \item \textbf{Identify the impact.} Could the attacker read, change, delete, impersonate, escalate, or disrupt?
    \item \textbf{Identify the control.} What concrete mechanism blocks or limits the attack?
    \item \textbf{Identify the evidence.} If the attack happens anyway, what will you log, alert on, or audit?
\end{enumerate}

\begin{examtipbox}
In exams and interviews, security answers become much stronger when you explain \emph{attack path + impact + control}, not just a list of buzzwords.
\end{examtipbox}

\section{OWASP Top 10 Vulnerabilities}

The \textbf{OWASP Top 10} is the most widely referenced list of web application security risks. Every developer must know these:

\subsection{1. Injection (SQL Injection, Command Injection)}

\textbf{What it is:} An attacker inserts malicious code into queries or commands by manipulating user input.

\textbf{Example of vulnerable code:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={SQL Injection: vulnerable vs.\,safe}]
# VULNERABLE -- NEVER DO THIS
query = f"SELECT * FROM users WHERE email = '{email}'"
# If email = "'; DROP TABLE users; --"
# The query becomes: SELECT * FROM users WHERE email = '';
#                    DROP TABLE users; --'
# YOUR ENTIRE USERS TABLE IS DELETED.

# SAFE -- Always use parameterised queries
cursor.execute(
    "SELECT * FROM users WHERE email = %s",
    (email,)  # parameter passed separately, NEVER concatenated
)

# SAFE -- Django ORM (always parameterised internally)
User.objects.filter(email=email)

# SAFE -- SQLAlchemy
session.query(User).filter_by(email=email).first()
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{2. Broken Authentication}

\textbf{What it is:} Weak passwords, exposed tokens, session hijacking, no brute-force protection.

\textbf{Prevention:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Hash passwords with \textbf{bcrypt} or \textbf{argon2} (never MD5 or SHA-256 without salt).
    \item Use short-lived access tokens (15 min) + longer refresh tokens (7 days).
    \item Implement account lockout after failed attempts.
    \item Use multi-factor authentication (MFA) for sensitive operations.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Password hashing with bcrypt}]
# pip install bcrypt
import bcrypt

# Hash a password (during registration)
password = "my_secure_password_123"
salt = bcrypt.gensalt(rounds=12)  # 12 rounds = good balance
hashed = bcrypt.hashpw(password.encode("utf-8"), salt)
# Store 'hashed' in your database. NEVER store plain password.

# Verify a password (during login)
input_password = "my_secure_password_123"
is_valid = bcrypt.checkpw(
    input_password.encode("utf-8"), hashed
)
print(f"Password valid: {is_valid}")  # True
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Password hashing in Node.js}]
const bcrypt = require("bcrypt");

// Hash
const hash = await bcrypt.hash("my_password", 12);

// Verify
const isValid = await bcrypt.compare("my_password", hash);
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{3. Cross-Site Scripting (XSS)}

\textbf{What it is:} An attacker injects malicious JavaScript into pages viewed by other users, stealing cookies, sessions, or personal data.

\textbf{Types:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Stored XSS:} Malicious script is saved in the database (e.g., in a comment) and executed when others view it.
    \item \textbf{Reflected XSS:} Malicious script is in a URL parameter and reflected back to the user.
    \item \textbf{DOM-based XSS:} JavaScript on the page processes user input unsafely.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Prevention:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Escape all output.} Django does this automatically in templates. React does this automatically with JSX.
    \item \textbf{Never use} \texttt{dangerouslySetInnerHTML} in React (unless you sanitise first).
    \item \textbf{Set Content Security Policy (CSP) headers} to restrict where scripts can load from.
    \item \textbf{Use HttpOnly cookies} for tokens (JavaScript cannot read them).
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={XSS prevention: sanitising user input}]
# pip install bleach
import bleach

def sanitize_html(user_input):
    return bleach.clean(
        user_input,
        tags=["b", "i", "u", "a", "p", "br"],  # allowed tags
        attributes={"a": ["href", "title"]},
        strip=True  # remove disallowed tags entirely
    )

# Example
dirty = '<script>alert("XSS")</script><b>Hello</b>'
clean = sanitize_html(dirty)
print(clean)  # '<b>Hello</b>' -- script tag removed
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{4. Cross-Site Request Forgery (CSRF)}

\textbf{What it is:} An attacker tricks a logged-in user into making unintended requests to your server (e.g., transferring money, changing email) by embedding requests in malicious pages.

\textbf{Prevention:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Use \textbf{CSRF tokens} in forms. Django includes \texttt{\{% csrf\_token \%\}} automatically.
    \item Set \textbf{SameSite=Strict} or \textbf{SameSite=Lax} on cookies.
    \item Verify \textbf{Origin/Referer} headers on state-changing requests.
    \item For APIs: use Bearer tokens instead of cookies (tokens are not sent automatically).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{5. Security Misconfiguration}

\textbf{What it is:} Default credentials, unnecessary features enabled, verbose error messages in production, missing security headers.

\textbf{Prevention:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Set \textbf{DEBUG = False} in production (Django).
    \item Set \texttt{NODE\_ENV=production} (Node.js).
    \item Remove default admin accounts and change default passwords.
    \item Disable directory listing on web servers.
    \item Return generic error messages to users (log details server-side).
\end{itemize}

\section{JWT (JSON Web Token) Security}

The figure below separates authentication from authorization in one secure login flow. This helps because learners often blur ``who are you?'' and ``are you allowed to do this?'' into one step.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=1.15cm]
    \node[databox] (login) {Login request};
    \node[flowbox, right=of login] (verify) {Verify password\\hash};
    \node[storagebox, right=of verify] (tokens) {Issue access +\\refresh tokens};
    \node[flowbox, right=of tokens] (api) {Protected API\\request};
    \node[warnbox, right=of api] (authz) {Authorization\\check};
    \node[databox, right=of authz] (result) {Allow or deny};

    \draw[flowarrow] (login) -- (verify);
    \draw[flowarrow] (verify) -- (tokens);
    \draw[flowarrow] (tokens) -- (api);
    \draw[flowarrow] (api) -- (authz);
    \draw[flowarrow] (authz) -- (result);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{A secure login and protected-request flow}
\label{fig:secure-login-flow}
\end{figure}

What you are looking at is two different decisions: authentication proves identity, then authorization checks whether that identity may perform the requested action.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Secure JWT implementation in Django}]
# settings.py -- Django Simple JWT configuration
from datetime import timedelta

SIMPLE_JWT = {
    "ACCESS_TOKEN_LIFETIME": timedelta(minutes=15),  # short-lived!
    "REFRESH_TOKEN_LIFETIME": timedelta(days=7),
    "ROTATE_REFRESH_TOKENS": True,   # new refresh token each time
    "BLACKLIST_AFTER_ROTATION": True, # old refresh token invalidated
    "ALGORITHM": "HS256",
    "SIGNING_KEY": SECRET_KEY,  # use a strong, unique secret
    "AUTH_HEADER_TYPES": ("Bearer",),
}
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Secure JWT in Node.js}]
const jwt = require("jsonwebtoken");

// Generate tokens
function generateTokens(userId) {
    const accessToken = jwt.sign(
        { sub: userId, type: "access" },
        process.env.JWT_ACCESS_SECRET,
        { expiresIn: "15m" }  // short-lived
    );
    const refreshToken = jwt.sign(
        { sub: userId, type: "refresh" },
        process.env.JWT_REFRESH_SECRET,  // different secret!
        { expiresIn: "7d" }
    );
    return { accessToken, refreshToken };
}

// Verify token middleware
function authMiddleware(req, res, next) {
    const token = req.headers.authorization?.split(" ")[1];
    if (!token) return res.status(401).json({ error: "No token" });
    try {
        const decoded = jwt.verify(token, process.env.JWT_ACCESS_SECRET);
        if (decoded.type !== "access") throw new Error("Wrong token type");
        req.user = decoded;
        next();
    } catch (err) {
        return res.status(401).json({ error: "Invalid or expired token" });
    }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Secure Headers}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Security headers (Django middleware)}]
# Django: use django-csp and SecurityMiddleware
MIDDLEWARE = [
    "django.middleware.security.SecurityMiddleware",
    # ...
]

# settings.py
SECURE_BROWSER_XSS_FILTER = True
SECURE_CONTENT_TYPE_NOSNIFF = True
SECURE_SSL_REDIRECT = True  # redirect HTTP to HTTPS
SESSION_COOKIE_SECURE = True  # only send cookies over HTTPS
CSRF_COOKIE_SECURE = True
SESSION_COOKIE_HTTPONLY = True  # JS cannot access session cookie
X_FRAME_OPTIONS = "DENY"  # prevent clickjacking
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Security headers (Express with helmet)}]
// npm install helmet
const helmet = require("helmet");
app.use(helmet());  // sets 11+ security headers automatically

// Includes: X-Content-Type-Options, X-Frame-Options,
// Strict-Transport-Security, Content-Security-Policy, etc.
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Rate Limiting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Rate limiting in Express}]
// npm install express-rate-limit
const rateLimit = require("express-rate-limit");

// General API rate limit
const apiLimiter = rateLimit({
    windowMs: 15 * 60 * 1000, // 15 minutes
    max: 100,                  // 100 requests per window
    message: { error: "Too many requests, try again later" }
});
app.use("/api/", apiLimiter);

// Stricter limit for auth endpoints (prevent brute force)
const authLimiter = rateLimit({
    windowMs: 15 * 60 * 1000,
    max: 5,  // only 5 login attempts per 15 minutes
    message: { error: "Too many login attempts" }
});
app.use("/api/auth/", authLimiter);
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Security Best Practices Checklist}

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Hash passwords} with bcrypt/argon2 (never store plain text or use MD5/SHA).
    \item Use \textbf{HTTPS everywhere} (TLS/SSL). Never transmit credentials over HTTP.
    \item Store secrets in \textbf{environment variables}, never in code or version control.
    \item Apply \textbf{least privilege}: give minimum permissions needed.
    \item Validate and sanitise \textbf{all user input} server-side (not just client-side).
    \item Set \textbf{secure cookie flags}: HttpOnly, Secure, SameSite.
    \item Use \textbf{parameterised queries} always (Django ORM does this automatically).
    \item Keep dependencies \textbf{updated} (\texttt{npm audit}, \texttt{pip-audit}, Dependabot).
    \item Implement \textbf{rate limiting} on all API endpoints.
    \item Log security events for \textbf{auditing} (failed logins, permission denials).
    \item Use \textbf{CORS} properly---never set \texttt{Access-Control-Allow-Origin: *} in production.
    \item \textbf{Rotate secrets} regularly (API keys, JWT secrets, database passwords).
\end{enumerate}

\section{Common Security Thinking Mistakes}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Trusting frontend validation.} Client-side checks improve UX, not security.
    \item \textbf{Thinking auth is enough without authorization.} Knowing who a user is does not prove they may perform an action.
    \item \textbf{Adding one control and assuming the problem is solved.} Security needs layers because real systems fail imperfectly.
    \item \textbf{Ignoring logs and monitoring.} A secure design also needs detection and traceability.
    \item \textbf{Treating security as a later polish pass.} Many security problems come from architecture and defaults, not just one bad line of code.
\end{itemize}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Security reasoning drill:}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Pick one endpoint from a project you know.
    \item Name the input it accepts.
    \item State one likely abuse case.
    \item State one preventive control and one monitoring signal.
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercisebox}

\begin{cautionbox}
Security is not optional. A single vulnerability can expose all your users' data. Your RevSync project handles ECU firmware---a security breach could have real-world physical consequences. Treat every line of security code as critical.
\end{cautionbox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Security starts with a mindset: distrust inputs, limit permissions, and assume failure paths matter.
    \item OWASP vulnerabilities are practical engineering problems, not abstract theory.
    \item Parameterized queries, escaping, CSRF protection, secure headers, rate limiting, and secret management are everyday controls.
    \item A secure system needs multiple layers so that one missed check does not become a total compromise.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Why does SQL injection happen?
    \item What is the practical difference between XSS and CSRF?
    \item Why should refresh tokens and access tokens have different lifetimes?
    \item What does ``defence in depth'' mean in a real backend?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand security deeply: OWASP Top 10 vulnerabilities (SQL injection, XSS, CSRF, broken auth, misconfiguration), password hashing with bcrypt, JWT security, secure headers, rate limiting, CORS, and security best practices. Apply these to every project you build.}


\section{Chapter Review: Security}

\subsection{Key Takeaways}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Never trust user input.} Validate, sanitise, and escape everything.
    \item SQL injection is prevented by \textbf{parameterised queries} (never concatenate user input into SQL).
    \item XSS is prevented by \textbf{escaping output} (React and Django do this automatically).
    \item Always hash passwords with \textbf{bcrypt} or \textbf{argon2}.
    \item Use \textbf{short-lived access tokens} (15 min) + \textbf{refresh tokens} (7 days) for JWT auth.
    \item Set \textbf{secure headers} on every response (use helmet in Express, SecurityMiddleware in Django).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Quick Recall Questions}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What is the difference between authentication and authorisation?
    \item Write a parameterised SQL query that prevents SQL injection.
    \item What are the three types of XSS? How do you prevent each?
    \item Why should you never use MD5 or SHA-256 alone for password hashing?
    \item What is CSRF and how do CSRF tokens prevent it?
    \item What does the HttpOnly cookie flag do?
\end{enumerate}




% CHAPTER 32 — TESTING + DEBUGGING + DOCUMENTATION
% ============================================================
\chapter{Testing + Debugging + Documentation}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{How to study this chapter:} treat it as a day-to-day engineering workflow chapter. These ideas matter most when code stops being a toy and starts being something other people rely on.
\end{studybox}

\begin{intuitionbox}
Testing, debugging, and documentation are all feedback systems. They help you answer three questions repeatedly: ``Does it work?'', ``Why did it fail?'', and ``Can someone else understand or trust it?''
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{How to Study This Chapter Without Getting Lost}

This chapter contains multiple workflows rather than one single concept. A useful order is:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item learn what a good test is
    \item learn the difference between unit, integration, and end-to-end tests
    \item learn how failures guide debugging
    \item learn how documentation preserves understanding over time
\end{enumerate}

\begin{notebox}
If you have ever written code that ``worked yesterday'' and then broke after a change, this chapter explains how professionals reduce that pain.
\end{notebox}

\section{Why Testing Matters}

\textbf{Testing} is how you prove your code works correctly and continues working as you make changes. Without tests, every code change is a gamble---you might break something without knowing.

\textbf{Types of tests (the testing pyramid):}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Unit tests (base of pyramid):} Test individual functions/methods in isolation. Fast, numerous, cheap. Should be the majority of your tests.
    \item \textbf{Integration tests (middle):} Test how components work together (e.g., API endpoint + database + authentication). Slower, fewer.
    \item \textbf{End-to-end (E2E) tests (top):} Test complete user workflows (e.g., login $\to$ browse $\to$ purchase). Slowest, fewest, most fragile.
\end{itemize}

\begin{notebox}
\textbf{The testing pyramid rule:} Write many unit tests, some integration tests, and few E2E tests. If E2E tests are the majority, your test suite will be slow and fragile.
\end{notebox}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}
    \draw[fill=primaryblue!10, draw=primaryblue!70] (0,0) -- (7,0) -- (6,1.2) -- (1,1.2) -- cycle;
    \draw[fill=tipgreen!10, draw=tipgreen!70!black] (1,1.2) -- (6,1.2) -- (4.8,2.4) -- (2.2,2.4) -- cycle;
    \draw[fill=warnyellow!15, draw=warnyellow!80!black] (2.2,2.4) -- (4.8,2.4) -- (4,3.5) -- (3,3.5) -- cycle;

    \node at (3.5,0.6) {\small Many unit tests};
    \node at (3.5,1.8) {\small Some integration tests};
    \node at (3.5,2.95) {\small Few end-to-end tests};
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{The testing pyramid}
\label{fig:testing-pyramid}
\end{figure}

This visual matters because it teaches test strategy at a glance: the faster and more focused tests should dominate the suite, while full user-flow tests should stay selective.

\section{Testing in Python (pytest)}

\textbf{pytest} is the standard Python testing framework. It is simpler and more powerful than the built-in \texttt{unittest}.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={pytest essentials}]
# test_calculator.py
import pytest

# ---- BASIC TESTS ----
def test_addition():
    assert 1 + 1 == 2

def test_string_upper():
    assert "hello".upper() == "HELLO"

def test_list_contains():
    fruits = ["apple", "banana", "cherry"]
    assert "banana" in fruits

# ---- TESTING FOR EXCEPTIONS ----
def test_division_by_zero():
    with pytest.raises(ZeroDivisionError):
        1 / 0

def test_invalid_input():
    with pytest.raises(ValueError, match="negative"):
        withdraw(-100)

# ---- FIXTURES (reusable test setup) ----
@pytest.fixture
def sample_user():
    return {"name": "Amzat", "email": "amzat@example.com", "age": 24}

@pytest.fixture
def db_connection():
    conn = create_connection()
    yield conn  # test runs here
    conn.close()  # cleanup after test

def test_user_name(sample_user):
    assert sample_user["name"] == "Amzat"

def test_user_email(sample_user):
    assert "@" in sample_user["email"]

# ---- PARAMETERISED TESTS (test multiple inputs) ----
@pytest.mark.parametrize("input,expected", [
    (2, 4),
    (3, 9),
    (4, 16),
    (-1, 1),
    (0, 0),
])
def test_square(input, expected):
    assert input ** 2 == expected
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=bash, caption={Running pytest}]
# Run all tests
pytest

# Run with verbose output
pytest -v

# Run specific file
pytest test_calculator.py

# Run specific test
pytest test_calculator.py::test_addition

# Run with coverage report
pytest --cov=myapp --cov-report=html

# Run only tests marked as "slow"
pytest -m slow

# Stop on first failure
pytest -x
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Testing Django APIs}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Django REST Framework testing}]
from rest_framework.test import APITestCase, APIClient
from rest_framework import status
from django.contrib.auth import get_user_model

User = get_user_model()

class ProductAPITest(APITestCase):
    def setUp(self):
        # Runs before every test method
        self.user = User.objects.create_user(
            username="testuser", password="testpass123"
        )
        self.client = APIClient()
        self.client.force_authenticate(user=self.user)

    def test_list_products(self):
        response = self.client.get("/api/products/")
        self.assertEqual(response.status_code, status.HTTP_200_OK)
        self.assertIsInstance(response.data, list)

    def test_create_product(self):
        data = {
            "title": "Test Tune",
            "price": "99.99",
            "category": "electronics"
        }
        response = self.client.post("/api/products/", data)
        self.assertEqual(response.status_code, status.HTTP_201_CREATED)
        self.assertEqual(response.data["title"], "Test Tune")

    def test_create_product_unauthenticated(self):
        self.client.force_authenticate(user=None)
        response = self.client.post("/api/products/", {"title": "Test"})
        self.assertEqual(response.status_code, status.HTTP_401_UNAUTHORIZED)

    def test_update_own_product(self):
        # Create first
        create_resp = self.client.post("/api/products/", {
            "title": "Old Title", "price": "50.00",
            "category": "electronics"
        })
        uuid = create_resp.data["uuid"]

        # Update
        response = self.client.patch(
            f"/api/products/{uuid}/",
            {"title": "New Title"}
        )
        self.assertEqual(response.status_code, status.HTTP_200_OK)
        self.assertEqual(response.data["title"], "New Title")

    def tearDown(self):
        # Runs after every test method (cleanup)
        pass
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Testing in JavaScript (Jest)}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Java, caption={Jest testing for Node.js}]
// calculator.test.js
const { add, divide, fetchUser } = require("./calculator");

// ---- BASIC TESTS ----
describe("Calculator", () => {
    test("adds two numbers", () => {
        expect(add(2, 3)).toBe(5);
    });

    test("handles negative numbers", () => {
        expect(add(-1, -2)).toBe(-3);
    });

    test("throws on division by zero", () => {
        expect(() => divide(10, 0)).toThrow("Cannot divide by zero");
    });
});

// ---- ASYNC TESTS ----
describe("User API", () => {
    test("fetches user by ID", async () => {
        const user = await fetchUser(1);
        expect(user).toHaveProperty("name");
        expect(user.name).toBeDefined();
    });
});

// ---- MOCKING ----
const axios = require("axios");
jest.mock("axios");  // mock the entire module

test("fetches users from API", async () => {
    // Setup: define what the mock should return
    axios.get.mockResolvedValue({
        data: [{ id: 1, name: "Amzat" }]
    });

    const users = await getUsers();
    expect(users).toHaveLength(1);
    expect(users[0].name).toBe("Amzat");
    expect(axios.get).toHaveBeenCalledWith("/api/users");
});
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Mocking}

\textbf{Mocking} replaces real dependencies (databases, APIs, external services) with controlled substitutes during testing. This makes tests fast, deterministic, and independent of external systems.

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Mocking in Python}]
from unittest.mock import patch, MagicMock

# Mock an external API call
@patch("myapp.services.requests.get")
def test_fetch_weather(mock_get):
    # Setup mock response
    mock_get.return_value.status_code = 200
    mock_get.return_value.json.return_value = {
        "temperature": 20, "condition": "sunny"
    }

    # Call the function that uses requests.get
    result = get_weather("London")

    assert result["temperature"] == 20
    mock_get.assert_called_once_with(
        "https://api.weather.com/London"
    )
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Test-Driven Development (TDD)}

\textbf{TDD} is a development approach where you write tests \emph{before} writing the code:

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Red:} Write a failing test that defines the desired behaviour.
    \item \textbf{Green:} Write the minimum code to make the test pass.
    \item \textbf{Refactor:} Clean up the code while keeping tests green.
    \item Repeat.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{tipbox}
TDD forces you to think about requirements and edge cases \emph{before} coding. It produces better-designed, more testable code. Not every scenario needs TDD, but it is excellent for complex logic, algorithms, and critical business rules.
\end{tipbox}

\section{What a Failed Test Is Trying to Tell You}

A failed test is not only a red mark. It is feedback about one of a few things:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item the code is wrong
    \item the test expectation is wrong
    \item the setup or fixture is wrong
    \item the interface changed and the test was not updated intentionally
    \item the test is flaky and depends on time, randomness, ordering, or external systems
\end{itemize}

\begin{warningbox}
