Do not train yourself to fear red tests. Train yourself to investigate them systematically. Teams become dangerous when they start treating failing tests as noise.
\end{warningbox}

\section{Debugging Strategy}

When something breaks, follow this systematic approach:

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Reproduce} the bug consistently. If you cannot reproduce it, you cannot fix it.
    \item \textbf{Read the error message} and stack trace carefully. It usually tells you exactly what went wrong and where.
    \item \textbf{Isolate} the problem. Which file? Which function? Which line?
    \item \textbf{Check assumptions.} Is the data what you think it is? Print/log variables.
    \item \textbf{Use the debugger.} Set breakpoints, step through code line by line.
    \begin{itemize}
        \item Python: \texttt{import pdb; pdb.set\_trace()} or VS Code breakpoints.
        \item JavaScript: \texttt{debugger;} statement or Chrome DevTools.
    \end{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Binary search.} If the bug is in a large block of code, comment out half and see if the bug persists. Repeat.
    \item \textbf{Check recent changes.} Use \texttt{git diff} or \texttt{git log} to see what changed since it last worked.
    \item \textbf{Fix the root cause}, not the symptom. A quick hack will come back to haunt you.
    \item \textbf{Write a regression test} to prevent the bug from recurring.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{examtipbox}
In technical interviews, strong debugging answers sound structured. Saying ``I would reproduce the issue, narrow the scope, inspect assumptions, and then add a regression test'' sounds far stronger than ``I would try a few things.''
\end{examtipbox}

\section{Documentation Best Practices}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{README:} Every project must have one. Include: what the project does, how to set it up, how to run it, architecture overview, tech stack, screenshots.
    \item \textbf{API documentation:} Document every endpoint with method, URL, request/response format, auth requirements, error codes. Use Swagger/OpenAPI or DRF's built-in auto-docs.
    \item \textbf{Code comments:} Explain \emph{why}, not \emph{what}. The code itself shows what it does---comments should explain non-obvious reasoning.
    \item \textbf{Docstrings:} Every public function and class should have a docstring explaining purpose, parameters, return values, and exceptions.
    \item \textbf{Architecture Decision Records (ADRs):} Document important technical decisions and their rationale.
\end{itemize}

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python, caption={Good vs.\,bad comments}]
# BAD: states the obvious
x = x + 1  # increment x by 1

# GOOD: explains WHY
x = x + 1  # compensate for 0-based indexing in the API response

# BAD: no docstring
def process(data):
    ...

# GOOD: clear docstring
def process_tune_file(file_path: str) -> dict:
    """Parse and validate an ECU tune file.

    Args:
        file_path: Absolute path to the .bin tune file.

    Returns:
        Dictionary with keys: checksum, maps, metadata.

    Raises:
        ValueError: If the file format is invalid.
        FileNotFoundError: If the file does not exist.
    """
    ...
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Testing gives you confidence to change code safely.
    \item Debugging is a structured investigation, not random trial and error.
    \item Documentation reduces repeated confusion and makes teams faster.
    \item Strong engineers do not just write features; they make those features observable, testable, and understandable.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Why does the testing pyramid place unit tests at the base?
    \item What makes a test deterministic?
    \item Why is reproducing a bug the first step in debugging?
    \item What belongs in a README, and what belongs in API documentation?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand testing deeply: the testing pyramid, pytest (fixtures, parametrize, exceptions), Django API testing, Jest for JavaScript, mocking, TDD workflow, systematic debugging strategy, and documentation best practices. Apply testing to every project you build.}


\section{Chapter Review: Testing}

\subsection{Key Takeaways}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item The \textbf{test pyramid}: many unit tests, some integration tests, few E2E tests.
    \item \textbf{pytest} is the standard Python test framework. Use fixtures for reusable setup.
    \item \textbf{Mocking} replaces real dependencies with controlled substitutes.
    \item \textbf{TDD} = write test first, then code, then refactor.
    \item \textbf{Debugging} is a systematic process, not random guessing.
    \item Comments should explain \emph{why}, not \emph{what}.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Practical Exercises}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Write unit tests for a calculator module with pytest, including edge cases.
    \item Write an integration test for a Django REST API endpoint.
    \item Practice mocking: test a function that calls an external weather API.
    \item Use TDD to build a password validator (min length, uppercase, number, special char).
\end{enumerate}




% CHAPTER 33 — SYSTEM DESIGN + ARCHITECTURE
% ============================================================
\chapter{System Design + Architecture}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{How to study this chapter:} read system design as a decision-making framework. Do not try to memorise diagrams. Instead, practice asking the right questions in the right order and explaining trade-offs calmly.
\end{studybox}

\section{What Is System Design?}

\textbf{System design} is the process of defining the architecture, components, data flow, and infrastructure of a software system to meet specific requirements. It is how you go from ``I need a motorcycle tuning marketplace'' to a detailed blueprint showing every server, database, cache, and queue.

\textbf{Why it matters:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item A poorly designed system collapses under load, costs too much, or becomes unmaintainable.
    \item System design is tested in senior engineering interviews at every major tech company.
    \item Understanding architecture helps you make better decisions even in day-to-day coding.
\end{itemize}

\begin{intuitionbox}
System design is the art of choosing what to optimize first. Every useful architecture is a set of trade-offs between simplicity, latency, consistency, throughput, cost, and operational complexity.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{System Design Process}

Follow this structured approach for any system design problem:

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Clarify requirements:}
    \begin{itemize}
        \item \textbf{Functional:} What does it do? (list products, process payments, flash ECU tunes)
        \item \textbf{Non-functional:} How well? (latency $< 200$ms, 99.9\% uptime, handle 10K users)
    \end{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Estimate scale:} How many users? Requests/second? Data volume? Read-heavy or write-heavy?
    \item \textbf{Define API:} What endpoints exist? What data flows between components?
    \item \textbf{Design data model:} Database schema, relationships, indexing strategy.
    \item \textbf{High-level architecture:} Draw boxes and arrows showing all major components.
    \item \textbf{Deep dive:} Zoom into critical components (how does the payment system work? how does search scale?).
    \item \textbf{Identify bottlenecks:} Where will the system break under 10x load?
    \item \textbf{Discuss trade-offs:} Every decision has pros and cons. Articulate them.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Requirements Checklist Before You Design}

Many weak system design answers jump into tools too early. Before drawing architecture, force yourself to answer a short checklist.

\subsection{Functional Requirements}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What exact user actions must the system support?
    \item Which actions are core and which are optional?
    \item Which workflows are synchronous and which can be asynchronous?
    \item What data must be stored permanently?
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Non-Functional Requirements}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Latency:} how fast must responses be?
    \item \textbf{Throughput:} how many requests or jobs per second must the system handle?
    \item \textbf{Availability:} what downtime is acceptable?
    \item \textbf{Consistency:} must reads always reflect the latest write?
    \item \textbf{Durability:} what data loss is unacceptable?
    \item \textbf{Security and compliance:} which data needs stronger protection or auditing?
    \item \textbf{Cost:} is this a startup-budget system or a large-scale enterprise system?
\end{itemize}

\begin{examtipbox}
In interviews, explicitly saying ``let me separate functional and non-functional requirements first'' instantly improves the perceived structure of your answer.
\end{examtipbox}

\section{Key Architectural Concepts}

\subsection{Scaling Strategies}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Vertical scaling (scale up):} Add more power to one machine---more CPU, RAM, faster SSD. Simple but has physical limits. Expensive at the top end.
    \item \textbf{Horizontal scaling (scale out):} Add more machines behind a load balancer. Virtually unlimited scaling. Requires stateless application design.
\end{itemize}

\begin{notebox}
Most modern systems use horizontal scaling. This means your application must be \textbf{stateless}---do not store session data in memory on one server. Use Redis, a database, or JWTs instead.
\end{notebox}

\subsection{Load Balancing}

A \textbf{load balancer} distributes incoming requests across multiple servers:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Round-robin:} Distributes requests evenly in order (Server 1, 2, 3, 1, 2, 3...).
    \item \textbf{Least connections:} Sends to the server with fewest active connections.
    \item \textbf{IP hash:} Routes based on client IP (session affinity).
    \item \textbf{Tools:} Nginx, HAProxy, AWS ALB (Application Load Balancer), AWS NLB.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Caching}

\textbf{Caching} stores frequently accessed data in fast storage (memory) to avoid expensive recomputation or database queries:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Client-side cache:} Browser cache, CDN cache. Reduces latency by serving from nearby edge servers.
    \item \textbf{Application cache:} Redis, Memcached. Store query results, session data, computed values.
    \item \textbf{Database cache:} Query cache, materialised views.
    \item \textbf{CDN (Content Delivery Network):} Caches static assets (images, CSS, JS) on servers worldwide. CloudFront, Cloudflare. Users get served from the nearest edge location.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Cache invalidation strategies:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{TTL (Time To Live):} Cache expires after a set time (e.g., 5 minutes).
    \item \textbf{Write-through:} Update cache when you update the database.
    \item \textbf{Cache-aside (lazy loading):} Check cache first; on miss, read from DB and populate cache.
\end{itemize}

\begin{warningbox}
``There are only two hard things in Computer Science: cache invalidation and naming things.'' --- Phil Karlton. Cache bugs are notoriously difficult to debug because the system works \emph{most of the time}.
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Database Design at Scale}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Replication:} Master database handles writes; read replicas handle reads. Improves read throughput.
    \item \textbf{Sharding (partitioning):} Split data across multiple databases by a shard key (e.g., user ID). Each shard holds a subset of data. Enables near-unlimited storage and write throughput.
    \item \textbf{SQL vs.\,NoSQL:}
    \begin{itemize}
        \item \textbf{SQL (PostgreSQL, MySQL):} Structured data, strong consistency, complex queries, transactions.
        \item \textbf{NoSQL (MongoDB, DynamoDB, Cassandra):} Flexible schema, horizontal scaling, eventual consistency. Good for: logs, analytics, real-time data, document storage.
    \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Message Queues}

\textbf{Message queues} enable asynchronous processing---the producer sends a message and continues immediately; the consumer processes it later:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Use cases:} Background jobs (sending emails, processing uploads, generating reports), decoupling services, handling traffic spikes.
    \item \textbf{Tools:} RabbitMQ, Apache Kafka, AWS SQS, Redis (as a simple queue), Celery (Python task queue).
    \item \textbf{Pattern:} Producer $\to$ Queue $\to$ Consumer. Multiple consumers can process in parallel.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{CAP Theorem}

In a \textbf{distributed system}, you can only guarantee two of three properties:

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Consistency (C):} Every read receives the most recent write. All nodes see the same data.
    \item \textbf{Availability (A):} Every request receives a response (success or failure), even if some nodes are down.
    \item \textbf{Partition tolerance (P):} The system continues operating even if network communication between nodes is lost.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{In practice:} Network partitions \emph{will} happen (P is non-negotiable), so you must choose between C and A:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{CP systems} (Consistency + Partition tolerance): Return errors rather than stale data. Example: banking systems, PostgreSQL.
    \item \textbf{AP systems} (Availability + Partition tolerance): Return potentially stale data rather than errors. Example: DNS, Cassandra, DynamoDB (eventual consistency).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Microservices vs.\,Monolith}

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Monolith:} Single codebase, single deployment. Simple, fast to start. Your RevSync backend is a monolith (Django).
    \item \textbf{Microservices:} Many small, independent services communicating via APIs. Each can be deployed, scaled, and updated independently.
    \item \textbf{When to use microservices:} When you have separate teams, different scaling needs per component, or need independent deployment. Do not start with microservices---extract them from a monolith when needed.
\end{itemize}

\section{Trade-Off Checklist}

System design is rarely about finding the one perfect answer. It is about choosing a direction that fits the constraints best.

\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Speed vs.\ complexity:} a monolith is faster to build; microservices may scale organisationally but add operational cost.
    \item \textbf{Consistency vs.\ availability:} strict consistency protects correctness but may increase failure rates during partitions.
    \item \textbf{Read performance vs.\ write complexity:} replication helps reads; cross-region writes can complicate consistency.
    \item \textbf{Low latency vs.\ fresh data:} aggressive caching improves speed but risks stale responses.
    \item \textbf{Operational simplicity vs.\ future scale:} simple systems are easier to maintain; over-engineering early can slow teams down.
    \item \textbf{Cost vs.\ resilience:} redundancy improves reliability but increases spend.
\end{itemize}

\begin{intuitionbox}
Strong candidates do not just name technologies. They explain \emph{what pain} each technology solves and \emph{what new pain} it introduces.
\end{intuitionbox}

\section{System Design Walkthrough: URL Shortener}

Let us walk through a complete system design example.

\textbf{Problem:} Design a URL shortener (like bit.ly). Users submit a long URL and get a short URL. Visitors to the short URL are redirected to the original.

\textbf{Step 1 --- Requirements:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Functional: Create short URLs, redirect to original, track click count.
    \item Non-functional: Low latency ($< 50$ms redirect), high availability, handle 1000 reads/second.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Step 2 --- API Design:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \texttt{POST /api/shorten} --- Body: \texttt{\{url: "https://long-url.com"\}} $\to$ Response: \texttt{\{short\_url: "abc123"\}}
    \item \texttt{GET /abc123} --- Redirects to original URL (HTTP 301/302).
    \item \texttt{GET /api/stats/abc123} --- Returns click count, referrers.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Step 3 --- Data Model:}
\begin{lstlisting}[language=SQL, caption={URL shortener schema}]
CREATE TABLE urls (
    id BIGSERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    short_code VARCHAR(10) UNIQUE NOT NULL,
    original_url TEXT NOT NULL,
    created_at TIMESTAMPTZ DEFAULT NOW(),
    click_count BIGINT DEFAULT 0,
    expires_at TIMESTAMPTZ
);
CREATE INDEX idx_short_code ON urls(short_code);
\end{lstlisting}

\textbf{Step 4 --- Architecture:}
\begin{verbatim}
User --> [Load Balancer] --> [App Servers (stateless)]
              |                     |
              v                     v
         [Redis Cache]      [PostgreSQL DB]
         (short_code         (persistent
          -> url mapping)     storage)
\end{verbatim}

\textbf{Step 5 --- Key Decisions:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Short code generation:} Base62 encoding of an auto-incrementing ID. 6 characters = $62^6 = 56$ billion unique URLs.
    \item \textbf{Caching:} Cache popular URLs in Redis. 80\% of traffic goes to 20\% of URLs (Pareto principle).
    \item \textbf{Redirect type:} 301 (permanent) for SEO, 302 (temporary) for tracking clicks.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Step 6 --- Scaling for 1M users:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Horizontal scaling: multiple app servers behind load balancer.
    \item Database read replicas for stats queries.
    \item Redis cluster for caching.
    \item CDN for the redirect (if possible).
\end{itemize}

\section{System Design: RevSync Architecture}

\begin{verbatim}
[React Native App]
     |
     | HTTPS (JWT Auth)
     v
[Nginx (Load Balancer + SSL)]
     |
     v
[Django + DRF (API Server)]
     |
     +--> [PostgreSQL (Users, Tunes, Orders)]
     |
     +--> [Redis (Caching, Session, Rate Limiting)]
     |
     +--> [S3 (Tune file storage)]
     |
     +--> [Celery Workers (Background jobs:
     |     tune validation, email, notifications)]
     |
     +--> [RabbitMQ/Redis (Message broker)]
     |
     v
[Monitoring: Sentry + Prometheus + Grafana]
\end{verbatim}

\section{How to Answer a System Design Interview}

\subsection{A Practical Speaking Template}

If you freeze in interviews, use this pattern:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item ``Let me clarify the scope and requirements first.''
    \item ``I will make a few scale assumptions so we can size the design.''
    \item ``I will sketch the high-level architecture before diving into one critical component.''
    \item ``Then I will discuss bottlenecks, trade-offs, and how the system evolves.''
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{What to Cover in a 10--15 Minute Answer}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item requirements and scope
    \item rough scale estimate
    \item high-level diagram
    \item data model
    \item one deep dive
    \item one or two bottlenecks and fixes
\end{itemize}

\subsection{What to Cover in a 30--45 Minute Answer}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item everything above
    \item API design details
    \item failure handling
    \item consistency choices
    \item observability and operations
    \item security considerations
    \item future scaling path
\end{itemize}

\section{Common System Design Mistakes}

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Starting with tools instead of requirements.
    \item Using microservices because they sound advanced.
    \item Ignoring write paths and only optimising reads.
    \item Forgetting background jobs, retries, and failure handling.
    \item Naming Redis, Kafka, and Kubernetes without explaining why they are needed.
    \item Never stating trade-offs, risks, or assumptions.
    \item Designing for internet-scale numbers when the business problem does not require it.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{interviewbox}
System design interviews test your ability to: (1) clarify requirements, (2) estimate scale, (3) design APIs, (4) choose the right database, (5) identify bottlenecks, (6) discuss trade-offs. Practice with: design Twitter, design Uber, design a file upload service, design a notification system. Always draw diagrams.
\end{interviewbox}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{System design drill:} take one product you know well and answer these in writing:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What are the top three functional requirements?
    \item What are the top three non-functional requirements?
    \item What is the simplest architecture that could work?
    \item What will break first at 10x growth?
    \item Which component would you cache, queue, or shard first, and why?
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercisebox}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Good system design starts with requirements and scale, not with tools.
    \item Caching, queues, load balancing, replication, and sharding solve different classes of problems.
    \item Strong answers explain why a choice was made and what trade-off it introduces.
    \item Interview-quality design is as much about communication and structure as it is about components.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Why should you separate functional and non-functional requirements?
    \item What does a load balancer actually solve?
    \item When is replication useful, and when is sharding necessary?
    \item Why is cache invalidation difficult?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\checkpoint{You now understand system design deeply: the design process (requirements $\to$ scale $\to$ API $\to$ data model $\to$ architecture $\to$ bottlenecks $\to$ trade-offs), scaling strategies, load balancing, caching (Redis, CDN), database replication and sharding, message queues, CAP theorem, microservices vs.\,monolith, and complete design walkthroughs. You can design systems like URL shorteners, marketplaces, and real-time platforms.}


\section{Chapter Review: System Design}

\subsection{Key Takeaways}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item System design = requirements $\to$ scale estimation $\to$ API $\to$ data model $\to$ architecture $\to$ bottlenecks $\to$ trade-offs.
    \item \textbf{Horizontal scaling} + \textbf{stateless servers} is the standard approach.
    \item \textbf{Caching} (Redis) and \textbf{CDNs} reduce latency dramatically.
    \item \textbf{CAP theorem}: you must choose between consistency and availability when networks partition.
    \item \textbf{Message queues} enable asynchronous processing and service decoupling.
    \item Start with a \textbf{monolith}; extract microservices only when needed.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Practice Problems}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item Design a file upload service (like Dropbox). Consider: storage, CDN, chunked uploads, metadata.
    \item Design a notification system (email, push, SMS). Consider: queues, templates, delivery tracking.
    \item Design RevSync's architecture for 100K users. Where are the bottlenecks?
    \item Explain the CAP theorem with a real-world example.
\end{enumerate}

\chapter{Project Case Studies from My CV}

\begin{studybox}
\textbf{Study goal:} learn how to turn projects into engineering stories. A strong case study explains the problem, the architecture, the trade-offs, the mistakes, and the next improvements. This is how projects become interview assets instead of just GitHub links.
\end{studybox}

\section{RevSync --- Motorcycle ECU Tuning Ecosystem}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Tech:} Django + DRF (backend), React Native + Expo (mobile), REST APIs, JWT auth, Docker, PostgreSQL.
    \item \textbf{Architecture:} Monolithic backend, mobile client, BLE firmware flashing.
    \item \textbf{Skills demonstrated:} Full-stack development, API design, database modelling, mobile engineering, security (firmware validation), system design.
    \item \textbf{Production improvements:} Add rate limiting, implement WebSocket for real-time flash progress, add comprehensive test suite, set up CI/CD pipeline, implement proper error monitoring (Sentry).
\end{itemize}

\textbf{How to talk about RevSync in interviews:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item explain why safety-critical workflows change product design
    \item discuss how backend truth and mobile truth must stay aligned
    \item highlight trade-offs between speed of building and reliability hardening
    \item mention where you would add stronger observability, audit trails, and device-state validation
\end{itemize}

\textbf{What this project teaches technically:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item full-stack architecture under real product constraints
    \item mobile workflow orchestration and state persistence
    \item backend permission design and API contract discipline
    \item thinking about trust, verification, and operational support
\end{itemize}

\section{VeriRAG --- Verifiable Retrieval-Augmented Generation}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{Tech:} Python, LLM integration (GPT), spaCy, vector search, evaluation metrics.
    \item \textbf{Architecture:} RAG pipeline with claim-level verification, verify-repair loops.
    \item \textbf{Skills demonstrated:} AI/ML engineering, NLP, evaluation methodology, safety-first design.
    \item \textbf{Research-grade improvements:} Formal evaluation benchmarks, comparison with baseline RAG, ablation studies, academic paper potential.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Interview angle:} this project demonstrates that you understand not just generation, but \emph{verification}, evaluation, and the engineering of trust in LLM systems.

\textbf{What this project teaches technically:}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item retrieval design and evaluation
    \item claim-level verification rather than naive generation
    \item why ``works in a demo'' is not enough for high-trust AI products
    \item how research-style thinking can improve practical product systems
\end{itemize}

\section{Other Projects}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item \textbf{AgriXnest:} Image classification (CNN/TensorFlow), plant identification, crop guidance API.
    \item \textbf{CareerForge AI:} LLM-powered career planning, CV parsing with spaCy, structured output.
    \item \textbf{Breast Cancer Detection:} ResNet-18, transfer learning, medical imaging, evaluation metrics.
    \item \textbf{Live Chat Bot:} Flask + Socket.IO, real-time WebSocket communication.
    \item \textbf{MyMusic Maestro:} Django, CRUD operations, template-based frontend.
    \item \textbf{Giving GRACE:} Full-stack event matching platform.
\end{itemize}

\begin{summarybox}
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item A project case study should explain problem, architecture, decisions, trade-offs, and next improvements.
    \item RevSync is a strong full-stack systems case study.
    \item VeriRAG is a strong AI-systems and evaluation case study.
    \item Smaller projects still matter when they clearly demonstrate one or two engineering strengths.
\end{itemize}
\end{summarybox}

\begin{recallbox}
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item What makes a project description credible to an interviewer?
    \item Why is architecture discussion more persuasive than a feature list?
    \item Which of your projects best demonstrates system design? Which best demonstrates AI engineering?
\end{enumerate}
\end{recallbox}

\begin{exercisebox}
\textbf{Practice task:} pick one of your projects and write a one-page case study using this structure:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=2em]
    \item problem and users
    \item architecture and stack
    \item one difficult technical challenge
    \item one trade-off you made
    \item what you would improve if you had four more weeks
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercisebox}
